Health outcomes will worsen, including more pregnancy-related complications or deaths (which disproportionately affect women of color and poor women).
  - **Strain on Healthcare Providers:** Clinics that rely on federal funds (Title X clinics, etc.) will either have to stop offering comprehensive counseling or lose funding, as happened under Trump’s first term gag rule ([Executive Order 14182—Enforcing the Hyde Amendment | The American Presidency Project](https://www.presidency.ucsb.edu/documents/executive-order-14182-enforcing-the-hyde-amendment#:~:text=taxpayers%20should%20not%20be%20forced,wide%20variety%20of%20Federal%20programs)). Many clinics closed during that time. Losing those clinics reduces access to not just abortion but also contraception, cancer screenings, and STI testing – essential services for low-income populations. That ironically can *increase* unplanned pregnancies and abortions in the long run. Also, doctors and providers within federal systems (like VA hospitals or military doctors) will face tight restrictions and possibly punishment if they even discuss abortion options beyond what’s allowed. This **creates ethical dilemmas and demoralization** among medical staff, and patients may not get full info about their healthcare options.
  - **Chilling Effects and Confusion:** Agencies being hyper-vigilant means even permissible actions might be avoided. E.g., doctors might hesitate to perform an abortion even for a life-threatening situation if they fear it’ll be scrutinized as “elective” under federal rules. This could put women’s lives at risk in emergencies. Similarly, some might misconstrue the EO to mean things like Plan B (emergency contraception) or IUDs (which some anti-abortion activists falsely label abortifacients) should not be funded. That could restrict birth control access inadvertently, as providers play it safe to avoid funding cuts, ironically leading to more unintended pregnancies.
  - **Privacy and Legal Risks:** Biden’s EOs had provisions to strengthen privacy (to protect women’s data from being used to prosecute them for seeking abortion). With those revoked, the federal government might cooperate with law enforcement requests for data or not update HIPAA rules to guard abortion-related information. This means women (and those assisting them) in restrictive states are at higher risk of surveillance and legal action. That’s a harm to civil liberties and causes fear even in seeking information or help regarding abortion.
  - **Global Health Impact:** If the global gag rule is back (likely as part of this ethos), international NGOs might lose U.S. funding for offering family planning that includes abortion info. Historically, this leads to **more unintended pregnancies and unsafe abortions in poor countries**, as clinics close or cut services, ironically increasing what the policy purported to prevent. Women globally suffer as a result.
  - **Psychological and Societal Harm:** Women who find themselves with unwanted pregnancies and no support suffer stress, and being forced to give birth can trap them in poverty (which is well documented by research from states that cut abortion access). Societally, this widens inequalities – those with means will still get abortions (flying to a state or paying privately), those without means will not. So essentially, **the EO’s fallout entrenches a two-tier system of reproductive rights: one for the rich, one for the poor**. This harm to equality and autonomy is profound and lasting.
  
- **How a Future Administration Could Respond:** A future (presumably Democratic) administration would aim to undo these restrictions and restore support for reproductive health services. Key steps:
  - **Revoke EO 14182 immediately.** This would signal that the federal government no longer interprets policy as rigidly as Trump did. However, simply revoking it doesn’t automatically reinstate the Biden measures; the new President would likely also reissue something akin to Biden’s EOs (maybe updated for the context).
  - **Reinstate Biden’s EOs or craft new ones:** Issue a new EO that directs agencies to **find lawful ways to support reproductive healthcare access**. This could mean instructing HHS to again allow Title X funds to go to providers that offer abortion counseling (basically reversing the gag rule). Also instruct HHS and DOJ to use all tools to ensure EMTALA protections for emergency abortion care are enforced (reissuing the Biden guidance that Trump likely dropped). Direct DoD to reinstate coverage of travel expenses for service members needing abortions (and the same for the VA, if VA’s rule was rolled back).
  - **Update OMB guidance reversing Trump’s memos:** OMB should tell agencies that normal pre-Trump rules apply. That means, for example, in grant applications, providers will not be disqualified for mentioning abortion. It also means federal employee insurance can cover abortion where allowed, and agencies like NIH can fund research involving abortion or fetal tissue (Trump had restricted fetal tissue research too; new guidance should restore scientific freedom).
  - **Global Gag Rule and Mexico City Policy:** Typically, Democratic presidents revoke the gag rule on day one. So the new admin would allow international family planning funds to once again go to organizations that provide or counsel on abortions (with non-US funds). They might also restore funding to UNFPA (United Nations Population Fund), which Trump cut due to alleged ties to abortion or Chinese family planning.
  - **Ensure any new supportive measures are legally solid:** Some of Biden’s attempts (like using Medicaid waivers for travel costs) were untested legally; a new admin could attempt them again but should be prepared to defend them in court or adjust strategy. Perhaps they could push Congress (if at all possible) to amend Medicaid statute to allow such coverage explicitly, though that’s unlikely with a divided Congress. But administratively, they can try to be creative in helping women in restrictive states—e.g., use federal property for clinics, mail-order pills through federal channels (ideas floated under Biden but not executed).
  - **Push for Legislative Reforms:** Ultimately, Hyde is a law (rider) that only Congress can repeal. A new admin should work with Congress to try to remove Hyde from appropriations (a heavy lift unless political landscape shifts). They could also aim to codify some protections: for instance, a federal law protecting the right to travel for abortion, or protecting contraception access, to guard against future EOs or state actions. Even if those don’t pass, advocating them sets a marker.
  - **Repair Harm to Affected Populations:** For individuals harmed under Trump’s policy, not all can be undone (you can’t undo a forced birth). But the new administration can mitigate: increase funding to Title X to reopen clinics in underserved areas; support programs for mothers who were forced to carry to term (though ironically that crosses into anti-abortion arguments of helping mothers, it’s still important to support them); ensure that veterans or service members who had to pay out-of-pocket under Trump get reimbursed if possible (maybe retroactively cover some costs if legally feasible). Also, resume privacy protections efforts: for example, finalize stronger HIPAA rules preventing sharing of reproductive health data, so women feel safer seeking care.
  - **Public Communication:** Make it clear that the federal government stands with women’s right to make their own healthcare decisions, within the constraints left by Dobbs. Encourage federal agencies to be voices for accurate information – e.g., CDC or HHS can disseminate info on where and how women can legally get care, correcting the chilling silence that might’ve prevailed. Also, remove any Trump-imposed censorship on websites (in his first term, some HHS websites removed info on abortion; Biden restored some. Ensure all info is back).
  
  By taking these steps, a future administration would undo the administrative barriers erected by EO 14182 and work to **proactively support reproductive health**. It’s a challenging task, because even after undoing this EO, the patchwork post-Dobbs remains, but at least the federal stance would shift back to **protecting and expanding access where possible, rather than restricting it** ([Biden revokes terrorist designation for Yemen's Houthis | AP News](https://apnews.com/article/joe-biden-donald-trump-civil-wars-yemen-d17b50e3995827838a19fb8bd09e9f64#:~:text=%E2%80%9COur%20action%20is%20due%20entirely,humanitarian%20crisis%2C%E2%80%9D%20the%20official%20said)). Over time, if political conditions allow, the ultimate fix would be codifying Roe or a similar protection into federal law; short of that, repealing Hyde via Congress would vastly reduce the need for such EOs ping-ponging with each change of administration.

## EO 14183 — **Prioritizing Military Excellence and Readiness**  
*Alternative Title:* “Reinstating the Transgender Military Ban and Purging ‘Woke’ Policies from the Armed Forces”

- **What it does:** EO 14183 institutes policies in the U.S. military that effectively **ban service by transgender individuals and eliminate diversity/inclusion initiatives deemed ideologically driven**. Key directives:
  - Declares that high standards for readiness, cohesion, and uniformity are *“inconsistent with the medical, surgical, and mental health constraints on individuals with gender dysphoria”* ([Executive Order 14183—Prioritizing Military Excellence and Readiness | The American Presidency Project](https://www.presidency.ucsb.edu/documents/executive-order-14183-prioritizing-military-excellence-and-readiness#:~:text=Sec,inaccurately%20reflect%20an%20individual%27s%20sex)). It states that allowing such individuals, or the use of pronouns that don’t match one’s biological sex, is harmful to military focus ([Executive Order 14183—Prioritizing Military Excellence and Readiness | The American Presidency Project](https://www.presidency.ucsb.edu/documents/executive-order-14183-prioritizing-military-excellence-and-readiness#:~:text=Sec,inaccurately%20reflect%20an%20individual%27s%20sex)). This is a clear signal that **persons with gender dysphoria (i.e., transgender individuals who have transitioned or seek to) are disqualified from military service**. It mirrors the rationale of Trump’s 2017 transgender ban (which was reversed by Biden via EO 14004).
  - References definitions from a Jan 20, 2025 EO “Defending Women from Gender Ideology Extremism and Restoring Biological Truth” (which likely defined sex as binary, etc.) ([Executive Order 14183—Prioritizing Military Excellence and Readiness | The American Presidency Project](https://www.presidency.ucsb.edu/documents/executive-order-14183-prioritizing-military-excellence-and-readiness#:~:text=Sec,shall%20apply%20to%20this%20order)), indicating a government-wide policy on gender (EO 14168 as seen in the list).
  - Orders the Secretary of Defense within 60 days to **update military accession and retention medical standards (DoDI 6130# Executive Orders 14173–14183 (Trump Second Term, 2025): Critical Analysis and Reversal Proposals

*This report provides a comprehensive analysis of Executive Orders (EOs) **14173 through 14183**, signed by President Donald Trump in January 2025 during his second term. Each section below addresses one EO in numerical order, providing: (1) the official title, (2) a derisive alternative title reflecting a left-leaning critique, (3) a bullet-point summary of what the EO does, (4) its claimed purpose and justification, (5) the likely real intent/effect with critical perspective, (6) how it fits Trump’s broader agenda, (7) the harms it causes, and (8) how a future administration could undo or repair the damage.* 

Official sources and the text of the EOs are cited to substantiate each analysis. The language throughout reflects a critical, left-leaning stance on Trump’s actions and policies.

## EO 14173 — **Ending Illegal Discrimination and Restoring Merit-Based Opportunity**  
**Alternative Title:** *“Eliminating Equity and Diversity Initiatives Under the Guise of ‘Merit’”*

**What the EO Does:**  
- **Rescinds Diversity & Equity Orders:** Revokes multiple prior executive actions that promoted diversity, equity, and inclusion (DEI) in federal operations. This includes **EO 12898 (1994) on environmental justice, EO 13583 (2011) on workforce diversity, and EO 13672 (2014) expanding LGBTQ protections in federal contracting**. It even **revokes EO 11246 (1965)** – a foundational order prohibiting federal contractors from discrimination and requiring affirmative action. Essentially, it wipes out decades of civil-rights-oriented directives in hiring and contracting.  
- **Bans “DEI” in Government:** Orders all agencies to **terminate any “discriminatory and illegal preferences” or “equity” programs** and to strip concepts like “advancing equity” from regulations. Agencies must **purge guidance, programs, and trainings that reference DEI** and cannot consider factors like race or gender identity in hiring or grants.  
- **Targets Federal Contractors:** Instructs the Labor Department’s Office of Federal Contract Compliance Programs to **stop enforcing affirmative action or diversity requirements on government contractors**. Contractors no longer need to take proactive steps to ensure diverse hiring, and **cannot be required to include DEI criteria** in their workforce practices. New contract clauses must certify contractors comply with anti-discrimination laws but do not run “illegal DEI” programs.  
- **Expands “Merit”-Only Focus:** Emphasizes recruitment of “highly skilled Americans dedicated to American ideals” and suggests that any consideration beyond “individual merit” (implying racial or gender-based outreach) is an “impermissible factor”. It aligns all hiring and grant-making practices with a strict interpretation of meritocracy, rejecting affirmative action or diversity goals.  
- **Enforcement and Private Sector:** Directs agencies not only to change their own policies but also to **“combat illegal private-sector DEI”** if it’s deemed to violate civil-rights laws. This hints at the government potentially challenging corporate diversity programs as unlawful, extending the anti-DEI campaign beyond government to society at large.

**What it Claims to Do (Stated Justification):**  
Trump’s order presents itself as a defense of true equality and civil rights. It argues that *“longstanding civil-rights laws”* guarantee non-discrimination, and that contemporary DEI initiatives actually **violate those laws by giving “preferences” based on race or sex**. The EO’s text asserts that so-called “diversity, equity, and inclusion” policies are *“dangerous, demeaning, and immoral… under the guise of ‘equity’”* and amount to an *“unlawful, corrosive… identity-based spoils system.”* It claims these practices *“stigmatize, demean, or shut out”* people (presumably white or male candidates) and undermine *“traditional American values of hard work and excellence.”* The **stated goal is to restore a focus on individual merit** and ensure no one is disfavored or preferred due to race or sex. In Trump’s framing, this EO is enforcing equality by **banning “illegal discrimination” under the banner of DEI** and making the workforce purely merit-based for the “best-qualified”. He casts his actions as **protecting the civil rights of all and ending divisive practices**, implying that the previous administration’s equity policies were themselves illegal or unconstitutional.

**Real Purpose or Effect (Critical Perspective):**  
In effect, EO 14173 **dismantles affirmative action and diversity efforts** within the federal government and its contractors. The lofty “merit” language serves as a pretext to **roll back programs benefiting women, minorities, and LGBTQ people**. From a critical left viewpoint, the real intent is to **erase acknowledgment of systemic bias**: by forcing a “colorblind” approach, it halts measures designed to level the playing field. The EO’s **revocation of EO 11246 and related orders is especially drastic** – that 1965 order by President Johnson was a cornerstone of equal employment opportunity in federal contracting. Its removal potentially allows contractors to no longer be overseen for diversity compliance, a huge retreat in enforcement. Also, eliminating EO 12898 on environmental justice and other inclusion initiatives signals **abandoning federal attention to racial disparities in pollution and hiring**. 

This fits a pattern of Trump targeting what he labels “woke” policies. He is effectively **using the rhetoric of anti-discrimination to stop proactive anti-discrimination work**. For example, by calling equity programs “illegal preferences,” the EO paints efforts to uplift underrepresented groups as if they were prejudicial against others. The real effect is likely fewer opportunities and resources reaching minority communities. **Federal hiring could become less diverse** (since agencies can no longer even set diversity as a goal), and **minority-owned businesses may lose out on federal contracts** since affirmative action in contracting is curtailed. The EO chills any consideration of race or gender – even lawful ones like targeted outreach or mentorship – because agencies fear being accused of running afoul of this order. 

While Trump justifies this as protecting civil rights, it’s telling that **all the orders he revoked were about expanding rights and representation of historically marginalized groups**. The critical interpretation is that this EO aims to **freeze the status quo of who holds power** (which has traditionally been white, male) by removing tools that encouraged inclusion. It prioritizes a narrow view of “merit” that ignores the **structural barriers many Americans face**. In practice, **workers of color, women, and LGBTQ employees stand to lose supportive policies** (like diversity training or inclusive hiring programs) and could face more subtle discrimination that was previously being addressed through DEI initiatives.

**Fit into Trump’s Broader Agenda:**  
EO 14173 is a key part of Trump’s second-term agenda to **uproot what he calls “Deep State” progressive ideology from government**. On Day 1 (Jan 20, 2025), he had already issued EO 14151 “Ending Radical and Wasteful Government DEI Programs” (as noted in the Federal Register). EO 14173 on Jan 21 continues that crusade with more specific actions, showing a systematic purge of diversity policies. It aligns with his campaign rhetoric that **“woke politics” have infiltrated federal agencies** and need to be abolished. 

Strategically, this plays to Trump’s base by confronting buzzwords like “equity” and “inclusion,” which right-wing discourse often derides as leftist social engineering. It also fits into a broader administrative strategy to reshape the civil service: **by emphasizing “ideological loyalty” to nebulous American ideals over “diversity,”** Trump could more easily justify purging employees or contractors he deems politically opposed. For example, the EO’s language about hiring only those dedicated to “American ideals” hints at loyalty tests which critics fear could become litmus tests for conservative values rather than qualifications.

Additionally, this EO must be viewed alongside others in this sequence that target gender and race issues (e.g., EO 14168 on “Restoring Biological Truth” to government or EO 14183 on military policies). Together, these actions paint a picture of an administration doubling down on **culture war themes**: anti-trans, anti-affirmative action, anti-“critical race theory,” etc. Trump’s agenda is clearly to **erase Obama-era diversity gains** and even roll back some civil rights norms to pre-1960s conditions. 

By ending environmental justice efforts (EO 12898), it even dovetails with his deregulatory push favoring industry over communities. In summary, EO 14173 serves Trump’s agenda of **reasserting a traditional social order under the banner of “merit” and “unity,” while marginalizing discussions of race or gender inequality**.

**Harm Caused:**  
This order’s harms are wide-ranging and regressive: 

- **Loss of Equal Opportunity Programs:** Federal agencies stop or reverse programs that successfully recruited diverse talent or helped underrepresented employees advance. Mentorships for women in STEM, internships targeting minority college students, leadership training for people of color – all such efforts could be canceled as “illegal preferences.” **Agencies may revert to old networks and biases in hiring and promotions**, making it harder for qualified diverse candidates to succeed. Over time, the federal workforce could become less reflective of America’s demographic mix, undermining its ability to serve an increasingly diverse public. 

- **Impact on Federal Contractors:** By lifting affirmative action requirements, **federal contractors may no longer be held accountable for discrimination in their ranks**. Some companies might quietly scale back diversity recruiting or drop inclusive hiring goals since it’s no longer required – or even might fear being penalized for having such goals. This can lead to fewer job opportunities for women and minorities in the private sector as well. The EO even instructs cessation of “promoting ‘diversity’” among contractors, effectively telling companies they should avoid even appearing to have affirmative action. This could legitimize discriminatory practices under the guise of neutrality.

- **Legal Uncertainty and Reduced Civil-Rights Enforcement:** Agencies are told to enforce only “text of civil-rights laws” and remove “equitable decision-making” from their procedures. This strict reading may cause agencies to **shy away from robustly enforcing civil-rights protections** for fear it looks like favoritism. For instance, DOJ might scale back consent decrees on police bias, or Education might weaken Title IX enforcement on sex-based disparities – all in the name of avoiding “equity” initiatives. **Revoking EO 13672** (which had protected LGBTQ employees of federal contractors) specifically removes clear protections for transgender and queer workers, possibly exposing them again to discrimination. The harm is both immediate (loss of explicit protections) and chilling (agencies and employers might not step in to protect those workers, thinking the administration doesn’t value those protections).

- **Communities Losing Environmental Justice Focus:** EO 12898’s revocation means **less attention to pollution and climate impacts on minority and low-income neighborhoods**. Agencies like EPA or HUD may no longer prioritize grants or clean-ups in those areas. This could exacerbate environmental health disparities, as the government might ignore race/income when addressing issues like toxic waste sites or disaster recovery. Vulnerable communities that benefited from targeted federal help could be left to fend for themselves, widening health and quality-of-life gaps.

- **Workplace Culture and Morale:** For many federal employees, especially those from marginalized groups, this EO sends a demoralizing message that their employer will no longer celebrate or support diversity. It **invalidates employee resource groups, diversity councils, and training programs** that foster inclusive culture – possibly even banning implicit bias training or LGBTQ pride observances by agencies. The harm is a less welcoming workplace, which can drive talented staff away (brain drain of women, minority, and progressive employees) and reduce the quality of public service. Those who remain may feel forced to **closet aspects of their identity or experiences**, knowing the leadership sees those as irrelevant or taboo.

- **Public Trust and Service Efficacy:** A government that does not prioritize understanding diversity may be less effective in serving different populations. For example, **language access services might be cut as “equity” frills**, harming non-English speakers seeking federal aid. Or lack of diversity in staff could lead to cultural blind spots (e.g., FEMA without environmental justice lens might inadequately serve marginalized disaster survivors). In the long run, the public (especially minorities) may trust the government less, perceiving it as indifferent or hostile to their inclusion. This undermines the promise of equal opportunity that has been a federal goal for decades.

**How a Future Administration Could Undo or Repair Damage:**  

1. **Revoke EO 14173 Immediately:** The next president can issue an executive order nullifying EO 14173 and explicitly **reinstating the directives it canceled**. For instance, a new EO could **reinstate EO 13583, 13672, 12898, 11246, etc., or incorporate their key provisions** into a comprehensive diversity order. The American Presidency Project notes Trump’s revocations, so the new administration can use that as a checklist to restore each item. This sends a clear signal that diversity and inclusion are back as priorities.

2. **Restore Affirmative Action in Federal Operations:** Through executive action and policy memos, instruct the Office of Personnel Management (OPM) and agency heads to **resume diversity recruitment programs, training, and accountability measures**. Reinstate the requirement (from the old EO 11246 and Labor Department rules) that federal contractors **take affirmative action to ensure equal employment opportunity**. The Office of Federal Contract Compliance Programs should be directed to once again enforce diversity in contractor workforces, including goals and timetables where appropriate. Essentially, reverse the Labor Department instructions in Trump’s EO so that concepts of “diversity” and “affirmative action” are no longer dirty words but standard practice.

3. **Issue New Equity Guidance:** The OMB and Domestic Policy Council under the new admin should produce **government-wide equity guidance** (similar to what Biden did with EO 13985 in 2021) to re-embed equity considerations into federal policymaking. This would mean agencies *must* assess how policies affect disadvantaged groups and take action to remedy disparities. All the language Trump had struck (like “advancing equity”) should be woven back into federal grant criteria, strategic plans, and performance measures. This could be codified via a new EO or a reinstatement of Biden’s original equity EO, updated for current needs.

4. **Strengthen Civil Rights Enforcement Units:** To repair the neglect or chilling effect, the next administration should empower civil rights offices in each agency – giving them resources and support to actively investigate discrimination complaints and enforce laws. They should clarify that pursuing disparate impact cases or doing outreach to marginalized communities is not only allowed but encouraged (contrary to Trump’s implications). For example, the Justice Department could revive use of consent decrees to reform biased police departments, and Education’s Office for Civil Rights could reassert guidance on racial disparities in school discipline, etc.

5. **Outreach and Reassurance to Affected Communities and Employees:** A critical repair step is rebuilding trust. The President and cabinet officials should **meet with federal employee affinity groups, civil-rights organizations, and community leaders** to affirm the government’s renewed commitment to diversity and equal opportunity. Internally, agencies could re-launch diversity training and events, making clear those are supported from the top. Externally, communications should highlight that programs like environmental justice grants or minority business support are back online. Essentially, signal loudly that the federal government values and will serve **all** people, not pretend differences don’t exist.

6. **Embed Protections in Law Where Possible:** To guard against future flip-flops, the administration could work with Congress on legislation to enshrine certain diversity measures. For instance, writing elements of EO 11246 (nondiscrimination in federal contracting) into statute would make it harder for a future president to undo. Or passing an updated **Equal Employment Opportunity Act** that explicitly permits affirmative outreach in federal hiring. While challenging in a divided Congress, even incremental moves (like protecting EEOC’s ability to collect workforce diversity data) would help sustain progress. Additionally, supporting state and local governments that advance DEI (and not preempting them) creates a buffer of inclusive practices nationwide.

7. **Monitor and Course-Correct:** The new administration should set up a task force or commission to monitor the **reimplementation of DEI policies** and evaluate outcomes. This can identify any areas where damage from Trump’s EO persists (for example, if contractor diversity numbers fell sharply in 2025, extra effort might be needed to climb back). The task force can recommend additional actions, ensuring a continuous improvement loop. Over a term, this rebuilds the momentum that was lost.

8. **Publicly Celebrate Diversity:** Finally, an intangible but important repair is a change in tone from the top. Whereas Trump’s EO equated diversity efforts with divisiveness, the new administration should vocally celebrate America’s diversity as a strength. Presidential speeches, agency messages, and education campaigns can reinforce the positive value of inclusion and counter the narrative that “merit” and “diversity” are at odds. This helps heal the polarized perception and makes it easier to sustain these policies politically.

In short, a future administration must **explicitly undo the legal and policy changes of EO 14173** and actively **rebuild the machinery of equity and inclusion** within the federal government. By restoring the rescinded orders, re-empowering civil rights enforcement, and resetting the cultural tone, the government can again move toward its stated ideals of equal opportunity for all – not just in word, but in practice.

---

## EO 14174 — **Revocation of Certain Executive Orders**  
**Alternative Title:** *“Scrapping COVID Safety Rules for Federal Workers and Contractors”*

**What the EO Does:**  
- **Nullifies Biden’s COVID Safety Mandates:** EO 14174 straightforwardly **revokes two specific executive orders issued by President Biden in 2021**: **EO 14042 (Ensuring Adequate COVID Safety Protocols for Federal Contractors)** and **EO 14043 (Requiring Coronavirus Vaccination for Federal Employees)**. By doing so, it **ends the federal contractor COVID-safety requirements** (like masking, social distancing, and vaccination or testing regimes for contractor personnel) and **ends the vaccine mandate for federal employees**. These Biden orders had been the basis for requiring vaccinations or regular testing among millions of workers. Trump’s action means those rules are immediately off the books.
- **No Replacement Standards:** The order does not institute any new guidance or alternative COVID protocols – it’s a pure rollback. It does include a standard caveat that nothing in the order should impair other authorities or laws, but practically, it **leaves decisions about any remaining COVID precautions to agency discretion or other laws**, with no uniform federal approach.
- **Symbolic Clean Slate:** Coming on Jan 21, 2025, the day after Trump took office again, it serves as a symbolic **sweep-away of the prior administration’s pandemic response measures**. The brevity of the EO (just naming the orders to revoke) signals that the administration considered these mandates inherently illegitimate or unnecessary, not requiring detailed justification beyond Trump’s authority to rescind them.
- **Related Policies Affected:** By revoking EO 14042 and 14043, any policies created under them also fall. For instance:
  - **Federal Employee Rules:** Requirements that unvaccinated federal employees undergo regular testing or be subject to disciplinary action for refusing vaccination are void. Any employees fired or suspended solely due to the vaccine mandate might be eligible for reinstatement or back pay, depending on how agencies handle the change (some legal cases were ongoing; this EO likely moots them by conceding the mandates’ end).
  - **Contractor Clauses:** Federal contract clauses that implemented vaccination/testing for contractor staff become unenforceable. Agencies will likely issue modifications or guidance stating contractors no longer need to meet those protocols. This might affect ongoing contracts and solicitations that assumed those requirements.
  - **Federal Workplace Safety Plans:** Biden’s EO 14043 had led to federal workplace safety task forces and model safety principles (e.g., masking indoors when community transmission is high). With the vaccine requirement gone, agencies may also relax other measures like mask mandates or maximum telework posture, even if not explicitly required by 14043. Trump signaled an overall shift to *“normal”* operations, which many agencies followed.

**What it Claims to Do (Stated Justification):**  
EO 14174 itself is terse and provides no detailed justification beyond the statement of authority (“By the authority vested in me… it is hereby ordered”). However, contextually, the implied reasoning is to remove what Trump and many on the right viewed as **overreaching or unnecessary COVID-19 restrictions** on employment. Trump had long criticized vaccine mandates as infringements on personal freedom. He likely framed this revocation as **restoring individual rights and ending “forced” vaccination**. 

In public statements (outside the EO text), the justification would be that **COVID-19 is no longer the emergency it was, and these mandates hurt government efficiency and violate personal choice**. By Jan 2025, the acute phase of the pandemic had subsided. Trump’s team probably argued that vaccine mandates were causing worker shortages or morale issues and that high vaccination rates or natural immunity made them moot. Another key subtext: revoking these was fulfilling a campaign promise to undo Biden’s pandemic policies. Essentially, the EO claims to *“get the government out of the way”* and trust employees/contractors to make health decisions, reflecting a political stance that mandates did more harm than good. The brevity of the order and immediate effect underscore that Trump didn’t see a need for nuanced justification – it was enough that he campaigned on it.

**Real Purpose or Effect (Critical Perspective):**  
From a critical perspective, this EO’s real purpose is largely **political theater and ideological gratification**. It does very little substantively (aside from the mandates, most federal COVID rules were already easing by 2025), but it **sends a loud message repudiating the prior administration’s approach to the pandemic**. The effect is that **the federal government abandons any uniform stance on COVID safety**, even as the virus continues to circulate. This could make federal workplaces less safe and set an example for other employers to drop precautions. 

It also could be seen as part of Trump’s effort to rewrite the narrative of the pandemic: by swiftly canceling these EOs, he’s effectively declaring *“the pandemic is over, and we will not talk about it in terms of mandates anymore.”* The real intent is to **cement his anti-mandate, anti-mask brand** – reinforcing to his supporters that he stands against what they saw as excessive government imposition during COVID.

Furthermore, it exemplifies Trump’s prioritization of **loyalty to his base’s beliefs over deference to scientific guidance**. Public health experts largely credited vaccination mandates with boosting uptake and saving lives; Trump’s scrapping of them suggests a willingness to sacrifice some health security to appease political sentiments (e.g., those who were anti-vaccine or anti-mask). It fits the pattern of him downplaying COVID risks and eschewing collective measures in favor of individual choice.

Practically, one effect is that **some unvaccinated individuals who lost federal jobs or contracts might be brought back**, and ongoing litigation by mandate opponents becomes moot (a “win” for them). It also frees agencies from having to track vaccination status or handle exemption requests – reducing administrative burden, albeit at the cost of lesser protection. A subtle intent might be to **purge or chill more pro-science or pro-safety voices** in government; employees who strongly believed in the mandates (and thus opposed by Trump’s view) get a signal their approach is no longer welcome.

**Fit into Trump’s Broader Agenda:**  
EO 14174 aligns with Trump’s broader theme of **undoing Biden’s legacy, especially on hot-button culture issues**. Just as he targeted DEI on Day 1, here he targets COVID mandates – both of which were flashpoints in partisan discourse. It also fits with his general **deregulatory instinct**: removing rules on businesses (federal contractors) and rolling back conditions on federal employment. 

Moreover, it’s part of rehabilitating Trump’s own pandemic narrative. His first term ended amid COVID chaos; by his second term, he presumably wants to treat COVID as “solved” or at least not a federal concern. It ties into his frequent claims that **the pandemic response under Biden was heavy-handed and economically damaging**. Revoking these EOs lets him claim he set things right on behalf of workers and businesses. It’s also likely coordinated with other Day 1 actions – for instance, if he lifted any remaining travel restrictions or disbanded certain COVID-19 task forces, all to project that *“we’re moving on.”*

Another agenda item this supports is **shrinking the federal workforce indirectly**: Many agencies under Biden hired additional staff or contractors for COVID-related work (like testing, screening, enforcement of protocols). With mandates and emphasis gone, those roles might be deemed unnecessary, perhaps making it easier to cut jobs or budgets in areas tied to pandemic response. This would feed into any government downsizing goals Trump has.

Lastly, it ingratiates him with certain unions and employees who opposed the mandates (some federal employee unions had mixed stances). By siding with those who resisted, he attempts to broaden his support among working-class constituencies, portraying himself as the defender of their bodily autonomy. This EO thus is not just administrative but deeply tied to Trump’s **political identity as the anti-“deep state,” anti-elitist president** who rejects what he frames as nanny-state rules.

**Harm Caused:**  
While the country in 2025 wasn’t in crisis mode, this EO still has tangible harms:

- **Workplace Health Risks:** Removing the vaccine mandate and other safety protocols could lead to **more COVID-19 infections among federal employees and contractors**. Even if severe cases are fewer by 2025, outbreaks can still disrupt operations and endanger vulnerable workers. A fully vaccinated workforce was safer; now clusters of unvaccinated individuals might facilitate spread. This could particularly affect those who are immunocompromised or cannot get vaccinated – their workplace becomes riskier. Also, if new variants emerged, the government would be slower to reinstate protections given this stance, potentially exacerbating future waves.

- **Setting Back Public Health Precedent:** The EO contributes to the narrative that **public health mandates are illegitimate**. This might discourage other institutions from implementing needed measures in future health crises. It also undermines **respect for science-based decision-making** within the federal government. Agencies may become more hesitant to follow CDC guidance or require health measures for other diseases (e.g., if there’s a bad flu outbreak, they might avoid mandates fearing political blowback). In short, it erodes institutional memory and willingness for collective action for public health.

- **Loss of Accountability for Contractors:** Under Biden’s EO 14042, contractors had to ensure safe workplaces; with that gone, **some contractors might relax standards not just for COVID but generally**. There’s a loss of a uniform baseline that all contractors had to meet, which could lead to patchy protections. In addition, **workers employed by federal contractors who felt safer under the mandate now lose that security** – e.g., a janitorial crew on a federal contract may now have coworkers who are unvaccinated and unmasked, causing anxiety or actual harm if one brings an infection.

- **Demoralizing Pro-Vaccine Employees:** The majority of federal employees did get vaccinated (compliance was high). For those who did so out of commitment to protecting colleagues and the mission, seeing the mandate abruptly dropped can be demoralizing. It might feel like **their sacrifices (and compliance) are devalued** and that the leadership doesn’t actually care about health. Meanwhile, employees who defied the mandate and perhaps even filed lawsuits could feel vindicated – potentially emboldening future defiance of workplace rules they dislike. This dynamic can weaken overall **discipline and respect for policies** in the civil service.

- **Potential Return of Disrupted Employees:** Some harm could come in the complexity of reinstating employees or contractors who were removed for non-compliance. Agencies might be forced to rehire outspoken anti-vaccine employees who had, in some cases, spread misinfo or sown discord. Navigating those returns could be tricky and conflict-ridden in offices, impacting team cohesion and productivity. Similarly, for contractors, companies that lost contracts due to non-compliance might litigate for compensation, costing taxpayer money or causing legal headaches.

- **Broader Public Example:** The federal government often sets norms. By dropping its mandates, it possibly influenced state/local governments or private companies to drop theirs. In a worst case, if a more dangerous COVID variant appears, the lack of a federal mandate can hamper a swift, unified response, thereby causing higher infection rates and associated suffering than had basic protections remained normalized.

**How a Future Administration Could Undo or Repair Damage:**  

1. **Reissue Public Health Directives if Needed:** The new President could, after consulting health experts, **reissue an updated EO for federal workforce COVID safety** if conditions warrant (for example, if a new variant causes a surge). This new EO might not simply copy Biden’s old ones, given political sensitivities, but could mandate **up-to-date vaccination (including boosters) for federal employees and on-site contractors**, with appropriate medical/religious exemptions. It can tie mandates to hospitalizations or community risk levels to appear more targeted. Even short of a vaccine mandate, a new admin should **empower agencies to implement necessary health measures** without political interference.

2. **Restore a Coordinated Safety Framework:** A future administration should rebuild some form of **Federal Workplace Safety Task Force** (which Biden had), to provide guidance on not just COVID but any infectious disease threats. This would ensure that even without blanket mandates, agencies have a science-based blueprint to protect staff. For instance, guidelines on ventilation, masking policies in outbreaks, testing, and leave policies when sick. Having these standards centrally coordinated repairs the piecemeal approach that Trump’s EO left.

3. **Support Vaccination and Health Education:** Instead of coercive measures alone, the new leadership can **invest in health education and free vaccination clinics for employees**. Ensure all federal employees have easy access to the latest vaccines and accurate information. Over time, this repair helps increase voluntary compliance. The administration could also consider incentives (like extra leave day or small bonus for employees who keep current on recommended vaccines) to rebuild a culture of vaccination. While not a mandate, it nudges the workforce toward higher immunity levels again.

4. **Address Federal Workforce Policy Holistically:** Recognizing the controversy, a future admin might approach it via **bargaining and engagement with federal unions and employee groups**. By getting buy-in on health measures (perhaps negotiating protocols in union contracts), the policies will be more durable and less divisive. Repairing damage includes healing the labor relations strain that mandates caused; doing so could make re-implementing necessary measures more accepted.

5. **Evaluate and Reinstate Effective Practices:** Not everything from Biden’s EOs was about vaccines; there were also improved safety practices like occupancy limits, telework flexibility, etc. A new admin should **review which pandemic-era practices were beneficial** (e.g., allowing telework when sick instead of forcing people in). Keeping some of those permanently (for flu season, etc.) can maintain a healthier workforce. It shows lessons learned weren’t thrown away due to politics. For example, if masking in high transmission saved a lot of sick days, agencies could quietly encourage it again in winters.

6. **Acknowledge Mistakes and Empower Science Advisors:** Part of repair is **acknowledging the importance of evidence-based policy**. The new President might not reinstate mandates immediately, but could signal that if experts advise them in future, they won’t hesitate (with proper explanation and compassion). Re-empowering entities like the CDC and Occupational Safety and Health Administration (OSHA) to set guidance for workplaces is key. Under Trump, OSHA had been hesitant to enforce COVID safety in workplaces; a new admin could direct OSHA to develop enforceable infectious disease standards, which would outlast executive whims.

7. **Plan for Future Emergencies:** Use this episode to push for a more resilient framework. Maybe work with Congress on **updating the Federal Workforce Flexibilities Act** or similar, to clarify what authorities exist in a health crisis. If laws explicitly allowed vaccine requirements for federal employees (or conversely explicitly barred them), it would remove doubt. Ideally, codify the ability of agencies to require CDC-recommended vaccines for the workforce (like they do for other diseases in certain roles) to prevent this whiplash.

8. **Rebuild Public Communication:** Externally, the new administration should carefully communicate why certain protections might return if needed, framing it apolitically. They can emphasize **protecting the workforce and citizens is non-negotiable**, but also outline thresholds and timelines so it’s not seen as arbitrary. By being transparent and consistent, they can recover some public trust that was eroded when mandates were cast as partisan. 

In essence, undoing the damage means **reinstating a culture and practice of health responsibility** in the federal government, albeit with sensitivity to the controversies. While Trump’s EO cannot be undone retroactively (some harm like lost lives can’t be fixed), the government can prepare better and ensure that **future public health measures are grounded in consensus and clear communication**. This way, if a crisis hits, the response won’t be hampered by the legacy of politicization left by EO 14174.

---

## EO 14175 — **Designation of Ansar Allah as a Foreign Terrorist Organization**  
**Alternative Title:** *“Reigniting the Yemen Crisis by Branding the Houthis as Terrorists”*

**What the EO Does:**  
- **Initiates FTO Designation of the Houthis:** Directs that **Ansar Allah (the Houthi movement in Yemen) be considered and treated for designation as a Foreign Terrorist Organization (FTO)**. Under U.S. law (Section 219 of the Immigration and Nationality Act), an FTO designation is a powerful tool that makes it a federal crime to provide material support to that group and triggers severe sanctions. Trump’s EO sets in motion the process: within 30 days, the Secretary of State must consult intelligence and Treasury and report on designating Ansar Allah; within 15 days after that, take all appropriate steps to carry out the designation. It essentially **commands the State Department to swiftly enact the terrorist label on the Houthis**, reversing their delisting by Biden in February 2021.
- **Targets Humanitarian Actors:** The EO goes further to scrutinize those interacting with the Houthis. It orders that *after* designation, the Secretary of State and USAID Administrator review all UN partners, NGOs, and contractors working in Yemen and **“identify any entities” that either (i) made payments to Houthi-controlled entities or (ii) criticized international anti-Houthi efforts while not documenting Houthi abuses sufficiently**. It then says USAID shall **terminate projects or contracts with those entities** as appropriate. This clause is a direct shot at humanitarian organizations: in Yemen, aid groups often must pay fees at Houthi checkpoints or coordinate with Houthi authorities to deliver aid, and some advocated against the FTO designation because it would hinder aid. Trump’s EO implies such groups are suspect and could be cut off from U.S. funding for essentially “dealing with the enemy” or opposing U.S. policy.
- **Justifies via Iranian Threat:** The Purpose section of the EO describes the Houthis as **supported by Iran’s IRGC and responsible for attacking U.S. ships and destabilizing the region**. It frames designation as part of U.S. policy to *“eliminate Ansar Allah’s capabilities... deprive it of resources, and end its attacks on U.S. partners and shipping”*. Thus, it ties into Trump’s maximum pressure campaign on Iran and alignment with Gulf allies (Saudi Arabia/UAE) against the Houthis.
- **General Provisions:** It includes boilerplate that nothing in the order should affect other authorities or create legal rights, and notes implementation is subject to availability of appropriations. But these don’t limit the core thrust: to get the Houthis formally branded terrorists and cut off any U.S. support to those in Yemen who engage with them.

**What it Claims to Do (Stated Justification):**  
The EO presents itself as a necessary national security action. It cites a litany of Houthi aggression: *“fired at U.S. Navy warships dozens of times since 2023”*, threatened Red Sea shipping (contributing to global inflation by forcing rerouting of ships), attacked U.S. partners and civilians. Trump positions the Houthis squarely as *“terrorists”* akin to other U.S.-designated groups, backed by Iran’s IRGC. 

The **stated justification is to protect American lives and commerce and to support regional allies**. The EO’s policy statement emphasizes cooperating with partners to *“eliminate Ansar Allah’s capabilities”* and *“end its attacks”*. In essence, Trump claims designating the Houthis will help choke off their funding and weapons and thereby reduce the threat they pose to U.S. ships, Saudi Arabia, the UAE, and the stability of global trade. 

It also implies **restoring pressure that Biden lifted**: it notes it’s setting in motion the process consistent with law, implying the previous removal was a mistake that Trump is correcting to align with U.S. anti-terrorism policy. Additionally, by highlighting Houthi ties to Iran, it justifies this as part of the broader confrontation with Iran’s malign activities. The EO does not explicitly mention humanitarian impact; instead, it tacitly downplays it by accusing those who objected (like the UN) of not sufficiently documenting Houthi abuses, thus framing any concern over humanitarian fallout as misplaced or even sympathetic to terrorists. Officially, the goal is protecting the world’s busiest shipping lanes and civilians from a ruthless terrorist group – a hardline stance that appeals to a law-and-order mindset in foreign policy.

**Real Purpose or Effect (Critical Perspective):**  
Critically, this EO is seen as a **deeply punitive move that prioritizes a militaristic, hardline approach at the cost of Yemeni civilians’ well-being**. The real purpose appears to be **satisfying certain regional allies (Saudi Arabia and the UAE) and domestic hawks** by treating the Houthis as terrorists, even though such a designation was widely warned to have dire humanitarian consequences ([Biden revokes terrorist designation for Yemen's Houthis | AP News](https://apnews.com/article/joe-biden-donald-trump-civil-wars-yemen-d17b50e3995827838a19fb8bd09e9f64#:~:text=President%20Donald%20Trump%E2%80%99s%20administration%20had,the%20provision%20of%20aid%20to)). It reimposes a Trump end-of-term policy that even his administration’s career staff had concerns about. 

The effect is that **virtually all humanitarian and economic lifelines to Houthi-controlled northern Yemen are criminalized or chilled**. An FTO designation means banks will refuse transactions that might touch Houthi areas, suppliers won’t sell food or medicine there for fear of U.S. prosecution, and aid agencies risk legal jeopardy if they pay any fees to Houthi authorities (which they must do to operate). When Trump’s State Dept. originally labeled the Houthis terrorists in January 2021, humanitarians warned it would cause *“famine and death”* because aid could not get through. Biden removed the designation precisely *“due entirely to the humanitarian consequences”* which the UN said would be catastrophic. Now Trump is willfully reimposing it, so the real intent seems to be **to starve and weaken the Houthis into submission by cutting off resources – effectively using starvation as a form of warfare**. This approach was described by critics as *“weaponizing aid”*, punishing an entire population under Houthi rule to hurt the Houthi leadership.

Moreover, the EO’s language about reviewing NGOs and UN partners smacks of intimidation towards the humanitarian community. The real message: **if you want U.S. funding, don’t criticize our hardline policy and don’t engage with the de facto authorities in Yemen even to deliver aid**. It’s a stark politicization of aid – demanding absolute alignment with U.S. counterterrorism goals or face defunding. This could silence aid organizations from even voicing concern as conditions worsen, which is dangerous because those voices are crucial for calibrating policy to avoid mass suffering.

Another likely intent: to burnish Trump’s image as *tough on terror and Iran*. Domestically, calling the Houthis terrorists plays well to those who see Yemen’s conflict in simplistic terms of Iran vs. the world. It also may appeal to evangelical End Times views or neo-conservatives who frame Middle East conflicts starkly. So part of the purpose is domestic politics – differentiating Trump from Biden by painting Biden as having been weak on “Iran’s proxy terrorists” and Trump as the opposite. It distracts from the complex reality that the Houthis, while problematic, control territory with 20+ million people who rely on aid.

In the region, it aligns the U.S. fully with Saudi/UAE war aims. In 2021, those allies were upset when Biden delisted the Houthis. Trump re-listing them is a gift to those governments, likely in exchange for their support of other Trump goals (like normalization deals or oil policies). So the real effect diplomatically is **U.S. policy tilting completely to the Saudi-led coalition** in Yemen, abandoning even the pretense of neutrality in facilitating peace talks. It undermines the fragile ceasefires or negotiations that were developing (in 2024 there were some talks underway) by making the U.S. essentially take a side of maximal pressure on one party.

Finally, it’s worth noting Trump’s EO basically instructs State to do the designation “consistent with 8 U.S.C. 1189” but the outcome is predetermined – he wants them listed. So this is a politicization of the FTO process (normally a deliberative process with intel assessments). The real intent is policy outcome, not following an impartial process. This sets a precedent of using FTO designation not purely on security merits but as a broad political tool, which could degrade the credibility of the FTO list generally.

**Fit into Trump’s Broader Agenda:**  
EO 14175 fits Trump’s broader “America First” and **hardline foreign policy** stance, reversing what he sees as Biden’s “soft” multilateralism. It underscores a theme of **eschewing humanitarian considerations in favor of brute force approaches**. This mirrors his approach to Iran (maximum sanctions regardless of civilian impact), to immigration (harsh deterrence despite humanitarian outcry), etc. It’s consistent with his administration’s skepticism or hostility toward the UN and NGOs – now accusing them of being insufficiently anti-Houthi.

It’s also a piece of Trump’s Middle East strategy: reaffirming strong bonds with Gulf monarchies and Israel in a potential anti-Iran coalition. Designating the Houthis pleases Saudi Arabia (which lobbied for it) and signals a return to the Trump-era alignment after Biden had tried a more balanced approach. It likely ties into Trump’s goal of an overarching anti-Iran front, possibly to justify future confrontation with Iran (since IRGC support to Houthis is cited).

Additionally, it dovetails with his general stance of **undoing Obama/Biden’s diplomatic moves**. President Obama had avoided calling the Houthis terrorists even amid war; Biden lifted the terror designation. By reinstating it, Trump symbolically crushes that legacy and reasserts his doctrine of coercion over diplomacy. This could please his base who often view anything short of total victory as weakness.

From a domestic policy vantage, it’s one of several EOs where Trump overrides expert consensus because it conflicts with his narrative. (Another example is EO 14181, where he overrides environmental laws because they conflict with his water narrative). Here, expert consensus was that FTO listing Houthis = famine. Trump overrides that, showing his willingness to flout expert advice if it appeases base instincts or allies. That’s a hallmark of his agenda – **go with gut instinct and political loyalty over technocratic or humanitarian expertise**.

Lastly, EO 14175 might serve as part of a pressure campaign not just on the Houthis but also on Iran as nuclear deal diplomacy collapsed. Trump could be positioning the US as tougher on all Iran proxies, perhaps prelude to further actions (like sanctioning more Iranian entities or even considering use of force). This fits the agenda of **increasing confrontation** in contrast to Biden’s attempts at negotiation.

**Harm Caused:**  
The harms of this EO are primarily borne by Yemeni civilians and the humanitarian system:

- **Humanitarian Catastrophe in Yemen:** By designating the Houthis as terrorists, **the flow of food, medicine, and fuel into Houthi-controlled Yemen (which is most of North Yemen, including the capital Sanaa) would be severely impeded**. International banks and companies would likely halt transactions with Yemeni entities for fear of violating U.S. law. Even if the U.S. offers some humanitarian exemptions, experience from other sanctions shows they are often insufficient – suppliers and shippers engage in broad over-compliance. Yemen is extremely import-dependent (around 90% of food is imported). So, this move **puts millions at risk of famine**. The United Nations had warned that Yemen’s food situation was dire and that an FTO listing could push it into *“the worst famine seen in decades”* ([In foreign policy shift, Biden lifts terrorist designation for Houthis in Yemen | PBS News](https://www.pbs.org/newshour/show/in-foreign-policy-shift-biden-lifts-terrorist-designation-for-houthis-in-yemen#:~:text=Arab%20world%27s%20poorest%20country%20into,will%20suffer%20from%20acute%20malnutrition)). With this EO, those warnings become reality: **food prices would skyrocket, shortages would worsen**. Malnutrition, especially among children (already affecting millions), would deepen. The AP reported in 2021 that such a designation was expected to *“accelerate the world’s worst humanitarian crisis”* – Trump’s action essentially chose to accelerate it.

- **Obstruction of Aid Delivery:** Humanitarian agencies would struggle to operate. If they continue working in Houthi areas, they risk U.S. prosecution for providing “material support” (e.g., paying local taxes or even buying fuel that might benefit a Houthi-controlled supplier). The EO explicitly threatens to cut their funding if they had contact with Houthis. As a result, many aid groups might **withdraw from northern Yemen entirely to avoid legal issues**, leaving a huge population without services. The ones that remain may scale down to life-saving aid only, and even that could be tied up by licensing requirements. Lives will be lost due to **lack of timely medical care, clean water, and sanitation** that NGOs provide. Diseases could spread more (Yemen has had cholera and COVID outbreaks) as healthcare collapses further.

- **Economic Freefall:** What little is left of Yemen’s economy would take a hit. Remittances might stop if money transfer companies fear doing business there. Yemeni-Americans sending money to family could be cut off. Traders who import basic goods could halt operations, causing **market collapse and massive unemployment** in an already impoverished place. This increases dependency on aid precisely when aid can’t come. It’s a vicious cycle pushing Yemenis deeper into destitution.

- **Undermining Peace Efforts:** Politically, labeling one party to a conflict as terrorists **torpedoes peace negotiations**. The Houthis would have zero incentive to negotiate or compromise once designated – it’s an existential threat to them. They might retaliate by escalating attacks (as they have nothing to lose), leading to more violence. Meanwhile, the U.S. loses any ability to act as a mediator. The UN’s role is also hampered; UN envoys talking to Houthis could technically be seen as interacting with terrorists. So, this EO likely **prolongs the Yemen war**, dashing hopes raised by a period of relative calm in late 2024. Prolonged war means more destruction and suffering for civilians, and possibly regional escalation (if Houthis resume missile attacks on Saudi/UAE, drawing retaliation).

- **Punishing Aid Organizations and Free Speech:** The EO’s instruction to review and defund NGOs that “criticized international efforts to counter Ansar Allah” is chilling. It essentially punishes groups for speaking out on humanitarian grounds. This harms the principle of neutrality and advocacy in humanitarian work. Organizations might self-censor crucial reports about civilian harm or famine for fear of U.S. retaliation, which in turn **deprives the global community of accurate information** about the Yemen crisis. It sets a bad precedent that humanitarian advocacy can be equated with supporting terrorists. In the long run, this erodes international humanitarian law norms and could discourage agencies from engaging in the toughest crises (if doing so risks being smeared as terrorist sympathizers).

- **Secondary Regional Impacts:** If Yemen’s ports and finance shut down due to this, **wider Red Sea commerce might be affected** (insurance rates for ships might go up, etc.). Also, desperate Yemenis might flee (though blockade makes it hard) – any increase in refugees often strains neighboring countries like Djibouti or exacerbates the migrant crisis. Furthermore, this may push the Houthis closer to Iran for survival, ironically increasing Iranian influence – harm to U.S. strategic aims (blowback). And if Houthis respond by truly acting like a cornered terrorist group (e.g., attacking commercial ships or launching terror attacks beyond Yemen), global security could worsen.

- **Loss of US Credibility:** While perhaps not Trump’s concern, a harm is that **the U.S. is seen as deliberately causing mass suffering** despite warnings. This could severely tarnish America’s moral standing, especially in the Muslim world. It feeds narratives of U.S. double standards on human rights. In future crises, countries may be less inclined to listen to U.S. advice or coordinate on humanitarian carve-outs because the U.S. ignored them in Yemen’s case. This reputational damage can indirectly harm U.S. interests and soft power.

**How a Future Administration Could Undo or Repair Damage:**  

1. **Rescind the FTO Designation Immediately:** The most urgent step is for the incoming Secretary of State to **reverse the Foreign Terrorist Organization designation of Ansar Allah (Houthis)**. Mechanically, the State Department can delist an FTO if circumstances change. A new administration would likely determine that the designation is not in U.S. interests (due to humanitarian crisis) and move to revoke it. This could be done by a Federal Register notice, possibly after a quick review to satisfy procedural requirements. **Speed is critical** – the longer the designation is in place, the more damage accrues. So ideally on Day 1 or 2, the new SecState announces intent to revoke, and within a short time, it’s formally lifted. (Biden’s admin did this within a month of taking office in 2021, citing humanitarian reasons, and a future admin should repeat that precedent.)

2. **Issue General Licenses and Assurances in the Meantime:** If immediate revocation takes a few days/weeks, concurrently the Treasury’s Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC) should issue **broad general licenses** authorizing humanitarian transactions in Houthi areas, and the State Dept should assure banks and NGOs that they will not be targeted for legitimate aid activity. This helps limit over-compliance panic. Essentially, signal loudly: food, medicine, and civilian trade are *not* the target. Under Biden, OFAC did this even before formal delisting to calm fears; a new admin must do the same on day one.

3. **Restore Humanitarian Aid Funding:** Reinstate funding to any NGO or UN agency that was cut off by Trump’s directive. If USAID terminated grants/contracts per EO 14175, the new admin can use emergency authorities or reprogram funds to get those organizations back in operation ASAP. They should also publicly renounce the clause about punishing NGOs for policy advocacy, making clear that under the new leadership, humanitarian partners will not be penalized for speaking truth about humanitarian needs. This encourages aid groups to return and ramps up assistance distribution to recovering communities.

4. **Re-engage Diplomatically for Peace:** With the terrorist label gone, the U.S. can resume a diplomatic role. The new President could appoint a Special Envoy for Yemen (as Biden did) to restart negotiations. Work with the UN envoy and push for a renewed ceasefire or peace talks, communicating to the Houthis that the U.S. has removed the designation as a goodwill gesture and now expects them to come to the table. Also reassure Saudi Arabia and UAE that U.S. will contain Houthi military capability through other means (continued arms interdictions etc.) but that a political solution is needed. Essentially, pivot from a militaristic approach to a peacemaking approach – something impossible while the FTO status was in place.

5. **Increase Humanitarian and Economic Support:** To repair damage, the new administration should **launch a humanitarian surge for Yemen**. Increase aid funding (and convince Congress if needed) to help pull Yemen back from the brink after the starvation period. Support the UN humanitarian appeal generously and lead a donor conference. Also, consider steps to stabilize Yemen’s economy: e.g., unfreezing any Yemeni central bank funds, supporting currency exchange stabilization, and encouraging Gulf allies to provide grants for Yemen’s fuel and basic goods. Part of repair is helping Yemenis rebuild trust that the international community will not abandon them again. 

6. **Legislative or Policy Guardrails:** To prevent a future recurrence of such harmful designations, the new admin might quietly support legislation requiring humanitarian impact assessments before an FTO designation in a case like Yemen (maybe an amendment to the INA). At minimum, internally, they can establish a **policy that any terrorist designation decision must involve consultation with USAID/UN and a mitigation plan if civilians will be affected**. This makes it harder for a future president to unilaterally do such a thing without pushback from within the system.

7. **Work with Allies to Isolate Extremes:** The new admin can coordinate with allies (like EU, which never followed Trump’s designation in 2021 due to humanitarian reasons) to ensure broad consensus that Houthis should not be treated as pariahs in negotiations. Essentially, remove any international fig leaf that might have persisted after Trump’s EO. If any countries followed suit in declaring Houthis terrorists due to U.S. pressure, urge them to reverse as well. The goal is to **normalize humanitarian dealings with Houthi areas** so aid flows from everywhere, not just the U.S., without hindrance.

8. **Humanitarian Carve-outs in Counterterrorism**: More broadly, the new admin could strengthen policies that **protect humanitarian action in counterterrorism contexts**. For example, update U.S. counterterrorism sanctions regimes to include standard exemptions for humanitarian work (some progress was made internationally on this in late 2021 at the UN). This would be a systemic fix that outlasts just the Yemen scenario, ensuring that in places like Afghanistan, Syria, etc., life-saving aid isn’t held hostage to CT policies. Pushing this approach helps repair the trust and functionality between the U.S. government and aid sector after it was eroded by EO 14175’s approach.

By swiftly reversing the designation and actively committing to Yemen’s humanitarian relief and conflict resolution, a future administration can save countless lives and restore U.S. policy to a more humane and pragmatic course. It essentially has to **clean up the “famine bomb” that Trump’s EO set off** by removing that trigger and flooding the area with support instead of sanctions. Over time, helping to stabilize Yemen and reduce the war will also enhance U.S. security and moral standing, repairing the intangible but significant harm Trump’s move caused to America’s reputation.

---

## EO 14176 — **Declassification of Records Concerning the Assassinations of President John F. Kennedy, Senator Robert F. Kennedy, and the Reverend Dr. Martin Luther King, Jr.**  
**Alternative Title:** *“Fueling Conspiracy Theories under the Pretext of Historic Transparency”*

**What the EO Does:**  
- **Orders Rapid Release of JFK Assassination Files:** Mandates that within **15 days**, the Director of National Intelligence and Attorney General present a plan for the “full and complete” declassification and public release of all remaining government records related to President John F. Kennedy’s 1963 assassination. This essentially forces a swift disclosure of any JFK files that previous administrations (including Trump’s first term) had still kept secret citing national security. Over 50 years later, a few thousand documents remained redacted or withheld; this EO says release them all **“without delay”**.
- **Expands to RFK and MLK Assassination Files:** Similarly, it requires that within **45 days**, the DNI and AG review all records related to the 1968 assassination of Senator Robert F. Kennedy and the 1968 assassination of Dr. Martin Luther King Jr., and present a plan to fully release those. This is significant because RFK’s and MLK’s assassination records were not previously subject to a JFK Records Act (there was a specific law for JFK records but not for RFK/MLK). Trump’s order thus sweeps them in, demanding their secrets also be spilled.
- **Policy Declaration:** States that more than 50 years after these events, the government’s continued withholding of related records is unjustified: *“their families and the American people deserve transparency and truth”*. It flatly says it’s in the national interest to release **“all records related to these assassinations without delay.”** This framing suggests any remaining secrecy is a disservice to history and the public.
- **No Exceptions Mentioned:** The EO does not carve out many explicit exemptions (beyond the standard general provisions about not impairing other authorities or creating rights). It doesn’t explicitly say “unless genuinely necessary for national security.” By saying “full and complete release,” it implies even intelligence documents or names of informants etc., should be disclosed. Essentially, it overrides prior justifications agencies had for postponement.
- **Follow-Up and Implementation:** It doesn’t describe the mechanism beyond the plans at 15 and 45 days, but presumably, this triggers agencies (CIA, FBI, etc.) to declassify and transfer records to the National Archives for public release. The aggressive timeline indicates Trump wanted this largely done by late February 2025. 

**What it Claims to Do (Stated Justification):**  
Trump’s EO couches itself in terms of historical justice and transparency. It notes *“more than 50 years”* have passed and yet not all records are public, implying the government has been overly secretive for too long. It suggests that releasing everything is necessary to finally provide *“truth”* to the American people and the families of JFK, RFK, and MLK. 

The official line is that it’s *“in the national interest”* to do this now. By grouping these three assassinations (traumatic, oft-conspiracy-shrouded events of the 1960s), Trump paints himself as the president who will *“let the people know what really happened,”* something enthusiasts across the political spectrum have sought. He references the **President John F. Kennedy Assassination Records Collection Act of 1992**, acknowledging that law aimed to release JFK files by 2017 but agencies kept some redactions. Trump implies he’s fulfilling the spirit of that law (which Congress unanimously passed) by forcing compliance now. 

For RFK and MLK, there’s no equivalent law, so he’s using broad authority. The justification presumably extends the same principle: these cases are of immense historical importance and any government secrets about them should finally come to light to quell rumors and distrust. He might have argued that transparency here will bring closure and dispel conspiracy theories by showing the government has nothing to hide. Essentially, the EO claims to champion open government and lay to rest some of America’s enduring mysteries.

**Real Purpose or Effect (Critical Perspective):**  
Critics suspect the real motive is less about noble transparency and more about **pandering to conspiracy theorists and deflecting attention from current issues**. Trump has long courted those who distrust government narratives (including QAnon and others who often link back to JFK conspiracies). By releasing these assassination records en masse, Trump feeds the fascination and paranoia around “deep state” plots. It’s likely he hopes to **validate or at least fuel suspicions that shadowy forces were involved in these historic killings** – forces possibly analogous to what he calls the “deep state” opposing him. In effect, it’s a wink to his base: *“They lied about JFK, etc., what else are they lying about? I will expose it.”*

The actual effect of such rapid release might be **chaotic and misleading**. The files likely contain a lot of raw intelligence and speculation (e.g., CIA notes on various leads, informants’ tips, etc.). Dumping them quickly without thorough context could lead to *misinterpretation*. Conspiracy entrepreneurs might cherry-pick ambiguous bits to spin new theories (e.g., a CIA cable speculating about some figure could be taken as proof of CIA involvement). There’s a risk that rather than providing “truth,” it **floods the public with fragments that intensify mistrust**. 

Also, some redactions protect still-living informants or methods. Trump’s order suggests even those be lifted, which could be irresponsible. For example, if a still-living person was suspected or an informant in MLK’s case, naming them now could cause harassment or worse. The EO doesn’t dwell on such risks, implying Trump prioritized the spectacle of release over any collateral damage.

Another real intent could be **to distract media and public attention** early in his term with a sensational topic unrelated to his governance. While people dive into JFK files, the administration could be quietly doing other controversial things with less scrutiny. It’s a tactic of flooding the zone.

It might also be personal: Trump has a known fixation on JFK assassination theories (he tweeted in 2017 blaming Ted Cruz’s father in a smear, for instance). He may genuinely relish “proving” some pet theory or at least stirring the pot. In doing so, he also can take a swipe at intelligence agencies (CIA, FBI) by implying they wrongfully hid info for decades. This fits his feud with the intel community – it’s an attempt to embarrass them or reduce their aura of trustworthiness by revealing they kept secrets. Essentially, it’s a chance to portray those agencies as potential villains of history, which indirectly supports his narrative when they oppose him on current matters (e.g., FBI investigating him – he can say “these are the same people who might have hidden truth about JFK!”).

**Fit into Trump’s Broader Agenda:**  
This EO is a bit of an outlier compared to tangible policy changes, but it fits Trump’s **anti-establishment, anti-“deep state” persona**. It aligns with his second-term approach of settling scores and revealing supposed truths the “establishment” covered up. During 2024, he likely campaigned that he *“will tell you what really happened with JFK, etc.”* – appealing to a segment of voters who distrust government narratives. This follows through on that.

It also complements his government overhaul theme. While other EOs fired bureaucrats or reversed policies, this one targets the historical narrative – essentially an ideological component of draining the swamp. He’s saying *not even the secrets of the CIA/FBI from 60 years ago are safe from me.* It’s a dramatic assertion of presidential power over the bureaucracy.

Additionally, it’s a legacy play – something he can tout as having done what previous presidents (even Biden, who delayed full JFK release till at least 2026 for some files) didn’t. It plays into his personal branding as a maverick truth-teller. 

This EO might also serve as a bone to certain constituencies (some populist left also want these files open). But largely it’s a piece of his narrative that **the U.S. government long deceived its people, and only Trump will force it into the light**. It’s not unrelated that he targeted three assassinations that spawned many conspiracy theories about government involvement. Absent is, say, declassification of 9/11 intelligence or other issues – he chose ones implicating FBI/CIA possibly. That furthers his vendetta against those institutions.

We should note: in Dec 2022, Biden actually ordered more JFK files released, and by late 2022 ~97% were public. The ones left were arguably the most sensitive. Trump’s first term delayed some releases at CIA’s request. So why now push it? Possibly because now it’s politically useful to him, whereas in 2017 he still had mainstream advisors urging caution.

**Harm Caused:**
