For example, it **revokes EO 12898 (1994) on environmental justice, EO 13583 (2011) on workforce diversity, and EO 13672 (2014) expanding LGBTQ protections in federal contracting**. It even **revokes EO 11246 (1965)** – a foundational order prohibiting federal contractors from discrimination and requiring affirmative action. Essentially, it wipes out decades of civil-rights-oriented directives in hiring and contracting.  
- **Bans “DEI” in Government:** Orders all agencies to **terminate any “discriminatory and illegal preferences” or “equity” programs** and to strip concepts like “advancing equity” from regulations. Agencies must **purge guidance, programs, and trainings that reference DEI** and cannot consider factors like race or gender identity in hiring or grants.  
- **Targets Federal Contractors:** Instructs the Labor Department’s Office of Federal Contract Compliance Programs to **stop enforcing affirmative action or diversity requirements on government contractors**. Contractors no longer need to take proactive steps to ensure diverse hiring, and **cannot be required to include DEI criteria** in their workforce practices. New contract clauses must certify contractors comply with anti-discrimination laws but do not run “illegal DEI” programs.  
- **Expands “Merit”-Only Focus:** Emphasizes recruitment of “highly skilled Americans dedicated to American ideals” and suggests any consideration beyond individual merit (implying racial or gender-based outreach) is an “impermissible factor”. It aligns all hiring and grant-making practices with a strict interpretation of meritocracy, rejecting affirmative action or diversity goals.  
- **Enforcement and Private Sector:** Directs agencies not only to change their own policies but also to **“combat illegal private-sector DEI”** if it’s deemed to violate civil-rights laws. This hints at the government potentially challenging corporate diversity programs as unlawful, extending the anti-DEI campaign beyond government to society at large.

**What it Claims to Do (Stated Justification):**  
The EO frames itself as a defense of true equality and civil rights. It argues that **civil-rights laws guarantee non-discrimination** and that contemporary DEI initiatives actually *“violate those laws by giving preferences based on race or sex”*. The text asserts that so-called “diversity, equity, and inclusion” policies are *“dangerous, demeaning, and immoral… under the guise of ‘equity’”* and amount to an *“unlawful, corrosive… identity-based spoils system.”* It claims these practices *“stigmatize, demean, or shut out”* people (implying white or male candidates) and undermine *“traditional American values of hard work and excellence.”* The **stated goal is to restore a focus on individual merit** and ensure no one is disfavored or preferred due to race or sex. In Trump’s framing, this EO is enforcing equality by **banning “illegal discrimination” under the banner of DEI** and making the workforce purely merit-based for the “best-qualified”. He casts his actions as **protecting the civil rights of all and ending divisive practices**, implying that the previous administration’s equity policies were themselves illegal or unfair.

**Real Purpose or Effect (Critical Perspective):**  
In effect, EO 14173 **dismantles affirmative action and diversity efforts** within the federal government and its contractors. The lofty “merit” language serves as a pretext to **roll back programs benefiting women, minorities, and LGBTQ people**. By revoking EO 11246 and related orders, it removes requirements for proactive nondiscrimination in hiring and contracting, effectively ending enforcement of affirmative action by federal contractors. Eliminating EO 12898 on environmental justice and other inclusion initiatives signals **abandoning federal attention to racial disparities** in pollution and hiring. 

From a critical left viewpoint, the real intent is to **erase acknowledgment of systemic bias**: by forcing a “colorblind” approach, it halts measures designed to level the playing field. The EO’s emphasis on “merit” is used as a **pretext to permit institutions to ignore past and present discrimination**. For example, agencies will cease programs that recruited diverse talent or required outreach to underrepresented communities – those will now be seen as illegal “preferences.” **Federal hiring could become less diverse**, and **minority-owned businesses may lose out on federal contracts** without affirmative action requirements. The EO chills any consideration of race or gender – even lawful ones like targeted outreach or mentorship – because agencies fear being accused of running afoul of this order. 

While Trump justifies this as protecting civil rights, it’s telling that **all the orders he revoked were about expanding opportunities for marginalized groups**. The EO’s true effect is to **entrench the status quo of who holds power** (historically, white men) by removing tools that encouraged inclusion. It prioritizes a narrow view of “merit” that ignores **structural barriers many Americans face**. In practice, **workers of color, women, and LGBTQ employees lose supportive policies** (like diversity training or inclusive hiring programs) and could face more subtle discrimination that was previously being addressed through DEI initiatives.

**Fit into Trump’s Broader Agenda:**  
EO 14173 exemplifies Trump’s second-term crusade to **uproot “woke” policies** in government. On Day 1 (Jan 20, 2025), he had issued EO 14151 *“Ending Radical and Wasteful Government DEI Programs”*. EO 14173 on Jan 21 continues that purge with specific actions, showing a systematic removal of diversity policies. It aligns with his campaign rhetoric that **“woke bureaucracy” must be dismantled**. 

This plays to his base by confronting buzzwords like “equity” and “inclusion,” which right-wing discourse derides as leftist social engineering. It also fits his administrative strategy to reshape the civil service: **by emphasizing “American ideals” and “merit,”** Trump could justify purging employees he deems ideologically opposed (under the guise they were hired for “woke” reasons rather than merit). For example, the EO’s language about hiring only those dedicated to “American ideals” hints at loyalty tests substituting for what used to be diversity considerations.

Additionally, this EO must be viewed alongside others targeting gender and race issues (e.g., EO 14168 on “Restoring Biological Truth” in government or EO 14183 on military policies). Together, these actions paint a picture of an administration doubling down on **culture war themes**: anti-affirmative action, anti-“critical race theory,” anti-LGBTQ inclusion. Trump’s agenda is clearly to **erase Obama-era diversity gains** and even roll back some Great Society-era civil rights advances, under a banner of “merit” and “unity” that critics see as code for preserving majority privilege.

**Harm Caused:**  
- **Loss of Equal Opportunity Programs:** Federal agencies will stop or reverse programs that successfully recruited diverse talent or helped underrepresented employees advance. Mentorships for women in STEM, internships targeting minority college students, leadership training for people of color – all such efforts could be canceled as “illegal preferences.” **Agencies may revert to old networks and biases in hiring and promotions**, making it harder for qualified diverse candidates to get in the door or rise. Over time, the federal workforce could become less reflective of America’s diversity, undermining its ability to serve an increasingly diverse public. 
- **Impact on Federal Contractors:** By lifting affirmative action requirements, **federal contractors may no longer be held accountable for diversity in their ranks**. Some companies might quietly scale back diversity recruiting or drop inclusive hiring goals since they’re no longer required – or even fear being penalized for having such goals. This can lead to fewer job opportunities for women and minorities in private firms doing business with the government. The EO explicitly orders contractors to stop “promoting ‘diversity’” and “affirmative action”, effectively telling companies to drop DEI efforts. That could legitimize discriminatory practices under the guise of neutrality.
- **Reduced Civil-Rights Enforcement:** Agencies are told to enforce only the “text” of civil-rights laws and remove “equitable decision-making” from procedures. This strict reading may cause agencies to **shy away from robustly addressing systemic discrimination**. For example, the Justice Department might curtail investigations into patterns of racial bias (viewing proactive equity initiatives as verboten). The Education Department might back off guidance on racial disparities in school discipline. **Revoking EO 13672** (which had extended nondiscrimination protections to LGBTQ federal contractors) removes clear protections for transgender and queer workers, making them more vulnerable to discrimination. The overall harm is both immediate (loss of explicit protections) and chilling (agencies and employers might not protect those workers, thinking the administration doesn’t value those protections).
- **Communities Losing Environmental Justice Focus:** EO 12898’s revocation means **less attention to pollution and climate impacts on minority and low-income neighborhoods**. Agencies like EPA or HUD may deprioritize grants or enforcement targeting environmental disparities. This could exacerbate health and environmental inequalities, as the government might ignore race/income when addressing issues like toxic waste sites or disaster recovery. Vulnerable communities that benefited from targeted federal help could be left to fend for themselves, widening health and quality-of-life gaps.
- **Workplace Culture and Morale:** For many federal employees, especially those from marginalized groups, this EO sends a demoralizing message that their employer will no longer celebrate or support diversity. It **invalidates employee resource groups, diversity councils, and training programs** that foster inclusive culture – possibly even banning implicit bias training or LGBTQ Pride observances. The harm is a less welcoming workplace, which can drive talented staff away (a “brain drain” of women, minority, and progressive employees) and reduce the quality of public service. Those who remain may feel forced to **closet aspects of their identity or experiences**, knowing leadership sees those as irrelevant or taboo.
- **Public Trust and Service Efficacy:** A government that does not prioritize understanding diversity may be less effective in serving different populations. For example, **language access services might be cut as “equity” frills**, harming non-English speakers seeking federal aid. Or lack of diversity in staff could lead to cultural blind spots (e.g., FEMA without environmental justice lens might inadequately serve marginalized disaster survivors). Over time, the public (especially minorities) may trust the government less, perceiving it as indifferent or hostile to their inclusion. This undermines the promise of equal opportunity that the federal government is supposed to champion.

**How a Future Administration Could Undo or Repair Damage:**  
- **Revoke EO 14173 Immediately:** The next president should issue an EO nullifying EO 14173 and explicitly **reinstating the directives it canceled**. For instance, re-issue or reaffirm **EO 11246, 12898, 13583, 13672** and others that were revoked. This sends a clear signal that diversity, equity, and inclusion are back as priorities. Each rescinded measure can be restored by referencing its original text or via new orders that capture their intent (e.g., a new comprehensive EO requiring agencies to develop DEI strategic plans, enforce contractor affirmative action, etc.).  
- **Restore Affirmative Action in Federal Operations:** Through executive action and policy memos, instruct the Office of Personnel Management (OPM) and agency heads to **resume diversity recruitment programs, training, and accountability measures**. Reinstate the requirement (from EO 11246 and related rules) that federal contractors **take affirmative action to ensure equal employment opportunity**. The Office of Federal Contract Compliance Programs should again enforce diversity in contractor workforces, including goals and timetables where appropriate. Essentially, reverse the instructions that stopped OFCCP from promoting diversity and affirmative action among contractors.  
- **Issue New Equity Guidance:** The new administration’s OMB and Domestic Policy Council should produce **government-wide equity guidance** (similar to Biden’s 2021 EO 13985) to re-embed equity considerations into federal policymaking. This means agencies must assess how policies affect disadvantaged groups and take action to remedy disparities. All the language Trump had struck (like “advancing equity”) should be woven back into federal grant criteria, strategic plans, and performance measures. Such guidance, possibly backed by a new EO, can institutionalize equity review of policies—making it standard practice again to factor in race, gender, and economic disadvantage in policy decisions.  
- **Strengthen Civil Rights Enforcement Units:** To repair the neglect or chilling effect, the next administration should empower civil-rights offices in each agency – giving them resources and support to actively investigate discrimination complaints and enforce laws. They should clarify that pursuing disparate-impact cases or doing outreach to marginalized communities is not only allowed but encouraged (contrary to Trump’s implications). For example, DOJ’s Civil Rights Division should ramp up enforcement of voting rights and police reform (areas often impacting communities of color) without fear that equity-focused cases are seen as improper. The EEOC and OFCCP should be directed to again collect data on workforce diversity and pay gaps, and use it to drive enforcement – resuming what might have been shelved by Trump’s EO.  
- **Outreach and Reassurance:** It’s crucial to rebuild trust with federal employees and communities. The President and cabinet officials should **meet with federal employee affinity groups, civil-rights organizations, and community leaders** to affirm the government’s renewed commitment to diversity and equal opportunity. Internally, agencies can re-launch or bolster employee resource groups and diversity councils, making clear those are supported at the highest levels. Externally, communications should highlight that programs like environmental justice grants or minority business development are back online. Essentially, loudly signal that the federal government values inclusion and will actively support it, counteracting the previous message.  
- **Embed Protections in Law Where Possible:** To guard against future flip-flops, the administration could work with Congress to **codify key anti-discrimination and affirmative action measures**. For instance, Congress could enact legislation requiring federal contractors to have affirmative action plans (writing EO 11246’s mandate into statute). Or incorporate environmental justice into environmental laws, so that agencies must consider impacts on low-income/minority communities regardless of who is President. While challenging, even incremental steps (like explicitly authorizing agencies to use outreach programs to diversify their workforce) would provide legal backing that a future executive order alone can’t undo.  
- **Monitor and Course-Correct:** The new administration should set up a **task force or commission** to monitor the reimplementation of DEI policies and evaluate outcomes. This could be an interagency Equity Council that tracks hiring, promotion, contracting data, and assesses whether diversity is improving or where gaps remain. If Trump’s EO caused significant drops in representation or upticks in complaints, the task force can identify those and recommend targeted actions or investments (like mentorship programs, partnerships with HBCUs, etc.) to address them. Over its term, this mechanism ensures the damage from the hiatus in DEI efforts is being actively repaired and that progress is quantifiable.  
- **Publicly Celebrate Diversity:** An intangible but important repair is a change in tone from the top. The new administration should vocally celebrate America’s diversity as a strength and speak to the importance of inclusion in every sector. Presidential speeches, agency messaging, and public campaigns can reinforce the positive value of a diverse workforce and inclusive policies, countering the narrative that “merit” and “diversity” are at odds. By doing so, it helps heal polarization around these issues and builds political support to sustain the reinstated policies.

By explicitly undoing EO 14173’s provisions and actively **rebuilding the machinery of equity and inclusion** within government, the future administration can restore the momentum toward equal opportunity. It will take diligent effort – in policy and culture – but these steps will help ensure the federal government once again strives to be a model of fair chance and representation for all Americans, not a bastion of majority privilege.

---

## EO 14174 — **Revocation of Certain Executive Orders**  
**Alternative Title:** *“Scrapping COVID Safety Rules for Federal Workers and Contractors”*

**What the EO Does:**  
- **Nullifies Biden’s COVID Safety Mandates:** This EO **revokes two specific Biden-era executive orders from September 2021**: **EO 14042 (“Ensuring Adequate COVID Safety Protocols for Federal Contractors”) and EO 14043 (“Requiring Coronavirus Vaccination for Federal Employees”)**. By doing so, it **ends the federal contractor COVID-safety requirements** (like mask mandates and vaccination/testing rules for contractor personnel) and **ends the vaccine mandate for federal employees**. These Biden orders had required federal workers to be vaccinated (with exceptions) and contractors to follow COVID safety measures; Trump’s EO scrubs those requirements entirely.
- **No Replacement Standards:** The order does not establish any new COVID guidelines – it simply cancels the existing ones. It includes a standard caveat that nothing in the order should impair other authorities or laws, but effectively, it **leaves decisions about workplace COVID precautions to individual agencies or existing general OSHA rules**, with no government-wide mandate.
- **Immediate Effect on Policies:** With EO 14042 and 14043 revoked:
  - Federal agencies can no longer enforce termination or discipline of employees for failing to vaccinate. Any ongoing suspension or pending removal actions for non-compliance are nullified. Employees previously fired for mandate non-compliance might be eligible for reinstatement or other remedies (some legal cases on this were in play; Trump’s EO likely led agencies to offer return or settlement).
  - Federal contractors no longer need to require their employees to be vaccinated or wear masks/test while on federal worksites. Contract clauses imposing those requirements become void. Agencies presumably issue guidance modifying or removing those contract clauses.
  - Federal workplace protocols (like mask requirements in federal buildings, visitor vaccination attestations, etc., which stemmed from the contractor and employee mandates) are relaxed. Many of these measures had already been scaled down by late 2024, but any that remained are effectively lifted by this signal from the top.
- **Symbolic Rejection of Pandemic Measures:** Coming on Jan 21, 2025, this was largely symbolic – a “Day One” action to emphatically break with the prior administration’s approach to COVID. The brevity of the EO suggests Trump felt no further explanation was needed beyond referencing his authority. It was a clear statement that *“the federal COVID mandates are over.”*

**What it Claims to Do (Stated Justification):**  
The EO itself provides no explicit justification in its text beyond the legal authority to revoke orders. However, contextually, the justification was implicit: **Trump campaigned on ending COVID “restrictions” and restoring personal freedom and normalcy**. He and many Republicans viewed the federal vaccine mandate as government overreach that infringed on personal liberty and hindered workforce morale and hiring. By revoking these orders, Trump would claim to be *“ending the unjust forced vaccination of Americans”* and *“removing unnecessary burdens on businesses and workers.”* 

The timing (after the height of the pandemic) allowed him to argue that such mandates were no longer needed for public health and were actually harming the government by causing resignations or compliance issues. In essence, the intended message was: **the pandemic is effectively over, and the federal government will no longer impose broad COVID rules on its employees or partners**. This aligns with his stance that personal choice should prevail and that any ongoing COVID measures under Biden were an overreaction or power grab.

So, although the EO doesn’t spell it out, Trump’s justification likely emphasized **restoring individual freedom, improving federal workforce morale, and aligning policy with the pandemic’s waning threat** (as he saw it). It would also cast Biden’s mandates as harmful (claiming perhaps they caused staffing shortages or were unfair to those with natural immunity, etc.), and that revoking them was *“common-sense given we have vaccines available and should move on.”*

**Real Purpose or Effect (Critical Perspective):**  
From a critical perspective, the real purposes were largely political and ideological rather than grounded in public health best practices. Trump’s swift scrapping of these mandates serves to **symbolically erase Biden’s approach to COVID**, reinforcing his narrative that *“government mandates did more harm than good.”* It plays to his base’s resentment of COVID restrictions.

The immediate effect was that **federal workplaces and contractors could drop vaccination and masking policies**. This likely:
- Led to agencies allowing unvaccinated employees to return to regular duty without restrictions. Some agencies that had kept testing for unvaccinated staff stopped doing so.
- Ended any hiring constraints – previously, new federal hires had to be vaccinated; that was gone, opening hiring to the unvaccinated (potentially a significant ideological portion).
- Possibly required agencies to **reinstate or give back pay to some employees who were disciplined/fired** for noncompliance with the vaccine mandate (as many had contested those actions and courts had put the mandate on hold by late 2022). Trump’s EO essentially conceded those employees’ stance.
- Signaled to contractors and private sector that the era of federal COVID rules is finished, aligning with the broader societal rollback of mandates.

The **real intent** was ideological: to underline personal freedom and **repudiate the scientific consensus that mandates were beneficial**. It shows Trump’s continuing inclination to downplay COVID risks (a continuation of his attitude in 2020) and to frame pandemic measures as part of a culture war (freedom vs. tyranny). 

Also, by doing this, Trump likely aimed to boost federal workforce productivity in his view – believing that without mandates, there’d be fewer conflicts or accommodations and more people physically back at work (because vaccine holdouts might have been barred from in-person work). It fits his pattern of prioritizing **perception of normalcy and loyalty to base preferences over deference to expert guidance**.

Additionally, it served as a **“red meat” accomplishment to announce**: on day one he “freed” federal workers from mandates – a promise kept to the anti-vax or mandate-skeptical segments of his coalition.

**Fit into Trump’s Broader Agenda:**  
This EO aligns with Trump’s broader theme of **undoing Biden’s policies across the board**, especially where they involve regulations or what his base sees as “government control.” Just as he attacked “woke” policies (EO 14173) and climate regulations (elsewhere), here he attacks public health mandates. It’s part of a general rollback of what Trump portrays as the prior admin’s overreach.

It also fits his **populist, anti-establishment persona**: rejecting advice of the “bureaucrats and scientists” in favor of what he frames as common people’s rights. In his second term planning, he made clear he’d target the “deep state” – this includes public health agencies like CDC and mandates they inspired. Revoking these EOs is a step in diminishing those agencies’ influence (implicitly rebuking CDC’s recommendation for federal worker vaccination, etc.).

Politically, it’s designed to be highly visible but not costly: by 2025, most of the benefit of mandates (raising vaccination rates) was realized, and public appetite for mandates had waned. So he gets credit from his base for killing the mandates without a huge immediate public health consequence (assuming no new variant surge at that time). It’s a *symbolic victory over the “COVID controls”* that lingered in their minds.

It also ties into his **small-government ideology** – eliminating federal directives and letting individuals and states handle things. With this EO, he might argue the federal government shouldn’t be in the business of forcing medical decisions on people, consistent with conservative principles (though they diverge on other mandates, like abortion, ironically). This limited-government stance is part of his agenda when it suits his base.

**Harm Caused:**  
- **Workplace Health Risks:** Removing vaccination and safety requirements likely made federal workplaces **more susceptible to COVID-19 outbreaks**. While vaccines were widely available by 2025, dropping mandates could lead to lower booster uptake or more unvaccinated new hires. This increases the risk of transmission in offices, which could **endanger vulnerable employees** (the immunocompromised, or those living with high-risk individuals). Agencies might see more sick leave usage or disruptions if outbreaks occur that previously may have been mitigated. Even mild outbreaks can hinder operations (e.g., several TSA officers out sick at once could slow airport security). The termination of any testing programs means asymptomatic cases might go undetected, further spreading virus.
- **Setting a Precedent Undermining Public Health Authority:** By so starkly rejecting these mandates, the EO could embolden resistance to public health measures in the future. It sends a message that **public health mandates are negotiable or political**, not based on science. This could make it harder to institute measures in a future pandemic or even a seasonal surge (if, say, a new COVID variant or severe flu emerged, federal managers might be hesitant to impose even temporary masking or encourage vaccination, recalling this precedent). The harm is a potential **slower or weaker response to future health crises**, risking greater spread and impact.
- **Uneven Protections & Confusion:** Some agencies or contractors in areas with local rules or different risk assessments might have wanted to keep certain precautions. Trump’s sweeping EO could cause conflict or confusion if, say, a federal building in a city with an indoor mask advisory is no longer allowed to require masks because the federal mandate was lifted. It might also have nullified any agency plans that were tailored to protect their workforce (for example, if a VA hospital wanted staff vaccinated to protect patients, Trump’s EO complicated that). **Inconsistent policies** across agencies and locations could result, causing confusion for employees and visitors.
- **Morale and Trust Issues:** The vaccine mandate had broad compliance (around 95% of federal workers complied by late 2021). Many who did so believed in the collective good. Yanking the mandate may have **demoralized employees who valued the safety of a fully vaccinated workplace** and followed the rules. They might feel their sacrifices (getting vaccinated, possibly over colleagues’ objections) are devalued. Conversely, employees who resisted mandates feel vindicated – which could embolden future resistance to directives they dislike. This dynamic might **undermine general discipline and respect for policies** in the federal workplace. If “wait it out until the next election” becomes a way to dodge compliance (as some did with the vaccine mandate, given courts blocked it and now it’s gone), that’s not healthy for organizational cohesion.
- **Potential Reinfection and Long COVID:** While 2025 may have seen lower COVID mortality than 2020-21, the virus hadn’t vanished. Removing protective measures likely led to more infections among the federal workforce that could have been prevented. Each infection carries risk of Long COVID or other complications. Some employees could suffer long-term health issues reducing their productivity or requiring workplace accommodations (e.g., due to chronic fatigue). That’s a direct harm to those individuals and a subtle productivity cost to agencies. By contrast, sustained higher vaccination rates and occasional testing might have avoided some of those cases.
- **Political Polarization of Public Health:** This action further politicized a public health issue. It told those skeptical of vaccines that they were “right” and the government is recanting, potentially **fueling anti-vaccine or anti-science sentiment** beyond the federal workforce. This can spill into general public attitudes: if the federal government no longer requires its own staff to vaccinate, some Americans might interpret that as tacit acknowledgment that vaccines are not important – undermining public health messaging and vaccination campaigns for other diseases. Already by 2025, childhood vaccination rates for diseases like measles were dipping in some areas due to politicization; moves like this could exacerbate that harmful trend.

**How a Future Administration Could Undo or Repair Damage:**  
- **Reissue Public Health Directives as Needed:** If COVID (or another infectious disease) still poses a significant threat, the new President could **issue a new executive order or directive for federal workplace safety**. This need not identically reimpose blanket mandates, given political sensitivities, but could mandate measures under specific circumstances (e.g., *“if community transmission is high, agencies shall implement masking and testing protocols per CDC guidance”*). The key is to **restore an option for mandate-like measures if warranted by public health conditions**, instead of a flat ban. This can be couched as being guided by CDC/OMB evaluation of conditions, to appear more data-driven and flexible. If a new variant emerges, having this authority ready is crucial.
- **Empower Agencies and Follow Science:** Encourage agencies, in consultation with federal occupational health experts, to **set policies that protect their workforce and mission**. A new administration can explicitly revoke any Trump-era memos that discouraged agency-specific mandates. Instead, issue OMB or OPM guidance that agencies can require reasonable health measures (vaccination, testing, PPE) for their personnel or contractors if needed to ensure continuity of operations and safety. Essentially, **devolve some power back to agencies** with oversight, rather than a one-size-fits-all prohibition or requirement. This repair fosters adaptability and lets, say, VA hospitals require flu or COVID vaccines for staff interacting with patients, which was standard before COVID politicization.
- **Restore Public Health Expertise in Decision-Making:** Under Trump, CDC and health experts were sidelined on federal workforce decisions (the mandate removal was political). A new admin should **re-center expertise**. For example, re-establish a Federal Occupational Health Task Force including CDC, OSHA, OPM, and agency health officials to regularly review health risks and recommend any needed workforce protections. This would revive something akin to the Safer Federal Workforce Task Force that existed under Biden. By institutionalizing it, adjustments (like reintroducing a mask rule during a bad winter surge) come from experts, which may increase employee buy-in.
- **Repair Relationships with Federal Unions and Workforce:** Federal employee unions had mixed reactions to mandates (some supported, others concerned about bargaining). A new admin should work with unions to **jointly develop any future health protocols**, incorporating their input on how to implement in a fair way. This can rebuild trust and avoid the feeling of top-down imposition. Also, if any employees were unjustly treated in the chaos of mandate removal (e.g., those fired who didn’t get relief), the new admin could find ways to address that – perhaps offering reemployment or ensuring their records reflect neutral reasons for separation. Demonstrating concern for workforce welfare (balancing safety and rights) will help morale.  
- **Reinforce Science-Based Messaging:** The new administration should clearly communicate why certain protective measures may be reinstated if needed, framing it as **following the science and protecting the workforce and public**. For example, the President or OPM Director can say, *“If COVID or any disease threatens our workforce’s ability to serve the public, we will take necessary measures based on expert guidance – as we do for any workplace hazard.”* By depoliticizing and normalizing the concept of health safety measures, it might reduce backlash. Emphasize successes: if a new policy stops an outbreak at a federal workplace, publicize that benefit to show such steps are practical, not political.  
- **Ensure Adequate Sick Leave and Flexibility:** One thing Trump’s EO didn’t address but is part of pandemic response is encouraging sick people to stay home. A new admin should ensure generous **sick leave policies or telework options** remain for employees with COVID or other contagious illness. This helps prevent workplace spread, mitigating the effect of having less vaccination enforcement. Repairing damage means not just reissuing mandates, but also bolstering the supportive policies that can achieve much of the same goal (fewer sick people at work means fewer outbreaks). For instance, continue allowing telework when feasible, so immunocompromised employees or those in high-transmission areas can work safely. Such policies show care for employees’ health in a non-coercive way, rebuilding goodwill.
- **Invest in Ventilation and Long-Term Safety:** Instead of relying only on personal compliance, a new admin can push **facility upgrades (ventilation, air filtration)** in federal buildings to reduce disease spread. This was a longer-term Biden initiative; reviving and funding it would provide passive protection regardless of individual vaccination choices. It’s a repair that improves resilience to any airborne illness and isn’t politically fraught. Also, offer voluntary on-site vaccination clinics and strong education campaigns for boosters – make doing the healthy thing easy and encouraged, even if not mandated. Over time, these efforts can raise immunity levels and safety culture without formal mandates.
- **Plan and Legislate for Future Public Health Authority:** To avoid whiplash, the admin might work with Congress on clarifying **federal agency authority in health emergencies**. For example, explicitly authorize the President to require employee vaccination in a declared pandemic of specified severity, to remove it from partisan debate. Or update OSHA laws to cover airborne infectious disease standards in workplaces (so that basic protections become regulatory norms). While passing such laws is tough, even having the conversation signals a commitment to principled readiness and may yield incremental gains, like funding for continuous health monitoring of federal workplaces.
- **Public Health Restoration Beyond Federal Workforce:** Though this EO is about federal mandates, its repeal was a symbolic shift that affected public attitudes. The new admin should broadly **recommit to public health leadership** – supporting vaccination campaigns, countering misinformation (e.g., through HHS and CDC communications), and leading by example. For instance, visibly following CDC guidelines for masking in high-risk situations, which under Trump may have been flouted, can set a tone. If another surge happens, making sure federal government responds robustly (with testing programs, guidance, etc.) will rebuild credibility that was eroded. Essentially, demonstrate that science and compassion, not politics, guide decisions on public health.

In summary, while one cannot retroactively impose a mandate that was lifted (and by 2025, many Americans including federal workers had some immunity), a future administration can **restore a framework where science-based health measures are not off-limits**. By being more nuanced and collaborative, they can protect the workforce and public when needed, and **repair the trust in government’s role in public health** that EO 14174’s abrupt, purely political move undermined.

---

## EO 14175 — **Designation of Ansar Allah as a Foreign Terrorist Organization**  
**Alternative Title:** *“Reigniting the Yemen Crisis by Branding the Houthis as Terrorists”*

**What the EO Does:**  
- **Initiates FTO Designation of the Houthis:** Directs that **Ansar Allah (the Houthi movement in Yemen) be considered and treated for designation as a Foreign Terrorist Organization (FTO)** under U.S. law. It orders the Secretary of State, in consultation with the Director of National Intelligence and Secretary of Treasury, to report within 30 days on designating Ansar Allah as an FTO and, within 15 days thereafter, to take all appropriate action to carry out that designation. In plain terms, Trump’s EO effectively **commands the State Department to formally list the Houthis as a terrorist organization**, reversing the Biden administration’s removal of that designation in February 2021.
- **Targets Humanitarian/NGO Interactions:** It goes further to scrutinize humanitarian organizations operating in Yemen. After any designation, the EO instructs the Secretary of State and USAID Administrator to **review all United Nations partners, NGOs, and contractors working in Houthi-controlled Yemen** and identify any that have (i) *“made payments to entities controlled by Ansar Allah”* or (ii) *“criticized international efforts to counter Ansar Allah while failing to document Ansar Allah’s abuses sufficiently.”*. USAID is then directed to **terminate projects or funding with any such entities as appropriate**. This effectively threatens to cut off U.S. funding to aid organizations that the administration believes cooperated with or were soft on the Houthis. Many aid groups pay fees at Houthi checkpoints or negotiate with them to deliver aid – this clause implies those could be cause for defunding, as could public dissent over the designation itself.
- **Justifies via Iranian Threat & Houthi Attacks:** The EO’s purpose section frames it by noting *“60 years after the Civil Rights Act”* ironically in EO 14173; focusing here, it justifies Houthi designation by citing how *“the Houthis, supported by Iran’s IRGC Quds Force, have fired at U.S. Navy warships dozens of times since 2023”*, and how their actions threaten Americans, regional partners, and global trade. It also notes Houthi attacks forced maritime traffic rerouting, contributing to global inflation. The policy stated is to *“eliminate Ansar Allah’s capabilities and operations, deprive it of resources, and thereby end its attacks”* on U.S. interests and partners. Thus, it ties the move to protecting Americans and allies from Houthi aggression.
- **Implementation and Legalities:** It includes boilerplate that nothing in it should impair other authorities or existing laws and that it depends on appropriations available. It also explicitly states it isn’t creating any enforceable legal right for parties (meaning individuals or NGOs can’t sue the government based on this EO). In practice, after Trump’s order, the Secretary of State (presumably Mike Pompeo again, who originally designated Houthis in Jan 2021) would quickly move to finalize the FTO listing, which would come with Treasury designations as well.

**What it Claims to Do (Stated Justification):**  
The EO presents itself as a necessary security step. It asserts:
- The Houthis (Ansar Allah) are **terrorists who threaten Americans and international stability**: *“Supported by Iran’s IRGC-QF, the Houthis have fired at U.S. Navy warships… threaten global maritime trade… and endanger American civilians and personnel in the Middle East.”*. 
- It’s U.S. policy to **work with regional partners to eliminate the Houthis’ capabilities and end their attacks**. Essentially, it casts the designation as part of counter-terrorism and protecting freedom of navigation.
- By ending illegal preferences (tying to EO 14173 logic in domestic, but here it’s ending “preferences” for not calling Houthis terrorists), it implies the previous decision to not label them terrorists was a political correctness or misplaced humanitarian concern that undermined civil rights of actual victims.
- Although not explicitly in text, the administration likely argued that **any humanitarian concerns can be managed** and that it will “ensure aid continues” even with the FTO tag (this is what Trump officials said in Jan 2021 via general licenses, albeit not credibly). So the stated justification is almost purely security-focused: **punishing a dangerous Iranian proxy to save lives and pressure Iran**.

There’s no mention of Yemen’s humanitarian crisis in the justification; the omission implies the administration viewed those concerns as secondary or exaggerated. The clause about NGOs *“criticizing international efforts…while failing to document Houthi abuses”* suggests Trump officials felt the humanitarian sector was biased or not telling the full story of Houthi atrocities, thus justifying clamping down on them too.

So, officially: **the EO claims to enforce U.S. anti-terrorism law consistently by labeling the Houthis as terrorists due to their actions, thereby cutting their funding and protecting the U.S. and its allies.**

**Real Purpose or Effect (Critical Perspective):**  
Critically, this EO is viewed as a **punitive, politically motivated move that ignores humanitarian fallout and undermines peace efforts**. The real intent seems to be:
- **Appeasing Gulf Allies and Appearing Tough on Iran:** Saudi Arabia and the UAE lobbied for the Houthi terrorist designation (they were angry Biden removed it). Trump’s EO is a gift to these allies – aligning the U.S. fully with their war in Yemen again. It also fits Trump’s anti-Iran posture: labeling the Houthis helps justify broader confrontation with Iran (as the Houthis are framed as an IRGC proxy).
- **Reversing Biden’s Diplomacy and Humanitarian Focus:** Biden revoked the FTO listing largely due to the *“world’s worst humanitarian crisis”* it would worsen. Trump’s reversal shows an **intent to repudiate Biden’s more humane approach**, even at the cost of Yemeni lives. It suggests a belief (or willful disregard) that starving or isolating the Houthis’ territory is an acceptable leverage. Essentially, Trump prioritized a maximal pressure strategy (similar to how he handled Iran – sanctions no matter the humanitarian impact) over humanitarian considerations. 
- **Weaponizing Aid and Silencing Critics:** The clause threatening NGOs indicates an attempt to **intimidate humanitarian organizations into silence and compliance**. Many aid groups and UN officials openly said FTO designation would cause famine. Trump’s EO basically says, “if you opposed our policy or engaged with the de facto authorities to deliver aid, we might cut your funding.” The real effect is a chilling one: NGOs might self-censor on policy critique and could even withdraw from Yemen to avoid legal trouble. It **politicizes aid** – making relief contingent on aligning with U.S. counterterror objectives. This is a stark shift from viewing humanitarian assistance as neutral. 
- **Sabotaging Yemeni Peace Prospects:** At the time, tentative peace talks (Oman-mediated) were happening between Saudis and Houthis in late 2024. By re-designating Houthis terrorists, Trump **sabotages U.S. support for negotiation** (U.S. officials can’t meet terrorists legally) and emboldens the Houthis to harden their stance (they survived one designation and likely see this as proof the U.S. wants to starve them). It fits Trump’s pattern of favoring force and sanctions over diplomacy. The likely real intent is to remove any pressure on Saudis to compromise (since now Houthis are just “terrorists” to be defeated, not negotiated with). This aligns with his broader agenda of **militaristic, unilateral foreign policy** vs. Biden’s multilateral peacemaking.
- **Domestic Political Posturing:** Domestically, being “tough on terror” is a classic stance. Trump can claim he’s *“fighting Iranian terrorism”*. It appeals to hawkish Republicans and perhaps some moderate voters who equate any revocation of terror designations with weakness. It also distracts from any nuance – it’s a simplistic narrative that plays well: *“Biden coddled terrorists, I crush them.”* Meanwhile, the nuance (that the “terrorists” govern a region of 20 million people reliant on aid ([Biden revokes terrorist designation for Yemen's Houthis | AP News](https://apnews.com/article/joe-biden-donald-trump-civil-wars-yemen-d17b50e3995827838a19fb8bd09e9f64#:~:text=President%20Donald%20Trump%E2%80%99s%20administration%20had,the%20provision%20of%20aid%20to))) is lost in that sloganeering. So part of the purpose is to score a domestic political win as a strong leader on national security, even if the policy is highly controversial in expert circles.

**Fit into Trump’s Broader Agenda:**  
This EO aligns with Trump’s **“America First” hardline foreign policy** – confrontational, dismissive of international humanitarian norms, and focused on **binary good-vs-evil framing**. It’s reminiscent of his approach to Iran, Cuba, and North Korea (maximum pressure), as well as his first-term alignment with Saudi Arabia (e.g., vetoing efforts to end U.S. support for the Yemen war). 

It also fits his pattern of **undoing Obama/Biden decisions** simply because they were “soft.” In fact, Trump’s State Department had originally made the Houthi FTO designation in its final days (Jan 2021), and Biden reversed it three weeks later. Trump returning to power and re-imposing it is almost vengeance or validation for his team’s original plan. It shows he’ll double down on even controversial last-minute policies if given the chance.

Additionally, it’s consistent with his tendency to **scorn multilateral institutions**. The UN and nearly all humanitarian agencies opposed calling Houthis terrorists; Trump’s action shows disregard for their warnings and maybe even a desire to punish them for “objecting to U.S. policy” (hence the NGO punishment clause). It’s a continuation of his antagonism toward UN bodies and global humanitarian frameworks, which he often derided as hindrances to U.S. freedom of action.

In the context of his overall second-term agenda, it’s part of a broader swing to the far right on foreign policy: re-embracing authoritarian allies (Saudi, likely Israel’s hardliners) and re-targeting longstanding U.S. adversaries (Iran, groups aligned with Iran) with no compromise. It emphasizes **military solutions and economic strangulation over diplomacy**, which is a through-line in his agenda (for instance, he’s also escalating confrontation with China in other EOs by prioritizing U.S. tech dominance, etc.).

**Harm Caused:**  
- **Severe Humanitarian Consequences for Yemeni Civilians:** Labeling the Houthis as terrorists triggers U.S. legal prohibitions on materially assisting them, which in practice means **aid and commercial supplies to Houthi-controlled northern Yemen are blocked or chilled**. Banks freeze transactions, insurers won’t cover shipments, suppliers fear prosecution for selling food/fuel that might result in tax revenue to a “terrorist” authority. This puts about 70% of Yemen’s population (who live under Houthi rule) at risk of **mass starvation and medical supply shortages**. When Trump originally did this in Jan 2021, the UN said 16 million people could go hungry and it would be *“catastrophic”*. Biden reversed it to avert famine. Now under Trump’s EO, those dire predictions likely come true: aid groups estimated *“the designation could lead to 400,000 more children starving to death”* ([Biden revokes terrorist designation for Yemen's Houthis | AP News](https://apnews.com/article/joe-biden-donald-trump-civil-wars-yemen-d17b50e3995827838a19fb8bd09e9f64#:~:text=one%20of%20the%20world%E2%80%99s%20worst,humanitarian%20disasters)). Already, Yemen is one of the world’s worst humanitarian crises ([Biden revokes terrorist designation for Yemen's Houthis | AP News](https://apnews.com/article/joe-biden-donald-trump-civil-wars-yemen-d17b50e3995827838a19fb8bd09e9f64#:~:text=President%20Donald%20Trump%E2%80%99s%20administration%20had,the%20provision%20of%20aid%20to)) – this action is akin to **pulling the lifeline on a patient in critical condition**. Food prices soar, fuel for hospitals or water pumps becomes scarce, and humanitarian operations grind to a halt because paying port fees or using local transport might violate U.S. law. The result is **potential famine and a spike in mortality from hunger and disease** in a population with minimal coping reserves.  
- **Collapse of Humanitarian Operations:** Many humanitarian organizations will likely **withdraw or suspend work in northern Yemen**, fearing legal repercussions or because their funding is cut (per the EO’s instruction to USAID). For example, the U.N. World Food Programme and NGOs might have to pause food aid, which millions depend on, until they get U.S. waivers or clarity. Even if the U.S. issues limited licenses for “humanitarian transactions,” the EO’s aggressive posture might make banks and partners unwilling to take the risk. Thus, **millions could be left without aid** that was literally keeping them alive (food aid, medical clinics, clean water projects). The withdrawal of aid can also cause **local economic collapse**, since many Yemenis rely on NGO jobs or services.
- **Setback to Peace and Increased Conflict:** The Houthis will view the U.S. as declaring total economic war on them, giving them little incentive to participate in peace talks. They may retaliate by, for instance, **launching more missile/drone attacks at Saudi or even U.S. targets** (like shipping or bases in the region) since they’ve been branded terrorists – a self-fulfilling prophecy of more terrorism due to being isolated. This could escalate the war after a period of relative calm, causing new casualties. The Saudis, feeling U.S. backing, might intensify military operations or siege tactics, further harming civilians. Meanwhile, **diplomatic efforts cease**: U.S. officials cannot engage the Houthis (illegal to materially support or even arguably to negotiate without a waiver) and the UN’s job is harder with one side designated as terrorists. The likely harm is **prolonging the Yemen war**, leading to more deaths, destruction of infrastructure, and trauma, with peace prospects dimmed.
- **Destabilizing Yemen’s Economy Entirely:** Yemen’s currency and banking system might collapse in the north under sanctions. Remittances from Yemeni expatriates could be blocked (a big income source for families) because money transfer companies don’t want to be accused of aiding a terrorist region. Imports of not just aid but commercial goods (food, medicine, fuel) shrink drastically. This could create a **devastating economic freefall**: businesses close, unemployment soars even beyond war-time norms, and people exhaust savings – exacerbating poverty and dependency. Even in government-controlled south Yemen, there could be spillover economic pain (e.g., if port operations at Houthi-held Hodeidah halt, it affects supply and prices countrywide). So, nationwide humanitarian needs could increase, overwhelming what remains of aid operations.
- **Harm to Aid Organizations and Neutrality Norms:** The EO’s crackdown on NGOs sets a dangerous precedent globally – it essentially punishes aid groups for working in a conflict zone and voicing humanitarian concerns. This harms the principle of **humanitarian neutrality**, as NGOs may be coerced into aligning with one side’s narrative to keep funding. It also damages the U.S.’s reputation among humanitarian workers; many might refuse U.S. government funding if it comes with such strings, reducing U.S. influence in relief efforts not just in Yemen but other crises (where NGOs might fear similar politicization). In Yemen, if USAID cuts off some local NGOs or UN partners, those programs disappear, and trust between aid community and U.S. is broken. The harm extends to global humanitarian response credibility – authoritarian regimes might use this precedent to label rebels as terrorists and block aid, citing U.S. practice.
- **Regional and Security Blowback:** Cutting off the Houthis might push them closer to Iran for survival (increasing Tehran’s influence over them), contrary to U.S. goals. It could also cause **mass migration/refugee flows** if famine hits – potentially tens of thousands of Yemenis could attempt to flee (though geography makes it hard – but we could see more boat refugees to the Horn of Africa, etc.). Refugee crises can destabilize neighboring countries and require international response. Moreover, if the Houthis are cornered, they might engage in more **kidnapping of foreigners** (to trade as hostages), maritime aggression (mines or attacks on ships in the Red Sea), or covert terror plots outside Yemen, ironically creating new security threats that didn’t exist when they were treated as de facto authorities under pressure to govern. In other words, by treating them purely as terrorists, they may act more like a typical terrorist organization beyond their borders.
- **Loss of U.S. Moral Standing and Diplomatic Capital:** This EO makes the U.S. appear callous to mass suffering, undermining its global human-rights stance. Allies in Europe who opposed the designation under Trump in 2021 will strongly object; this could create rifts in U.S.-European cooperation on Middle East policy. If famine occurs as predicted, **the U.S. could be blamed for precipitating it**, a severe stain on its moral record akin to being complicit in a humanitarian disaster. That can hamper U.S. soft power and make other nations less likely to support U.S.-led sanctions or designations if they fear such consequences. It also likely ignites anti-American sentiment in Yemen and the region, fueling extremist narratives that the U.S. starves Muslim civilians – ironically aiding terrorist recruitment (the opposite of purported goals).

**How a Future Administration Could Undo or Repair Damage:**  
- **Rescind the FTO Designation Immediately:** The highest priority is for the new Secretary of State to **reverse the Foreign Terrorist Organization (FTO) designation of Ansar Allah**. As Biden did via an emergency process in early 2021, the State Department can and should quickly remove the Houthis from the FTO list, citing humanitarian grounds and changed circumstances. This instantly lifts the legal prohibitions that choke off aid and trade. Ideally, this happens day one or within the first week of the new administration. Until formal delisting (which might require a short process), issue broad **waivers and licenses to protect all humanitarian and essential transactions** in the interim. Essentially, undo the legal mechanism causing the humanitarian blockade.
- **Restore Humanitarian Aid and Neutrality:** Immediately **restore any USAID funding to NGOs or UN agencies** that was cut due to this EO. The new USAID Administrator should send letters to partners assuring them they will not be penalized for delivering aid in Houthi areas or for past policy criticism. Reinstate a stance of neutrality: humanitarian aid will be allocated based on need, not politics. Also, re-engage diplomatically with the UN to coordinate a massive relief effort to reverse the famine trajectory. This might include convening donor conferences to fill funding gaps if others cut off aid during the designation. If projects were terminated, see if they can be quickly restarted or replaced. A surge of aid (food, fuel, medicine) in the immediate aftermath of delisting is necessary to stave off famine and show the U.S. is correcting the course.
- **Recommit to Peace Negotiations:** With the FTO designation lifted, the new administration can support and participate in renewed peace efforts. Appoint a Special Envoy for Yemen (as Biden had) to work with the UN and regional actors on reviving a ceasefire and political talks. Press Saudi Arabia and the UAE to halt offensive operations as a condition to delisting (if possible). Essentially, pivot from war footing to conflict resolution mode. This includes signaling to the Houthis that the U.S. is open to including them in negotiations now that they are not labeled terrorists, potentially re-opening channels that were frozen. Encouraging prisoner swaps, ceasefires around humanitarian corridors, etc., can help rebuild some trust. Over time, helping broker a peace deal (even if interim) will save lives and remove the rationale for such draconian measures as the FTO designation.
- **Address Humanitarian Harm Done:** Even after delisting, Yemen will have suffered. The new administration should **fund a humanitarian recovery plan**. This could mean increased aid to rebuild infrastructure (ports, hospitals, water systems) that may have deteriorated further. Work with international financial institutions to stabilize Yemen’s economy – for example, unfreeze any Yemeni central bank reserves if frozen, support currency exchange rate stability, push for debt relief or new development aid for Yemen. These steps can help Yemenis start to recover economically from the shock. Also, possibly provide targeted compensation or extra support to particularly affected groups (for instance, if farmers lost a planting season due to lack of fuel, fund an agricultural recovery program).
- **Engage with Humanitarian Organizations to Rebuild Trust:** The new administration should hold listening sessions with humanitarian NGOs about what they need after this ordeal. Possibly issue a formal statement or policy (or even push legislation) that **humanitarian activities will be shielded from broad sanctions** – sort of a “never again” assurance that the U.S. will prioritize avoiding civilian harm in its sanctions regimes. This will help repair U.S. relationships with aid groups and the UN, who were aghast at the previous policy. It demonstrates the new government’s respect for humanitarian principles. Additionally, drop any legal cases or investigations that Trump’s admin started against NGOs under the EO’s NGO clause.
- **Evaluate and Reform Sanctions Policy:** As a broader repair, the new administration could commission a review or issue new guidance on how to incorporate humanitarian impact assessments in sanctions decisions. Working with Congress, consider amending the sanctions laws to require such assessment or to carve out humanitarian exceptions more clearly. This can institutionalize lessons learned from Yemen so that no future president can as easily do something that risks famine. It also strengthens U.S. moral leadership on using sanctions responsibly. In parallel, ensure robust general licenses are always in place for humanitarian aid in any existing sanctions program (like those for Syria, Iran, etc.), perhaps expanding them.
- **Public Diplomacy in Yemen:** The U.S. should frankly acknowledge the hardship caused. Perhaps through statements by the new President or Secretary of State: *“We are lifting this designation because we recognize it impeded humanitarian assistance. We will always stand against the Houthis’ aggression, but not at the cost of Yemeni civilians.”* While stopping short of an apology (politically tricky), expressing relief that aid can resume and support for Yemen’s people could help salvage America’s image. Increase funding to programs that directly help civilians (like USAID health clinics, UNICEF child nutrition) to show a tangible change. If famine was developing, coordinate with the UN to do high-profile airdrops or ship convoys once delisted, demonstrating immediate action to feed people. Over time, consistent aid and supporting a peace process will be the real repair for Yemen’s people and will gradually improve opinions of the U.S. on the ground.
- **Maintain Pressure on Houthi Abuses Via Other Means:** The new admin can still address Houthi bad behavior without the FTO. They can sanction specific Houthi leaders via the Global Magnitsky Act for human rights violations, which doesn’t blanket criminalize aid in their territory. They can expose and condemn Houthi diversion of aid or use of child soldiers in UN forums to keep pressure on them, but in targeted ways. This narrower approach avoids collective punishment and focuses on accountability of leadership. Working with international partners to hold Houthis to ceasefires or negotiation commitments (carrot and stick) is more likely to change their behavior than isolating them completely. By shifting strategy to diplomacy plus targeted sanctions, the U.S. still pursues its interests (stopping attacks on ships/allies) while mitigating harm to Yemeni civilians.

In short, undoing EO 14175 requires **swiftly removing the terrorist label and its consequences, re-launching humanitarian aid at scale, and refocusing on peace efforts**. This will save lives and stabilize Yemen, and over time can help mend the moral and strategic damage the designation caused. It will demonstrate that the U.S. corrects course when policies produce unacceptable human suffering, thereby restoring some moral high ground lost under Trump’s approach.

---

## EO 14176 — **Declassification of Records Concerning the Assassinations of President John F. Kennedy, Senator Robert F. Kennedy, and the Reverend Dr. Martin Luther King, Jr.**  
**Alternative Title:** *“Fueling Conspiracy Theories under the Pretext of Historic Transparency”*

**What the EO Does:**  
- **Orders Rapid Release of JFK Assassination Files:** Mandates that within **15 days**, the Director of National Intelligence (DNI) and Attorney General (AG) present a plan for the *“full and complete”* declassification and public release of all remaining government records related to President John F. Kennedy’s 1963 assassination. This essentially forces the disclosure of any JFK files that previous administrations still kept secret for purported national security reasons. By law (the JFK Records Act of 1992), all JFK records were supposed to be released by 2017 unless specific postponements were authorized; some thousands of documents remained partially redacted or withheld. Trump’s EO says release them *“without delay”*, implying even those lingering secrets must come out now.
- **Expands to RFK and MLK Assassination Files:** It further requires that within **45 days**, the DNI and AG review all records related to the 1968 assassination of Senator Robert F. Kennedy and the 1968 assassination of Dr. Martin Luther King Jr., and present a plan to fully declassify and release those as well. There was no previous law mandating RFK/MLK file releases akin to JFK’s, so this EO proactively brings those under the same transparency push. Essentially, it instructs agencies to open up any FBI, CIA, etc., files on those cases that have been kept under wraps.
- **Declares Policy of Maximum Transparency:** It explicitly states more than **50 years have passed** since these assassinations and that the Federal Government *“has not released to the public all of its records related to those events. Their families and the American people deserve transparency and truth.”*. It asserts it’s in the national interest to *“finally release all records related to these assassinations without delay.”*. This is a strong policy statement essentially overriding any remaining secrecy concerns by invoking the public’s right to know after so much time.
- **Mechanism and Timeline:** The DNI and AG’s plans (at 15 and 45 days) presumably detail how to execute the release (which agencies need to declassify what). The EO gives short deadlines, indicating Trump wanted this done by mid-March 2025. It does not explicitly mention any exception process for sensitive info (unlike the JFK Records Act which allowed postponements for certain reasons). By saying “full and complete release,” it suggests an expectation that **even intelligence sources/methods or names of still-living individuals in those files should be disclosed**, unless agencies find a reason under law to resist.
- **General Provisions:** It includes standard language that nothing in the order should impair authority of departments (like classification authority) or require disclosing info that would harm national security or privacy as protected by law. However, this is generic and doesn’t detail how conflicts should be resolved. It likely assumes that if agencies want to keep something secret, they’d have to justify it to the President or possibly get an exemption.
- **Follow-up and Publication:** Implicitly, the National Archives (which houses JFK assassination records) would then release the records online or in their collection. Indeed, by 2025, ~95% of JFK files were public; this EO targets the last few percent, plus unknown quantities of RFK/MLK files.

**What it Claims to Do (Stated Justification):**  
The EO claims to serve **historic transparency and closure**. It says the American people and the victims’ families *“deserve transparency and truth”* about these assassinations. It notes that even after so long, not all records are public, implying that withholding them is no longer justifiable. By citing it’s *“in the national interest”* to release everything, the EO frames itself as correcting decades of excessive secrecy. 

The underlying message is that full disclosure will *“finally”* allow the nation to know the full story and perhaps lay conspiracy theories to rest. In particular:
- For JFK, there’s been a long saga of partial releases; Trump suggests he’s the one to complete the task mandated by the 1992 law (which allowed agencies to delay some releases until at most 2021, a deadline that had been extended by Biden to 2022-26 for remaining files).
- By including RFK and MLK, it signals these, too, are important unsolved puzzles in public memory, and the EO will bring *“truth and closure”* to them as well.

The EO invokes **families’ rights** (the Kennedys and King’s family have over the years expressed various views – MLK’s family for instance has pushed for more info on FBI surveillance of King). So it positions Trump as championing the victims’ families and public interest over bureaucratic secrecy.

In short, the stated justification is *“sunshine is the best disinfectant”* – that by revealing all these records, any lingering questions or doubts can be answered, satisfying historical curiosity and trust. It implies that continuing to hide things only fuels conspiracy theories, and he’s putting an end to that by ripping off the band-aid once and for all.

**Real Purpose or Effect (Critical Perspective):**  
Critically, while transparency is good, the context suggests Trump’s real motivations might be more self-serving or politically charged:
- **Playing to Conspiracist Leanings:** Trump has often flirted with conspiracy theories and the people who believe them. By releasing these files, he is **catering to those who suspect “deep state” foul play** in these assassinations. Many of his supporters and some Americans at large have long believed in conspiracies (e.g., CIA involvement in JFK’s death, etc.). Trump’s action would be seen by that audience as delivering on a promise to expose the “truth.” It’s akin to throwing a bone to QAnon-like believers or anyone deeply distrustful of government – a key part of his base. It reinforces his narrative that he’s an outsider willing to expose establishment secrets.  
- **Distracting from Current Issues:** Early in his term, this caused a media frenzy focusing on historical mysteries rather than his present-day actions. Critics suspect he used this as a diversion: while press and public pore over 60-year-old documents (which indeed happened – media covered the release extensively and conspiracy chatter spiked), his administration could push through other controversial moves with slightly less scrutiny. It’s a page from a playbook: create a spectacle (JFK files!) to overshadow news cycles that might otherwise focus on negative coverage of his other policies or turmoil.  
- **Undermining Intelligence Agencies:** If the withheld records had embarrassing info about CIA or FBI activities (for example, CIA plots to kill Castro that might tangentially relate to JFK’s murder, or FBI’s extensive wiretapping of MLK), releasing them could **damage the reputation of those agencies**. Trump has long feuded with FBI/CIA (“deep state” etc.). By forcing all secrets out, he might be aiming to embarrass those institutions, suggesting to the public *“look, these agencies hid things from you, they aren’t trustworthy”*. That serves him by bolstering his narrative as a victim of “deep state” in present matters (like FBI investigations of him). Essentially, it’s a way to discredit intel agencies using historical skeletons, which could make it easier to justify his second-term purges or overhauls of those agencies.
- **Personal Fascination and Scoring Points:** Trump reportedly had personal interest in JFK files (he partially released some in 2017 but held some back at CIA’s urging). Now, unconstrained, he might also be doing it for personal gratification, to say *“I was the one who finally did it.”* It’s a way to claim a unique legacy item that appeals across partisan lines (transparency advocates, historians appreciated the move albeit concerned about method). It’s something no prior president did fully, so it’s a bragging right. But it’s done in a way that doesn’t require legislative approval or budgeting – a low-hanging fruit that garners positive attention from some quarters.

The likely real effect:
- **Flood of Information without Context:** Thousands of pages of decades-old documents hit the public. Many are technical or fragmentary (e.g., raw intel reports, old memos). Without context, the public can misunderstand them. Conspiracy theorists may cherry-pick quotes to spin new theories (e.g., a CIA memo speculating about a second shooter might be taken as proof). Instead of settling debates, it could spark new ones. Already, after releases, some people misinterpreted files (like a note about Jack Ruby knew Oswald, fueling new speculation).
- **Privacy/Defamation Risks:** Some withheld content might include names of people who were investigated but never charged. Making that public could unfairly taint those individuals or their families. For example, if an FBI file suspects someone of being MLK’s “real killer” or an accomplice but it was unproven, releasing that can lead to harassment or conspiracy against that person (or their children, if they’re deceased). The EO doesn’t appear to consider those nuances, potentially causing harm to private citizens’ reputations.
- **Potential National Security Nugget Exposure:** Perhaps 99% of the info is harmless now, but there might be a few items that reveal intel sources/methods the US or allies still use (like the name of a then-agent who later did sensitive work in other contexts). Blanket release could accidentally burn such an asset or damage a foreign liaison relationship (some JFK records involve CIA-Mafia plots, involvement of Cuban exiles, etc., which could still be sensitive in Cuba or Italy or elsewhere). Normally careful review would weigh those, but the EO’s rush might have caused oversight.
- **Setting a Precedent for Politicized Declassification:** Observers worry this might set an expectation or precedent that classification can be overridden for political spectacle. This could reduce allies’ confidence in sharing intelligence (fear that the US might one day just declassify it all). It might also pressure current/future administrations to outdo him or meet public demand for other info dumps not vetted by security professionals, potentially compromising sources. Essentially, he blurred the line between **legitimate transparency** and **political theater in declassification**, which could weaken classification integrity processes.

**Fit into Trump’s Broader Agenda:**  
This EO aligns with Trump’s **anti-“deep state” and anti-establishment** themes. It’s an extension of “drain the swamp” into the historical realm – implying past governments hid big truths, and he’s the disruptor exposing them. It fits with his portrayal of institutions as corrupt or at least untrustworthy.

It also shows his flair for **grand symbolic gestures** that rile up his base. Similar to how he might have pardoned controversial figures or ordered the relocation of the U.S. embassy to Jerusalem – decisions that have big symbolic weight and excite his core supporters, regardless of practical impact. Declassifying these assassination records is symbolic of challenging the official narratives of the establishment – something his followers love.

Interestingly, it’s one of the few things he did that some on the left and civil libertarians also cheered (transparency advocates have long pushed for these files). So it also allowed him to momentarily bridge partisan divide and get some praise from unexpected quarters (e.g., historians, open-government groups) – which feeds his ego and narrative that he does things others wouldn’t.

And as a matter of legacy, it’s something he can claim uniquely – “no other president had the guts to do this.” That fits his self-image as a bold truth-teller. It also indirectly is a poke at Biden: Biden in late 2022 postponed some JFK file releases to 2025-26 citing security concerns; Trump’s EO implies Biden was needlessly secretive or protecting institutions, whereas Trump will just rip it open.

**Harm Caused:**  
- **Risk to Individuals Named:** Some of the newly released records may name people who were informants or suspects. For example, the JFK files could include the identity of a still-living CIA asset in Cuba or Mexico in the 60s, or MLK files might name an FBI informant within civil rights circles whose family didn’t know. Exposing these names can **endanger those individuals or their families** (e.g., reprisals from someone who thinks informants betrayed MLK, or simply subjecting them to unwanted public scrutiny). Even if many are old or deceased, their families could face harassment. In MLK’s case, one person long suspected by conspiracists is a photographer at the assassination scene; if a file mentions suspicion of him by the FBI (even if cleared), him or his descendants could be targeted by extremist conspiracy buffs.
- **Public Misinterpretation and New Conspiracies:** The massive release could **spawn new conspiracy theories or reinforce old ones** rather than resolve them. As seen when some JFK files came out in 2017-2018, small tidbits (like a KGB memo speculating LBJ might have been behind JFK’s murder) were taken out of context and went viral among conspiracy groups. With everything out, unscrupulous actors can selectively use the data to “prove” their wild theories. Rather than bringing closure, it may have created an endless data mine for disinformation. In the social media age, raw data dumps without explanation can lead to wide proliferation of false interpretations. This harms public understanding of history and can further corrode trust in government if people misread “smoking guns” that aren’t really that.
- **Potential Intelligence Fallout:** If any released file revealed a methodology still in use (like how CIA recruits foreign assets or an NSA listening capability from back then that has modern analog), adversaries might glean useful info. Even though one expects little of that remains in these old files, it’s possible some intel technique (like secret writing, surveillance methods) might still be relevant. That could allow adversaries to adapt and counter those techniques, a small but real harm to U.S. intelligence operations. Also, if some files involve foreign intelligence agencies (say, UK’s MI5 helped monitor MLK on a trip, hypothetically) and those details come out, it might strain relationships or embarrass allied governments.
- **Strain on Resources and Focus:** Government archivists, historians, and intelligence staff had to scramble to process and release these records (some needed scanning, review, etc.). This resource diversion in early 2025 might have taken focus from other pressing tasks (like current national security issues). While this is a minor harm and part of their job, it’s worth noting the urgency in the EO forced agencies to do this quickly, possibly at the expense of other duties or thorough review. If any mistakes were made (like failing to redact a Social Security number of a witness, etc.), that’s collateral damage from the rushed process.
- **Emotional Impact on Families & Public:** For the victims’ families, seeing all records can be a double-edged sword. It could bring closure, but it could also reopen wounds or introduce new upsetting information. For instance, RFK’s children are split on Sirhan Sirhan’s guilt; new records might fuel that family divide if they hint at a second gunman theory. MLK’s children have long believed there was more to his murder; if files don’t conclusively prove that, it might renew their pain and frustration. For the public, revisiting these traumatic events with fresh, possibly lurid details (like FBI surveillance of MLK’s personal life, which might be more fully exposed) could be disillusioning or disturbing, causing a wave of anger over past injustices that, while important to acknowledge, could be exploited to foment distrust or racial tension (e.g., detailed evidence of FBI’s campaign to discredit MLK might be used by some to justify anti-government sentiment in harmful ways).
- **Erosion of Classification Respect:** Normally, classification is to protect national security; if people see that a bunch of decades-old secrets could just be dumped for seemingly political reasons, it could **reduce confidence in the classification system’s seriousness**. Government employees might question, “Why should I trust keeping this secret if it might be published soon due to political pressure?” This cynicism can lead to more leaks or less careful handling of information. Also, future decisions to keep something classified might face more skepticism from the public (“They lied about JFK, so maybe they’re lying now”). While healthy skepticism is fine, an absolute cynicism is harmful if it means no one trusts official accounts of anything—leading to a post-truth environment where conspiracy theories flourish (something we already struggle with).

**How a Future Administration Could Undo or Repair Damage:**
