- **Can’t Re-Classify, but Can Contextualize:** Once records are out, they cannot realistically be reclassified (they’re in public domain). So the focus is on **contextualizing and accurately communicating** the contents. The administration should commission a panel of respected historians and experts to review the released assassination records and issue public reports that *explain what’s new, what it means in context, and what conclusions can or cannot be drawn*. For instance, if speculation memos are in JFK files, historians can clarify, *“This was one agent’s guess, but later investigations found no evidence for it.”* By providing authoritative context (perhaps in partnership with the National Archives’ JFK Records Collection staff or via a report to Congress), the government can help prevent misinformation from filling the interpretive void. This panel’s findings could be widely publicized to counteract conspiratorial takes.
- **Protect Individuals Caught in the Release:** If any still-living person’s safety or reputation is jeopardized by the releases, the administration could quietly offer assistance or at least publicly clarify their status. For example, if an informant is alive and worried about retaliation, ensure they are safe or even relocated if needed (though likely minimal risk given time passed). If a private individual’s name was dragged through the mud in newly released docs, an official statement might note that person was investigated and cleared, to tamp down vigilante harassment. This is case-by-case damage control, working perhaps with families (e.g., if a deceased individual is implicated in a theory by these records, reassure their family by sharing full context).
- **Acknowledge Past Abuses and Emphasize Reforms:** Some records might highlight serious wrongdoing (e.g., FBI’s harassment of MLK, CIA’s plots) – the new administration should **openly acknowledge these historical abuses**, condemn them, and underscore the reforms that have since been implemented (like the Church Committee reforms in the ’70s) to prevent recurrence. For example, the AG could give a speech recognizing FBI’s wrongful surveillance of civil rights leaders and noting how oversight and laws now forbid such conduct. By doing so, the administration channels the information release into a productive discourse of reckoning and reassurance, rather than letting conspiracists frame it as “see, the FBI/CIA are evil and still are.” It tells the public: *“Yes, these things happened. They were wrong. We learned and changed.”* Such acknowledgment can somewhat restore faith that while the past had dark chapters, the government can correct itself.
- **Refocus Public on Present & Future:** While addressing the historical matters, the administration should gently guide public attention back to present issues once the initial wave of interest passes. After providing context and closure efforts, pivot to *“What can we learn for our democracy today?”* and then to current agenda items. For instance, use the conversation to emphasize transparency commitments moving forward (maybe commit to quicker declassification of historically important records generally, beyond these assassinations). And highlight how focusing on facts and evidence is crucial – a subtle pushback against conspiratorial thinking. Then, concurrently drive media attention to pressing matters like legislation, current policy successes, etc. The idea is not to suppress interest in the assassination records, but to ensure it doesn’t consume disproportionate oxygen for too long or lead to destructive cynicism.
- **Repair with Intelligence Community:** Internally, if the CIA, FBI, or others felt this mass release was mishandled, the new administration should smooth over friction. Possibly review if anything truly sensitive slipped out and address any fallout with foreign partners (e.g., if a UK intel operation from the ’60s got revealed, apologize and emphasize this was a one-time political decision, not standard practice). Reaffirm to the intelligence workforce that the administration values classification for genuine secrets, and that this declassification was an unusual circumstance unlikely to set a pattern for current ops. They might also want to implement a more orderly declassification review process for future historical cases to avoid such blunt interventions. For example, task the National Declassification Center to proactively review historically significant records (like maybe Iran-Contra files, etc.) for release on a schedule, to avoid last-minute politicized dumps. That could be a constructive outlet of this – building a system that regularly releases old secrets responsibly so there’s less temptation for a president to do it dramatically.
- **Legislate Archives Reforms:** If any shortcomings in the JFK Records Act implementation came to light (which was why Biden extended deadlines), consider working with Congress to update that law or similar ones. Maybe create an independent board to oversee declassification of historically significant records (the JFK Act had an Assassination Records Review Board in the 90s; something like that could be permanent for other topics). Codifying a norm that after X years, records should default to public unless a high bar is met can be positive – it achieves transparency without needing a Trump-like EO. This channels the public’s appetite for transparency (which Trump leveraged) into a sustainable legal framework, preventing future politicization.
- **Guide Media and Public Education:** Encourage educational institutions or documentary filmmakers to make sense of the newly released files. The administration could even fund a grant via NEH (National Endowment for Humanities) for projects that analyze the new findings and educate the public. By promoting factual, scholarly interpretation (through books, documentaries, museum exhibits), it drowns out less credible voices. For example, an NEH grant to a history professors’ consortium to create a public website “Understanding the JFK Assassination Records” with plain-language summaries and scanned docs could preempt conspiratorial sites from dominating search results. 

In essence, while the genie can’t be put back in the bottle, a future administration can **ensure the genie doesn’t wreak havoc**. By owning the narrative of what these releases mean (acknowledging truths, debunking falsehoods), protecting anyone inadvertently exposed, and strengthening processes for future transparency, the harm can be minimized and the benefits of this openness (public knowledge, trust via truth-telling) can be maximized.

---

## EO 14177 — **President’s Council of Advisors on Science and Technology**  
**Alternative Title:** *“Stacking a Token Science Council to Rubber-Stamp Anti-Regulation Tech Policies”*

**What the EO Does:**  
- **Establishes PCAST:** Formally re-establishes the **President’s Council of Advisors on Science and Technology (PCAST)** under Trump’s second term. PCAST is a high-level advisory body of outside experts (academia, industry, etc.) to counsel the President on science and tech issues. Many presidents have had one; Trump’s first term had an inactive PCAST until late. Here he revives it on Jan 23, 2025, signaling its importance in his agenda.
- **Sets Composition:** PCAST will have up to **24 members from outside the federal government** plus several administration officials. Notably, it designates that the **Assistant to the President for Science and Technology (APST)** and the **Special Advisor for AI & Crypto** will be members and serve as Co-Chairs. (Trump created that “Special Advisor for AI & Crypto” role via earlier order, highlighting his focus on those areas.) Other members are to be “distinguished individuals” with diverse expertise in science, tech, education, and innovation. The APST (likely also the OSTP Director) and the AI/Crypto Advisor co-chair, and they can appoint up to two vice-chairs from among the members. 
- **Defines Functions:** PCAST is tasked to **advise the President on matters involving science, technology, education, and innovation policy**, providing the President with science and tech information to inform policies related to the economy, workers, national security, etc.. It is to meet regularly and respond to requests from the President or Co-Chairs for analysis or advice. It should gather input from a broad range of stakeholders (research community, private sector, universities, labs, state/local governments, foundations, nonprofits). 
- **Statutory Advisory Roles:** The EO also uses PCAST to fulfill two specific advisory roles required by law:
  - It serves as the **President’s Innovation and Technology Advisory Committee** under the High-Performance Computing Act, which is an existing requirement for advice on HPC (previously done via PCAST).
  - It serves as the **National Nanotechnology Advisory Panel** under the 21st Century Nanotechnology R&D Act.
  By doing so, it covers legally mandated advisory functions with this one council.
- **Administration and Support:** The EO directs that **Department of Energy (DOE) shall fund and provide administrative support for PCAST** to the extent permitted by law. (Under Obama/Biden, OSTP funded PCAST; Trump shifting funding to DOE might reflect that OSTP’s budget was slim or that he wanted DOE, which he likely controlled, to manage logistics.)
- **Clearances:** It authorizes granting security clearances to PCAST members as needed so they can advise on classified matters (following EO 12968 procedures). 
- **General Provisions:** Standard stuff that nothing in the order shall interfere with legal authority of agencies or OMB, etc., which is routine.

**What it Claims to Do (Stated Justification):**  
The EO’s introduction paints an inspiring picture of American innovation, referencing Edison’s lightbulb, the Wright brothers, Armstrong’s moonwalk. It says *“Today, a new frontier of scientific discovery lies before us, defined by transformative technologies such as artificial intelligence, quantum computing, and advanced biotechnology.”*. Thus, the stated purpose is to harness that pioneering spirit by bringing together top advisors to guide the U.S. in these areas. 

It justifies establishing PCAST as ensuring the government gets **high-quality, diverse expert advice** to drive science and tech breakthroughs that will fuel economic growth, maintain American leadership, and address national needs. The EO implies that by convening this council, Trump’s administration will propel *“boundless creativity and bold ambition”* to solve problems and secure American preeminence in key technologies. 

Essentially, it claims to be about **keeping the U.S. at the forefront of innovation**. It doesn’t mention any ideological goals in the text – it sounds straightforward: get advice on science and tech to inform policy. The mention of AI and quantum suggests priorities, and including education means perhaps STEM education advice as well. The justification is that **the complexity and opportunity of modern science demands input from outside experts**, and PCAST provides that pipeline of ideas to the President. 

It’s presented as a continuation of a bipartisan tradition (though he doesn’t say that explicitly, it mirrors how previous presidents used PCAST). There is no hint in the official language of excluding certain topics; it *sounds* like it will cover all relevant issues broadly (though context suggests otherwise).

**Real Purpose or Effect (Critical Perspective):**  
In practice, while PCAST can be valuable, Trump’s approach likely aimed to **populate it with loyalists or industry allies to endorse his tech agenda** rather than to challenge or broaden his views:
- **Stacking Membership with Ideologues and Industry Figures:** Trump’s inclusion of the “Special Advisor for AI & Crypto” as co-chair is telling. That role was created to push his agenda on AI (unconstrained by “ideological bias”) and crypto (anti-CBDC) per EOs 14179 and 14178. By co-chairing PCAST, that ensures PCAST’s agenda and reports will align with those priorities. He might appoint members who share his skepticism of climate science or who favor minimal regulation of tech. For instance, we might see a CEO of a big tech or energy company who opposes emissions cuts on it, or an AI entrepreneur who thinks AI should not be regulated for bias. Under Trump’s first term, his few science appointments included people like William Happer (a climate change skeptic) in a climate panel (which didn’t fully materialize). With PCAST, he could include such contrarians to give weight to anti-regulatory stances. 
- **Narrow Issue Focus (AI, Quantum, Defense Tech) vs. Ignoring Others:** PCAST under Trump likely focused on areas he emphasized: AI, crypto, quantum, military tech, perhaps biomedical innovation (Operation Warp Speed was a success in his term, he might want credit there). It might pay scant attention to topics like climate change, environmental science, or social sciences in tech (e.g., ethics of AI, social impact of automation on jobs, etc.). If climate or pandemic preparedness were raised, the Co-Chairs might sideline them in favor of talk about “frontier technologies” or direct them to fit his narrative (like focusing on adaptation technology vs. emissions reduction). So the advice coming out could be **skewed to align with Trump’s preferences**, leaving blind spots on issues he downplays (climate, environmental health, inequality in STEM).
- **Use PCAST to Endorse Deregulatory Moves:** Trump can point to PCAST’s “expert” recommendations as backing his policies. For instance, PCAST might issue a report saying *“AI innovation should not be hampered by overregulation; the U.S. must avoid the EU’s precautionary approach”*. This provides him cover to say his AI EO (which removed regulations) is supported by experts. Similarly, PCAST could recommend speeding up nuclear energy or geoengineering research instead of climate mitigation, aligning with his fossil-fuel-friendly stance. If PCAST doesn’t address an issue, he can claim no experts raised it, so it’s not a priority. In short, it can act as a **rubber stamp or at least a friendly echo chamber** rather than a critical advisory group that might tell him inconvenient truths.
- **Sidelining the OSTP and Other Science Voices:** Traditionally, OSTP (Office of Science and Tech Policy) staff and other advisory committees feed into PCAST. If Trump’s OSTP is weak (as it was in his first term – he operated without a confirmed science advisor for a long time), PCAST might be the only visible science advice channel, but one he controls. Meanwhile, he eliminated or ignored many other advisory boards (like on climate, environment, etc.). So PCAST could be a fig leaf – *“See, I have science advisors,”* while he’s actually disbanded or muzzled many others. It allows him to claim he’s guided by science even if in reality he’s cherry-picked the science he listens to. 
- **DOE Control & Focus on Energy Tech:** Having DOE fund PCAST might mean meetings and support staff come through DOE’s lens (DOE might steer agenda towards nuclear energy, fracking tech, carbon capture, etc., which Trump favored, and away from renewable energy transformation or climate science). Also, if OSTP (which is usually the conduit for PCAST’s operation) is circumvented, there’s less involvement of generalist science policy staff who might bring up things like public health or environment. DOE’s involvement could tilt PCAST’s priorities to match Trump’s energy agenda (fossil fuel development, maybe some talk of futuristic things like hydrogen, but not societal issues).
- **Legitimizing Crypto and AI Stances:** Including the Special Advisor for AI & Crypto ensures PCAST will put heavy emphasis on those domains, likely producing recommendations aligned with **EO 14178 and 14179** – e.g., praising blockchain adoption, urging government use of crypto (maybe even recommending that Fed not pursue CBDC, to give pseudo-expert backing to Trump’s ban), or pushing for AI in government without “bias filters.” This cloaks politically driven tech policies in the garb of expert consensus.

Overall, instead of PCAST being a balanced brain trust, under Trump it was likely more of a curated club to **validate his tech and industry priorities and signal the U.S. is pro-innovation** (as he defines it). It might ignore or gloss over externalities (like ignoring AI’s threat to privacy or jobs, ignoring crypto’s role in fraud, ignoring climate change’s tech solutions in favor of fossil tech).

**Fit into Trump’s Broader Agenda:**  
This EO fits his effort to project that he’s **making America cutting-edge again** (just as he claimed on economy, military, etc.). It complements his tech-related EOs (AI, crypto) by setting up the advisory structure to continue those themes. 

It also resonates with his tendency to replace what he sees as “liberal-dominated” expert bodies with ones he influences. For example, Biden’s PCAST was diverse (it had co-chairs including Frances Arnold, a Nobel chemist, and Maria Zuber, a geophysicist, and tackled climate and pandemic issues head-on). Trump’s PCAST by design elevates an AI/Crypto advisor presumably aligned with right-leaning tech views as co-chair, likely tipping balance away from climate or social sciences. It’s part of his agenda to **reshape scientific discourse in government to exclude or minimize fields he politically opposes (climate, DEI in STEM, etc.)** and amplify those he likes (defense tech, industry-friendly innovation).

Additionally, having a PCAST allows him to claim he’s pro-science, despite the fact that, for instance, he denied climate science and fought with medical experts. It’s a form of **scientific tokenism**: the existence of a prestigious-sounding council to deflect criticisms that his administration is anti-science. It parallels how he handled COVID at times – sidelining actual CDC experts but bringing in a few contrarian doctors to say he’s listening to science (like Dr. Atlas). PCAST could serve a similar role across broader science policy: sidelining mainstream consensus but platforming a specific subset of experts who echo his views.

**Harm Caused:**  
- **Skewed Policy Advice:** If PCAST under Trump does not seriously grapple with key scientific challenges (like climate change, environmental pollution, public health threats) because it’s busy championing deregulation in AI or space exploration, **policy could be misguided or insufficient** in critical areas. For example, lack of PCAST emphasis on climate change might contribute to weak climate actions, exacerbating harm from extreme weather, etc. Or if PCAST doesn’t address pandemic preparedness (Biden’s PCAST did recommendations on that), government might neglect stockpiling or early warning improvements, making us more vulnerable to the next pandemic.
- **Missed Opportunities and Innovation Bias:** An industry-skewed PCAST might favor big corporate interests (like recommending subsidies for certain emerging tech companies) rather than public-interest research (like funding for climate resilience or antibiotic resistance, which might not have immediate corporate champions). This could distort federal R&D priorities, **neglecting important research areas that lack political cachet**. For instance, if no one on PCAST is highlighting the need for social science research on AI’s impact on jobs, the government might underinvest in retraining programs. Or ignoring renewable energy research in favor of, say, nuclear or “clean coal” could slow the energy transition. The harm is the **opportunity cost** of not fully addressing some issues or exploring beneficial technologies because they weren’t favored by Trump’s appointees.
- **Erosion of Independent Science Advice Norms:** If PCAST is seen as politicized, top scientists might decline to serve or engage, reducing the quality and legitimacy of advice. The harm is **diminished trust in White House science advisory processes**. After seeing how Trump populated PCAST, some experts might worry any future PCAST could also be stacked, which may discourage candid advice or cross-party participation. It also signals to career scientists in agencies that science advice can be cherry-picked, potentially hurting morale or driving more scientists out of government (brain drain). 
- **Poor Incorporation of Equity and Ethics in Tech:** A PCAST that avoids social implications means technological policies might lack ethical guardrails. For example, if PCAST pushes rapid deployment of facial recognition for policing without discussing bias or privacy, it could influence policy to adopt such tech widely, **harming civil liberties and exacerbating discrimination**. Or pushing gene editing technology without robust ethical review could lead to public backlash or real ethical breaches. Without voices raising these concerns in PCAST, those aspects might be overlooked in policy development.
- **Climate and Environment Ignored:** If PCAST didn’t prioritize climate solutions (given Trump’s stance, likely minimal focus), the harm is that the administration misses out on high-level advice on climate innovation (like advanced battery storage, carbon removal – things that could align with even conservative interests if framed economically). Not elevating these topics can slow the government’s response to climate change, indirectly causing more environmental harm, economic damage from disasters, etc., than if robust strategies were considered. It also denies the President a chance to hear from leading climate scientists directly – a lost chance to perhaps influence his thinking.
- **Biased Recommendations Becoming Policy:** PCAST’s recommendations often feed into budget decisions or executive actions. If a biased PCAST recommends, say, cutting “red tape” for pharma innovation, it might lead to policies that **reduce safety oversight on drug approvals**, risking public health. Or if it downplays AI regulation, that could become part of official policy, leading to the harms discussed under EO 14179 (bias, unsafe AI). The council’s one-sided advice could thus be codified into policy, amplifying harm beyond the advisory stage.
- **Research Community Alienation:** The broader U.S. scientific community might feel excluded or misrepresented by a PCAST that doesn’t reflect their consensus concerns. For instance, if no prominent climate scientist or public health expert is on PCAST, those communities see that as a snub – the administration doesn’t care about their input. That could discourage collaboration with the government (scientists might decline advisory roles in agencies, etc.) and reduce the government’s access to independent expertise. It also could push young scientists away from federal careers if they perceive science policy is driven by politics not evidence. Over time, that diminishes the government's scientific capacity and the country's ability to make evidence-based decisions.

**How a Future Administration Could Undo or Repair Damage:**  
- **Reconstitute PCAST with Balance and Independence:** The new President should **dissolve Trump’s PCAST** and re-establish it with a new charter. Appoint **highly respected experts across a wide range of fields**, ensuring diversity in expertise (climate, biomedical, social sciences, as well as AI, etc.) and demographics. For example, include top scientists who might have been sidelined (like leading climate scientists, public health experts, etc.) alongside industry innovators and academics. Make sure co-chairs are apolitical eminent scientists (as Biden did, e.g., an accomplished academic and industry leader). This sends a clear message that PCAST is back to being about science, not politics. 
- **Broaden PCAST’s Agenda:** Direct the new PCAST to address **a broad spectrum of scientific challenges**, not just narrow tech boosterism. Specifically request reports on climate change mitigation technologies, pandemic preparedness, STEM education equity, environmental health, etc., in addition to continuing focus on AI and quantum. This will produce more well-rounded advice. Ensure working groups or subcommittees exist for critical areas Trump’s PCAST ignored (e.g., one on climate & environment, one on health & society impacts of tech). The President can attend a meeting or publicly charge PCAST with tackling these issues, to show prioritization.
- **Implement PCAST Recommendations that Were Neglected:** Review any useful recommendations that might have been overlooked under Trump due to his biases. For example, if Trump’s PCAST did produce a report on AI or quantum that had merit but was twisted by politics, salvage the good bits (like need for more R&D funding in certain areas) and incorporate them into a more balanced strategy. Simultaneously, if Trump’s PCAST ignored issues (say it never addressed climate innovation), commission PCAST 2.0 to produce a strategy on that within the first year. This both corrects omissions and leverages any groundwork laid.
- **Re-engage OSTP and Other Advisory Bodies:** The new admin should fully empower the **White House Office of Science and Technology Policy (OSTP)**, which will coordinate PCAST. OSTP was likely downplayed by Trump; rebuilding it with strong leadership (a respected Science Advisor) and staff means PCAST’s advice gets properly integrated into policy memos and decisions. Also, **restore other advisory committees** disbanded or gutted under Trump (like scientific advisory boards at EPA, FDA, etc.). Diversity of advice prevents over-reliance on one council. A robust OSTP can filter PCAST’s advice with input from agency scientists to make more balanced recommendations to the President.
- **Re-embed Ethics and Social Considerations:** To repair the lack of ethics and equity focus, incorporate advisors or sub-groups specifically for **AI ethics, biotech ethics, and impacts on labor and society**. E.g., put a leading AI ethicist on PCAST. Or create a standing **PCAST subcommittee on Societal and Ethical Implications** that reviews all PCAST suggestions. That way, any recommendation for rapid tech deployment is accompanied by mitigation advice for any downsides. This reintegrates what Trump’s approach ignored.
- **Transparent and Inclusive Operations:** Have PCAST hold **some public meetings or listening sessions** with stakeholder communities (e.g., a town hall with climate scientists, a forum with workers about automation). This openness repairs the secretive or insular image. Additionally, publish PCAST reports promptly and include minority opinions if any (ensuring if there was dissent on a recommendation, it’s noted, to show it wasn’t monolithic if it wasn’t). Encouraging a culture where PCAST members feel free to voice differing views, even if they contradict administration policy, strengthens the integrity of advice and signals scientists won’t be punished for honesty (countering any chill from Trump’s era).
- **Ensure PCAST Advice Leads to Action on Neglected Issues:** Follow through on PCAST’s recommendations, especially on areas Trump neglected. For example, if new PCAST says “invest in climate resilience R&D $X billion” – incorporate that into the budget proposal. Show that advice in domains Trump sidelined is now actually guiding policy. Conversely, if Trump’s PCAST urged something potentially harmful (like completely hands-off AI regulation), the new admin can explicitly choose a different path, explaining it considered that view but took a more balanced approach following broader input. Clarifying decisions in this way educates the public on how advice was weighed, making them less likely to think science advice is a mere rubber stamp.
- **Legislate Diversity of Advisory Bodies:** Over the long term, consider working with Congress to formalize some aspects of scientific advice to insulate it from politicization. For instance, recharter PCAST via legislation that requires balanced representation of disciplines and bipartisan membership (maybe similar to National Science Board selection process). Or require periodic science advice reports to Congress (not just President) so that transparency is maintained. While presidents will always pick people they’re comfortable with to some degree, formal guidelines (like at least 1/3 of PCAST members must be from academia with no financial conflicts) could ensure baseline integrity. If that’s too rigid to pass, at least strengthening the notion that PCAST is nonpartisan (perhaps inviting one or two members from the opposite political party’s scientific community to join) could help normatively. Biden did somewhat by including a tech CEO known as a Republican (Kelvin Droegemeier) on his PCAST – gestures like that repair the sense that science shouldn’t be partisan.

By resetting PCAST to a **truly independent, diverse, and forward-thinking body**, the future administration can both **reverse any biased outcomes of Trump’s PCAST and reap the full benefits of expert science advice**. This ensures that U.S. science and technology policy once again addresses broad public needs (climate, health, equity in innovation) and is guided by evidence from all corners, not just the corners that echo the President’s preferences. Over time, this helps **rebuild trust** – among scientists that their input matters and isn’t misused, and among the public that science policy is aimed at the common good, not special interests or ideologies.

---

## EO 14178 — **Strengthening American Leadership in Digital Financial Technology**  
**Alternative Title:** *“Unleashing Unregulated Crypto and Banning the Digital Dollar”*

**What the EO Does:**  
- **Promotes Cryptocurrency & Blockchain Use:** Declares it U.S. policy to **support the responsible growth and lawful use of digital assets, blockchain technology, and related innovations across all sectors of the economy**. It explicitly vows to **protect individuals’ and businesses’ ability to access and use open public blockchain networks for lawful purposes “without persecution,”** including developing software, mining/validating transactions, transacting peer-to-peer, and holding one’s own digital assets (self-custody). This is basically a green light for the crypto industry: the government should not impede common cryptocurrency activities as long as they’re not explicitly illegal. The phrase “without persecution” suggests a view that previous regulatory actions were akin to unjust harassment of crypto users. 
- **Supports Dollar-Backed Stablecoins:** Commits to **promoting and protecting the development of lawful, legitimate dollar-backed stablecoins worldwide**. This means the administration favors private stablecoins (cryptocurrencies pegged to the U.S. dollar like USDC or Tether) as a means to extend the dollar’s influence digitally, rather than a government-issued digital dollar. It suggests actions to encourage their growth and integration into the financial system (likely by clarifying rules or encouraging adoption) so they bolster the dollar’s status.
- **Ensures Bank Access for Crypto:** Aims to **prevent financial “deplatforming” of lawful crypto actors by promoting fair access to banking**. In practice, this signals to bank regulators (Fed, OCC, FDIC) that banks should not be discouraging or cutting off services to cryptocurrency businesses or individuals purely due to the nature of their transactions. It likely addresses complaints that regulators in 2023 discouraged banks from doing business with crypto firms (the so-called “Operation Choke Point 2.0” theory). So, regulators should allow **crypto companies to have bank accounts and loans** if they’re following the law, preventing a shadow banking problem.
- **Regulatory Clarity & Tech-Neutral Rules:** Calls for **technology-neutral, clear regulations and well-defined jurisdictional boundaries among agencies** for digital assets. This means the administration wants to eliminate uncertainty about which regulator (SEC, CFTC, etc.) oversees what and to avoid regulations that single out crypto versus similar traditional products, thereby encouraging innovation by providing certainty. It implies pushing regulators to coordinate and not issue conflicting guidance.
- **Takes Measures Against a Central Bank Digital Currency (CBDC):** Orders **prohibiting the establishment or use of a U.S. CBDC** on grounds it would threaten financial stability, individual privacy, and U.S. sovereignty. This is a very strong stance: it forbids the Federal Reserve or any part of government from pursuing a digital version of the dollar. It effectively kills any ongoing exploration by the Fed of a digital dollar (which Biden’s EO 14067 had encouraged to study). It also might aim to block any introduction of foreign CBDCs in the U.S. (though legally the Fed can block foreign currencies as legal tender anyway). The rationale given is that a government digital currency could enable surveillance and crowd out private sector innovation, a view held by some libertarians and conservatives, and perhaps that it could upset the current banking system (since people might bank directly with Fed accounts).
- **Revokes Biden’s Crypto Initiatives:** **Revokes EO 14067 of March 9, 2022 (“Ensuring Responsible Development of Digital Assets”)** and **orders the Treasury to immediately revoke its “Framework for International Engagement on Digital Assets” (issued July 2022)**. This effectively **nullifies Biden’s entire crypto strategy**, which was a cautious, multi-agency approach to integrating crypto with safeguards and exploring a CBDC. It also **withdraws the U.S. from active international regulatory cooperation** the Treasury had started with allies. All policies, directives, and guidance from those initiatives are rescinded or to be rescinded to the extent inconsistent with Trump’s new pro-crypto stance. Essentially, wipe the slate of the prior administration’s crypto policy and replace it with this more laissez-faire approach.
- **Establishes a Working Group on Digital Asset Markets:** Sets up a **President’s Working Group on Digital Asset Markets** within the National Economic Council, chaired by the Special Advisor for AI & Crypto. This working group includes relevant agency heads (likely Treasury, SEC, CFTC, Fed, etc.) and is to coordinate efforts on implementing this EO’s policies. It’s reminiscent of an existing President’s Working Group that produced a stablecoin report in 2021, but here the chair is a White House official likely pushing Trump's line. This ensures the bureaucracy follows through on aligning regulations with this pro-crypto framework, under direct White House supervision.
- **General Provisions:** As usual, it says nothing in the EO shall limit existing authority or OMB’s budget role, and it should be implemented consistent with law and budget availability. It also doesn’t create any enforceable rights (so nobody can sue claiming, e.g., a bank “persecuted” them in violation of this EO, it’s just policy guidance).

**What it Claims to Do (Stated Justification):**  
The EO frames itself as positioning America to **lead in the digital asset revolution while protecting economic liberty**. It notes that the digital asset industry is crucial for innovation and international leadership, so the policy is to support its growth responsibly. Key justifications:
- **Innovation & Competitiveness:** It suggests that embracing blockchain and crypto will spur innovation, jobs, and keep the U.S. at the forefront of finance and tech. There’s an underlying argument that heavy regulation drives innovation offshore, so by providing freedom and clarity, the U.S. will attract talent and companies (this counters the narrative that earlier crackdowns caused firms to leave the U.S.).
- **Economic Freedom & Censorship Resistance:** Protecting usage of open blockchain networks is cast as protecting freedom – the ability to transact without “unlawful censorship” or undue restrictions. This implies a view that decentralized networks empower individuals and should be free from heavy-handed control. The EO asserts the government will not unjustly interfere in lawful crypto activities, almost a libertarian ethos.
- **Strengthening the Dollar via Stablecoins:** By promoting dollar-backed stablecoins, it claims to enhance the dollar’s global role in a new digital era. The idea is if people worldwide use USD-pegged cryptocurrencies, the dollar remains dominant (as opposed to, say, them using a Chinese digital yuan or Bitcoin). So stablecoins are framed as a competitive tool to ensure U.S. monetary supremacy continues.
- **Regulatory Clarity & Innovation:** It acknowledges the need for clear rules and not treating crypto with one-size-fits-all heavy regulation. This implies the justification that **uncertainty was stifling innovation**, and by clarifying and streamlining, legitimate businesses can innovate without fear. It's a common industry ask – clarity on which tokens are securities vs. commodities, etc., so they know how to comply.
- **CBDC Threats to Freedom & Stability:** The EO explicitly states CBDCs pose threats to financial stability, privacy, and sovereignty. This is justifying banning a Fed digital dollar by raising the specter of government surveillance (privacy concern), potential disruption of the banking system (if people use CBDC instead of bank deposits, it could disintermediate banks – a stability issue), and hinting that a CBDC could be a control mechanism (so banning it preserves “sovereignty” – likely meaning keep money in private hands vs. central control). So Trump is taking a populist stand: *“We won’t allow a Big Brother digital dollar.”*

Additionally, by revoking Biden’s EO and international framework, it implies those were overly restrictive or misguided, and this new direction is the correct course for economic growth and liberty. There is an undertone that **previous regulation equaled “persecution”** of the crypto sector, which Trump claims to end.

**Real Purpose or Effect (Critical Perspective):**  
Critically, this EO largely serves to **deregulate the crypto industry and cement an ideological stance against central bank digital currencies**. The real intent seems to be:
- **Halting Regulatory Crackdown:** After numerous crypto scandals in 2022-2023 (FTX collapse, etc.), regulators were ramping up enforcement. Trump’s EO slams the brakes on that. It likely *indirectly* signals the SEC to drop or settle major cases against crypto exchanges and issuers, or that legislation/regulation should carve out new rules friendlier to crypto. It prevents new rules (like if the Fed/OCC were considering limits on banks’ crypto exposures) from taking effect. Essentially, it’s a **policy pivot from trying to tame crypto risks to embracing crypto innovation**, heavily favoring industry interests. That means a less restrained environment: new coins can launch without registering as securities, banks can dive into crypto offerings, etc., which might lead to a short-term boom but at cost of potential fraud/unchecked speculation.
- **Politicizing Currency (Anti-CBDC):** The strong anti-CBDC stance plays to conservative fears of surveillance and government control. The effect is the Fed, which under Powell was cautiously researching CBDC, must shelve that entirely. This is ideological – many central banks are exploring CBDCs not to control citizens, but to modernize payment systems. But Trump’s position resonates with his base’s distrust of central authority and also with crypto believers who see CBDCs as competition. Real effect: the U.S. essentially opts out of a digital dollar, possibly ceding leadership in this area to other nations (like China with its digital yuan). However, politically it’s a symbolic win for anti-government-control narrative.
- **Benefiting Crypto Businesses & Investors:** The EO is practically a wish list for crypto companies: don’t cut us off from banks, don’t regulate stablecoins too harshly, don’t sue our token offerings as securities, and don’t make a FedCoin that competes with us. Real winners are stablecoin issuers like Circle (USDC) or Tether, Bitcoin miners, crypto exchanges, venture capitalists in crypto – they get a permissive regime. Traditional banks might be cautious, but some will jump in now that the roadblocks are lifted (e.g., offering crypto services or holding crypto on balance sheet). This could raise valuations and investments in the sector. The risk is some marginal or bad actors also flourish without oversight.
- **Pleasing Ideological Allies and Donors:** The EO aligns with certain GOP lawmakers and donors who are pro-crypto or anti-Fed. It likely satisfies the likes of Senator Cynthia Lummis or others pushing crypto-friendly legislation. There is also a suspicion that **Trump and/or his allies may have interests in crypto** (for example, some see parallels between anti-CBDC stance and lobbying by the crypto industry). So it might also be a case of catering to donor interests or personal beliefs (like Peter Thiel, a Trump ally, was a big crypto investor). 
- **Isolating the U.S. from Global Standards:** By revoking the international framework, Trump is saying the U.S. will not necessarily follow stringent global norms on crypto regulation. This might attract crypto businesses to the U.S. (if Europe or others regulate heavily, they come here), but it could also make the U.S. a bit of a rogue jurisdiction. It fits Trump’s unilateral approach globally. However, it could lead to friction: allies might restrict interactions with U.S.-based crypto players if they see the U.S. as too lax on illicit finance, etc. But Trump likely doesn’t mind that friction if he thinks the economic boost is worth it or it satisfies sovereignty concerns.
- **The Working Group Chair by a Politico:** The Special Advisor for AI & Crypto chairing the working group ensures **White House political control over how regulators implement this**. It reduces the independence of agencies like the SEC or Fed. Now, any regulatory action on crypto might have to be coordinated through this presumably pro-crypto working group. Real effect: agencies could be pressured to water down rules or hold off enforcement. It centralizes policy in an advisor who is ideologically aligned with Trump rather than in expert regulators. So, some nuanced regulatory approaches might be overridden by the working group in favor of blunt, industry-friendly moves.

**Fit into Trump’s Broader Agenda:**  
This EO is part of his larger push to **undo what he sees as overregulation and to spark economic growth via freeing markets**. It parallels his first term’s massive deregulation in environment, labor, etc., but now applied to the new realm of fintech and digital currency. It’s also a culture war element: he positions himself as against the “establishment” in finance (the Federal Reserve’s cautious approach) and with the populist concept of financial freedom (crypto is by some seen as populist finance). It’s a high-tech equivalent of his rhetoric for blue-collar jobs – here he’s “bringing innovation jobs back” by not scaring away crypto/AI.

Also, it aligns with his anti-globalist stance: rejecting global CBDC trends, pulling back from international coordination fits his America First mentality. It shows he’ll set U.S. policy to his liking even if the IMF, BIS, or others prefer more regulation or CBDC exploration.

This EO plus the AI EO show Trump’s approach to tech is **maximal exploitation for dominance** – encourage AI and crypto to flourish unconstrained, to beat China and Europe in these fields, rejecting the caution those competitors might show. It’s about winning a techno-economic race by being the wild west that fosters quick growth. It ties to his overall narrative of American greatness via unleashing business.

It may also be aimed at wooing younger or more libertarian voters – crypto is more popular among some younger demographics and tech-savvy groups, including some minorities and independents disillusioned with banks. By supporting crypto, he somewhat refreshes his brand beyond traditional GOP and could peel away some of that constituency.

**Harm Caused:**  
- **Consumer and Investor Protection Erosion:** By effectively dialing back enforcement and oversight, the EO exposes consumers to **higher risk of fraud, hacks, and losses in the crypto space**. During 2022-23, many people lost savings in crypto crashes and scams; stronger regulation was being formulated to protect them. Trump’s policy halts that, likely leading to a resurgence of risky products (like unregistered coin offerings, yield farming schemes, etc.) and **potential repeat of collapses**. New retail investors enticed by a now government-blessed crypto boom could be hurt if/when bubbles burst or if unscrupulous actors (now freer to operate) scam them. With the SEC presumably curbed, victims might have less recourse as well.
- **Financial Stability Risks:** Encouraging banks to engage more with crypto and promoting stablecoins without strict guardrails can reintroduce systemic risks. For example, if stablecoins grow huge with minimal regulation, a loss of confidence (like a run) could spill into traditional finance (stablecoins having to liquidate assets quickly, impacting markets). Or if banks get heavily into crypto lending/trading and the volatile market tanks, **banks could suffer significant losses**. In extreme, without careful limits, a major crypto market collapse could cause a broader financial crisis especially if it coincides with other stress. Biden’s regulators were concerned about this and thus cautious – Trump’s approach heightens this risk for the sake of innovation.
- **Illicit Finance & National Security:** Crypto is famously used for ransomware, darknet markets, sanction evasion (e.g., by North Korea hacking crypto to fund weapons). Biden’s framework was cracking down on these; Trump’s EO doesn’t mention them, likely deprioritizing the anti-illicit finance angle. If enforcement slackens (FinCEN not implementing new rules on crypto transactions, etc.), **criminals and rogue states will find it easier to use U.S.-linked crypto channels for dirty money**. We could see more ransomware attacks if perpetrators feel converting their crypto payouts is safe in the U.S. Also, anti-terror finance experts worry that unregulated crypto can be a haven for funding extremist groups. Trump prioritizes freedom over these concerns, which could **undermine sanctions and crime-fighting efforts**. Allies may take issue, possibly refusing to share intel if they think the U.S. is weak on stopping, say, Russian crypto laundering.
- **Losing a Tool (CBDC) for Financial Inclusion/Efficiency:** Banning CBDC forecloses potential benefits – e.g., a Fed digital dollar could have provided a low-cost banking option for the unbanked, faster settlement reducing transaction costs, or a backbone for innovative payment services. Without it, the U.S. relies on private sector solutions which may not prioritize inclusion or may charge fees. So **unbanked or underbanked Americans might remain so** (whereas a no-fee FedAccount could have helped). Also, international transactions might remain slower/costly compared to if many central banks had interoperable CBDCs. In crisis situations, a CBDC could allow direct stimulus (like rapid relief payments) – now off the table. Essentially, the U.S. voluntarily forgoes a modernization of currency that many experts think could yield efficiencies and safeguard the dollar long-term. If other major economies implement CBDCs that streamline trade and we don't, **the U.S. could fall behind in global payments tech** – ironically undermining the dollar’s long-term dominance, contrary to the EO’s intent.
- **Global Regulatory Friction & Illicit Arbitration:** If the U.S. goes very lax while Europe and Asia impose strict rules, **bad actors might flock to U.S. crypto markets** (“regulatory arbitrage”), which could make the U.S. a hub for shady activity, inviting international sanction or isolation. EU regulators might bar their banks from transacting with U.S.-based stablecoins if they see them as unregulated – fragmenting markets. We could see **financial diplomacy tensions**, with U.S. accused of undermining global anti-money laundering standards. It might also hamper future coordination on digital currency standards (e.g., how to tax crypto, how to handle cross-border uses), as U.S. is out of alignment. 
- **Energy/Environmental Impact:** The EO is silent on crypto mining’s huge energy usage. Biden’s admin was studying crypto’s climate impact; Trump’s likely halts that. If the U.S. encourages blockchain expansion “without persecution,” it could mean **more mining operations in the U.S., driving up electricity demand (often from fossil fuels) and increasing carbon emissions**. Bitcoin mining in particular is energy-intensive. States like Texas might see surges in mining (they already had some with China’s crackdown in 2021). That runs counter to climate goals and can strain local grids (causing higher power prices or instability). Environmental harms (and e-waste from mining rigs, etc.) could escalate, which the EO doesn’t address at all.
- **Potential Financial Scandals Under Government’s Watch:** If a major crypto fraud or collapse happens after these policies, it will appear that the administration facilitated it. For example, if Tether (a major stablecoin of somewhat opaque reserves) were to crash and hurt many, critics will say Trump’s lax stance enabled that. The harm is not just economic but political: a loss of faith in regulators and government for not protecting people. It could mirror the 2008 crisis blame on deregulation. So there's risk of **scandals or crises that hurt the credibility of U.S. financial oversight**. Additionally, such incidents could have ripple economic impacts – e.g., people losing retirement funds they unwisely put into crypto due to official endorsement, affecting consumer spending, etc.

**How a Future Administration Could Undo or Repair Damage:**  
- **Revoke EO 14178 and Reassert Balanced Crypto Oversight:** The new President should **revoke Trump’s crypto EO** and likely reinstate something akin to Biden’s EO 14067 or issue a new directive that puts the U.S. back on a path of responsible development of digital assets. It should explicitly:
  - Charge agencies to **resume enforcement of laws in the crypto space (securities laws, AML laws)** vigorously. Clarify that “without persecution” is not policy – rather, fair enforcement is.
  - Reiterate support for **exploring a U.S. CBDC** in consultation with the Fed. Perhaps not commit to launching one immediately (that might need Congress or Fed support), but remove the prohibition and actively study it again. Possibly require a pilot or detailed report on CBDC benefits/risks within a year.
  - Reestablish the **international cooperation** framework on digital assets. Treasury should re-engage with the G7, G20, and FSB to coordinate regulations (the US had been a leader in pushing crypto AML standards via FATF – rebuild that role).
  - Encourage innovation but “with appropriate safeguards” – basically revive Biden’s language of fostering innovation in crypto/fintech while mitigating risks to consumers, financial stability, national security, and climate.
- **Empower Regulatory Agencies Again:** Send clear signals (through both EO and personnel choices) that agencies like the SEC, CFTC, bank regulators, and CFPB are expected to **carry out their mandates regarding crypto**. For example:
  - The SEC should continue determining which digital tokens are securities and enforcing registration requirements – support them with resources to fight major fraud cases put on hold.
  - Direct the Treasury to finalize FinCEN rules on cryptocurrency wallet reporting or other AML measures that Trump might have stalled.
  - The Fed and OCC should be allowed to issue guidance on safe crypto integration for banks (like capital requirements for crypto exposures) to protect stability.
  - Essentially reverse any informal pressure from Trump’s working group that told regulators to ease up. Possibly disband any remnants of that working group.
- **Stablecoin Regulation & Legislation:** Work with Congress (since stablecoins might need statutory clarity) to **establish guardrails for stablecoin issuers**. This could mean requiring them to hold high-quality reserves and undergo audits, possibly even a charter framework (like a special bank charter for stablecoin issuers, which the PWG report under Biden recommended). At a minimum, have regulators use existing authority (like the NYDFS did for Paxos etc.) to supervise stablecoins if Congress is slow. This repairs the risk of stablecoin runs by ensuring they truly are stable and transparent, addressing a key vulnerability Trump's EO left unaddressed. 
- **Reprioritize Consumer Protection:** The new admin’s CFPB and FTC should take an active role in the crypto sphere, policing unfair or deceptive practices (like false advertising of returns, or undisclosed risks). Re-launch public awareness campaigns about crypto risks that a pro-crypto admin would have muted. Possibly push for **investor protection legislation** for crypto assets, or update laws to cover crypto under existing consumer protection (for example, clarify that crypto falls under EFTA for fraud recourse when feasible). If big failures occurred under Trump’s watch, address the fallout by setting up restitution funds or bankruptcy reforms (as needed).
- **AML and Sanctions Enforcement:** To repair any lapse in anti-illicit efforts, the new administration should have Treasury and DOJ **crack down on illicit crypto uses**. That might involve sanctioning more crypto mixers (like how Tornado Cash was sanctioned) or prosecuting exchanges that facilitate laundering. Reinforce cooperation with allies on tracking and stopping illicit flows (re-engage with FATF’s Crypto Travel Rule enforcement globally). Possibly designate a “crypto czar” at DOJ to coordinate enforcement across agencies, signaling it's again a priority to prevent crypto from undermining sanctions (e.g., against Russia or terrorist funding).
- **Energy and Climate Mitigation:** Consider actions to address the environmental impact of crypto mining. For instance, the EPA and DOE could be directed to set **energy efficiency standards or reporting requirements for large crypto miners**, or encourage miners to use flared gas or renewables to mitigate emissions. Reintroduce the interagency process from Biden’s EO to evaluate climate effects and solutions (like incentivizing more efficient mining tech or even exploring alternatives to energy-intensive proof-of-work consensus). While not directly undoing Trump's EO, it counters one of its major oversights by tackling the environmental harm head-on.
- **Public Engagement and Realignment of Narrative:** The new administration should communicate a **balanced narrative on crypto**: acknowledge innovation potential (so as not to alienate the industry entirely or crypto enthusiasts) but emphasize safeguarding the public and stability. They can cite the aftermath of any issues in 2024-25 as lessons learned (if an exchange collapsed or a stablecoin depegged during Trump, use that example: *“We saw what happens when there's no oversight - people get hurt. We won't let that happen again.”*). Engaging with the crypto industry in dialogue (but not letting them dictate policy) can help craft smarter regulation that the industry can live with, preventing an antagonistic whiplash. 
- **Reopen CBDC Research Collaboration:** If the Fed is open to it, support them in resuming at least pilot studies of a digital dollar. Perhaps take a measured approach like exploring a wholesale CBDC (for banks) or a narrow-use retail CBDC (for government payments) to build confidence and show it's not a surveillance tool. Also, implement any privacy-preserving tech in the CBDC design to counter arguments. By showing a commitment to privacy and not surveilling transactions, the admin can blunt one major criticism. This repair step is important to ensure the U.S. isn't left behind: by 2028, many major economies may have some form of CBDC; the U.S. should be ready or at least well-informed to decide.
- **International Recommitment:** Rejoin and possibly lead global regulatory discussions for crypto. For example, maybe host an international summit on crypto regulation to come up with common standards (like on stablecoin reserves, exchanges KYC rules, etc.). Show allies the U.S. is back to being a team player on combating crypto illicit finance, which repairs trust and prevents bad actors from exploiting differences between jurisdictions.
- **Legislative Guardrails:** While difficult given partisan divides, if there's any appetite, push for bipartisan legislation on certain crypto issues—some areas have cross-party interest, like stablecoin rules (which both some Democrats and Republicans wanted to clarify) or clarifying agency jurisdictions to end turf wars. Even partial legislative progress can provide stability to the regulatory environment beyond the term. And codifying consumer protections or AML requirements for crypto would make it harder for another President to swing the pendulum so widely again.

By reversing the outright ban on CBDC and reinstating a **comprehensive approach that balances innovation with risk mitigation**, the future administration can protect consumers and the financial system while still fostering fintech growth. Essentially, **go back to the roadmap set in early 2022** but updated with lessons from 2022-25. This will likely mean a safer crypto ecosystem (fewer scams going unchecked), better integration of crypto with mainstream finance under rule of law, and a U.S. that leads by example in making digital finance serve society, not just speculators. It also reintroduces **flexibility**: if crypto tech turns out beneficial, it can flourish under sensible oversight; if it falters or is harmful, regulators have the mandate to step in – something Trump’s EO took away.

---

## EO 14179 — **Removing Barriers to American Leadership in Artificial Intelligence**  
**Alternative Title:** *“Eliminating AI Safety and Ethics Protections to ‘Dominate’ AI at Any Cost”*

**What the EO Does:**  
- **Revokes Biden’s AI Order & Principles:** **Revokes EO 14110 of Oct 30, 2023** (President Biden’s order titled “Safe, Secure, and Trustworthy Development and Use of AI”) and instructs officials to review and undo any regulations, policies, or actions taken under that order that might impede AI innovation. Biden’s EO 14110 had implemented numerous safeguards (e.g., requiring red-team testing of advanced AI models, setting standards for AI in government, addressing algorithmic discrimination, etc.). Trump’s order nullifies those directives, essentially **removing federal requirements on AI developers to assess and mitigate risks**. It says agencies should *“suspend, revise, or rescind”* any actions derived from EO 14110 that are inconsistent with Trump’s new policy【38†L105-L## EO 14179 — **Removing Barriers to American Leadership in Artificial Intelligence**  
**Alternative Title:** *“Eliminating AI Safety and Ethics Protections to ‘Dominate’ AI at Any Cost”*

**What the EO Does:**  
- **Revokes Biden’s AI Order & Rules:** **Revokes EO 14110 of Oct 30, 2023** (President Biden’s “Safe, Secure, and Trustworthy Development and Use of Artificial Intelligence”). EO 14110 had put in place guardrails for AI – requiring federal agencies to do algorithmic impact assessments, directing development of AI safety standards, addressing bias, etc. By revoking it, Trump’s EO **removes those mandates**. It instructs the Assistant to the President for Science & Technology, the Special Advisor for AI & Crypto, and the National Security Advisor, in coordination with agency heads, to **immediately review all policies, directives, regulations, and actions taken pursuant to EO 14110 and identify those that are or may be “obstacles” to the new pro-AI-dominance policy**. For any such actions, agencies are to **suspend, revise, or rescind them** promptly. If something can’t be undone instantly (maybe due to rulemaking processes), agencies must give all possible exemptions in the interim. In effect, this halts Biden-initiated efforts like requiring government contractors to adhere to AI ethics guidelines, or requiring agencies to test AI systems for bias. 
- **Declares Policy of AI Dominance:** Proclaims it is **U.S. policy to sustain and enhance America’s global leadership (“dominance”) in AI** to promote economic competitiveness, national security, and “human flourishing”. This sets a tone that **winning the AI race** is a top priority, and implies that any rules that slow AI development could impede that. 
- **Eliminates “Ideological” Constraints:** The purpose section explicitly says AI systems *“must be free from ideological bias or engineered social agendas”*. This is a jab at efforts to make AI outputs align with certain values (like not producing hate speech or misinformation – which some on the right call “woke” bias). Removing “engineered social agendas” suggests the EO opposes programming AI with fairness or anti-bias constraints that might influence results. So it’s effectively instructing that AI development should prioritize raw capability over concerns like avoiding offensive content or ensuring equitable outcomes.
- **Develops an AI Action Plan:** Orders that within **180 days**, the Assistant to the President for Science & Tech (APST), the Special Advisor for AI & Crypto, and the National Security Advisor, in coordination with other key officials (like the Economic and Domestic Policy Advisors, OMB Director, etc.), shall **develop and submit to the President an Action Plan to achieve the dominance policy**. This plan likely will outline how to accelerate AI R&D, deploy AI in government and military, and remove remaining barriers. It’s a coordinated strategy document to realize the EO’s goals. 
- **Amends OMB Guidance:** Instructs that within **60 days**, the OMB Director, in coordination with APST, shall revise or rescind two specific OMB Memoranda (M-24-10 and M-24-18) as necessary to align with the new policy. These memos were issued under Biden to guide agencies on AI – one was about government AI procurement and risk management, another about implementing AI safeguards in the federal use. Revising them likely means stripping out requirements like ensuring AI is transparent, documenting algorithmic impacts, etc., and replacing them with more permissive language focusing on efficiency and innovation.
- **General Provisions:** Standard legal boilerplate that nothing in the EO shall impair legal authorities of departments or OMB’s budget role, and that it be implemented consistent with applicable law and budget constraints. It also says it doesn’t create enforceable rights, meaning no one can sue saying “I was prevented from deploying AI, violating this EO” – it’s guiding internal policy, not guaranteeing rights.

**What it Claims to Do (Stated Justification):**  
The EO frames itself as necessary to **keep the U.S. at the forefront of AI innovation** by clearing away needless impediments:
- **Maintain Global AI Leadership:** It explicitly says the U.S. must sustain and enhance global AI dominance to promote human flourishing, competitiveness, and security. The implication is that to remain number one, the government must act quickly and decisively, not get bogged down in caution or red tape. It casts any slow or restrictive approach as a threat to that leadership position.
- **Remove Barriers to Innovation:** The EO’s whole thrust is that **existing policies (like Biden’s EO) imposed barriers or “obstacles” to AI innovation**. By removing them, it claims AI development can proceed faster and more efficiently. It speaks of *“decisively acting”* to retain global leadership – meaning regulations or processes that slowed decisive action should go.
- **Prevent Ideological Bias in AI:** It argues that AI systems *“must be free from ideological bias or engineered social agendas”*. The justification here is likely that injecting politics or social ideologies into AI (for instance, making an AI refuse to generate offensive content) “hampers” the objective performance or utility of AI. It suggests that previous policies tried to make AI “politically correct” at the expense of truth or capability. So this EO claims to ensure AI output is not distorted by any agenda – implicitly promising more “neutral” or unfiltered AI that some on the right desired (many conservatives felt AI like ChatGPT had a liberal bias, as it refused certain content).
- **Encourage Use of AI for National Benefit:** Though not extremely detailed in the text, by using phrases like “promote human flourishing”, it implies that the freer use of AI will yield societal benefits – medical breakthroughs, economic growth, improved government services – and the best way to get there is to not over-regulate or constrain AI creativity. Essentially, trust the innovators and remove handcuffs.
- **Modernize Government Use of AI:** Implicitly, by rescinding Biden’s complex process for agencies to evaluate AI, Trump’s EO could be claiming to **streamline government adoption of AI** (no need for lengthy bias reviews, etc., just deploy AI to improve services or defense). It might argue that burdensome compliance steps slow down agencies from using AI to serve citizens, so removing those yields a more efficient government.

In summary, the stated justification is that **America’s competitive edge in AI was being blunted by overly cautious, “woke,” or bureaucratic rules**. By eliminating those, the U.S. can innovate faster, implement AI more widely, and thus stay ahead of rivals like China – leading to prosperity and security.

**Real Purpose or Effect (Critical Perspective):**  
This EO’s real purpose is to **prioritize rapid AI development and deployment over safety, ethics, or potential social harms**. It is heavily influenced by an ideological stance that concerns about bias or misuse are hindrances. Likely real effects include:
- **Halting AI Oversight Mechanisms:** All the processes Biden set up – algorithmic bias assessments, AI system certification, requiring big AI models to be tested for safety or watermarked – are stopped. Agencies that were preparing AI governance policies (like guidance on using AI in hiring decisions or policing) will cease those efforts. For example, OMB memos M-24-10 and M-24-18 that Trump ordered revised had requirements for agencies to inventory AI systems, assess them for risks, and ensure human oversight. Those will be gutted, meaning agencies can now implement AI tools without such checks. If the EPA or HUD had started examining how AI might inadvertently discriminate, that work likely stops.
- **Greenlight for Controversial AI Uses:** With “no ideological agenda” imposed, agencies like DOJ or DHS may feel freer to use AI surveillance or profiling tools that previously would’ve been flagged for bias or civil liberties issues. E.g., FBI might deploy face recognition broadly despite known racial bias issues, or TSA might integrate AI that “profiles” passengers without having to consider disparate impact. The EO’s tone basically says *“go ahead, use AI, don’t worry about political correctness.”* So one effect is potentially **wider use of AI in law enforcement, immigration, intelligence, etc., with fewer safeguards**, raising risks of wrongful arrests, privacy invasion, or harassment of minorities due to uncorrected biases in data.
- **Encourage Unfiltered AI Outputs:** In the private sector, although the EO directly affects government and policy, its ethos of removing “bias filters” could influence how companies approach AI too (especially if any regulatory pressure to moderate AI outputs is lifted). For instance, under Biden, agencies like FTC hinted that harmful AI outputs could be seen as unfair practices; under Trump’s policy, that pressure is gone. So AI companies might feel freer to let their models produce whatever (violent, extremist, or harassing content) in the name of free discourse, whereas earlier they tried to moderate to avoid public backlash or regulatory scrutiny. **This could lead to more AI-generated disinformation, hate speech, or extremist recruiting material circulating**, since the government signaled it’s not concerned with filtering that.
- **Industry Relief from Potential Regulations:** Trump’s EO would stop efforts that might have led to new regulations on AI. For example, Biden’s order tasked NIST to set AI safety standards and agencies to possibly make them mandatory for high-risk AI. Trump’s reversal means no new binding standards. AI developers can self-police, which many won’t rigorously do if not required. It’s a **deregulatory boon for big tech and startups in AI**, letting them iterate fast without compliance burdens. But as seen with social media, lack of guardrails can also cause social harms that eventually invite a bigger backlash. In short term, they avoid costs of compliance; long term, a scandal (like AI causing a major accident or injustice) could prompt an even more draconian response down the line.
- **Faster Government Adoption (with risks):** Government agencies might adopt AI tools more quickly now – e.g., use AI to triage veterans’ medical claims or automate parts of IRS auditing – because they don’t have to do lengthy risk assessments first. This may improve efficiency somewhat, but doing so without careful testing could lead to errors harming citizens (like an AI incorrectly denying benefits to eligible people, and those decisions not being caught because human oversight was minimal). The removal of Biden’s checks means **citizens dealing with government AIs have less protection** if the AI is wrong or biased. They might not even know an AI made the decision (transparency requirements were likely removed).
- **National Security Focus:** The heavy involvement of the National Security Advisor and mention of global competition indicates a big impetus was military/intel usage of AI. Trump’s admin likely felt Biden’s AI EO hindered DOD – for instance, Biden’s order said autonomous military systems should have appropriate human judgment. Trump’s approach likely relaxes such constraints, speeding up development of things like autonomous drones or AI surveillance in intelligence. The effect is possibly **accelerated deployment of AI in warfare or spying**, with less ethical review (the “bias” language could also cover perceived left-leaning ethics concerns in autonomous weapons). This could raise moral issues or even international instability if the U.S. deploys, say, lethal autonomous systems more readily. It also might escalate AI arms race dynamics: e.g., if the U.S. drops some commitments to not have AI make kill decisions, other nations might follow suit, eroding nascent global norms.
- **Loss of Global Trust and Coordination on AI Ethics:** Biden’s AI agenda was aligned with allies on promoting trustworthy AI (the EU, for instance, is regulating AI for safety/bias). Trump’s pivot to pure dominance and scrapping ethics might distance the U.S. from allied cooperation on setting AI standards. Allies could see the U.S. as going rogue or being reckless, undermining collaborations on AI ethics (like within the OECD or G7). This could harm the U.S.’s soft power and moral leadership in tech governance. Companies might face challenges if their AI products conflict with stricter EU rules vs. lax US rules, possibly having to choose which standard to build for – ironically, if EU’s big market leads them to build to EU standards, U.S. domestic users might get less safe products under our rules. So a fragmentation risk emerges.

**Fit into Trump’s Broader Agenda:**  
This EO aligns with Trump’s broader drive to **remove what he sees as “woke” influences from government policy** (here, AI bias mitigation is painted as woke social engineering) and to favor **unfettered American economic and military power**. It echoes his regulatory rollbacks in other fields: just as he removed environmental rules to boost drilling, he removes AI rules to boost tech development. It's a pattern of short-circuiting careful processes in favor of immediate assertiveness.

It also fits into his **culture war**: Many conservatives believed AI like ChatGPT was biased against them. Trump addressing that (even indirectly through policy) plays to his base’s grievance that Silicon Valley censors them. This EO is a dog-whistle that his administration will ensure AI isn’t another “liberal indoctrination tool” – a framing many on the right had. So it’s as much cultural signalling as economic policy.

Additionally, it aligns with his **America First** approach internationally: outcompete China in AI at all costs. He often criticized China’s unethical practices but also envied their ability to push tech without regulatory hindrance. This EO effectively says the U.S. will be just as aggressive, not letting bureaucracy hold it back, akin to how he viewed China’s single-minded industrial policies.

It’s also consistent with his disregard for traditional expert input. Where Biden convened ethicists and civil society in AI planning, Trump’s EO – co-written likely by people like his AI/Crypto advisor – explicitly sidelines those voices in favor of industry and national security perspectives. This continues his trend of **diminishing scientific/academic expertise in policymaking** if it conflicts with his goals (similar to climate science being sidelined under him).

**Harm Caused:**  
- **Unchecked AI Discrimination & Errors:** By removing requirements to check AI systems for bias or error, the EO sets the stage for **more incidents of AI causing unjust outcomes**. For example, an AI used in hiring might reject women or minority candidates at disproportionate rates (as has happened) and no one is required now to catch or correct that. Or an AI in healthcare might work worse for certain ethnic groups (due to training data bias), leading to misdiagnosis or unequal care. Without mandated testing and transparency, these harms can go undetected longer, impacting many lives. Essentially, **people could face discrimination or denial of opportunities/services by algorithm, with little recourse**, because the safeguards that would have flagged or prevented it were removed. This undermines civil rights progress and could violate anti-discrimination laws, leading to lawsuits or social strife when patterns become apparent.
- **Privacy Erosion & Surveillance:** Freed from “ideological” constraints, agencies or companies might deploy AI surveillance tools more widely. This could mean **more face recognition cameras in public, AI analyzing our online speech, or governments using AI to profile for policing**. The harm is to privacy and free expression – people may be constantly monitored or judged by algorithms without consent. The chilling effect on dissent or marginalized communities (who historically get over-policed) could intensify. A specific example: ICE might use AI to predict who is undocumented in a community by data-mining utility records – a practice that could sweep up even citizens, and previously might have been curbed by ethical guidelines.
- **Public Safety & Health Risks:** Removing requirements for thorough safety testing of advanced AI (like self-driving cars or AI in medical devices) could literally be life-threatening. If, say, self-driving car companies are not pushed to thoroughly test edge cases (because regulations got relaxed), **more accidents could occur**. Or if FDA under pressure rushes approval of AI diagnostic tools without rigorous trials (to not be seen as impeding innovation), misdiagnoses could harm patients. In the military realm, deploying AI weapons quickly might lead to a tragedy (e.g., an autonomous drone misidentifies a civilian target). So physical harm to life and property is a real risk when complex tech isn’t carefully vetted.
- **Misinformation and Extremism Spread:** Without a stance against AI-generated disinformation, this EO essentially permits an environment where deepfakes and AI fake news can proliferate. No serious effort from government to regulate or discourage it will happen (Biden’s EO had things like watermarking AI content to help identify deepfakes; Trump’s removal of that means such measures won’t be pursued). This could harm democracy – e.g., in 2026 or 2028 elections, there might be a flood of convincing AI deepfakes and fake articles targeting candidates or voter groups, and fewer tools to combat it. Social cohesion could suffer as truth becomes harder to discern (the “post-truth” problem on steroids via AI). Extremist groups might also exploit unfiltered AI to create propaganda or even automate the generation of recruitment content tailored to susceptible individuals – fueling radicalization.
- **Loss of AI Ethics Leadership Globally:** The U.S. was, under Biden, positioning as a leader in ethical AI (e.g., launching the Global Partnership on AI, etc.). Trump’s turn away likely ceded that leadership. Countries that care about responsible AI (like many EU nations, Canada, etc.) might no longer look to collaborate with the U.S. on AI norms, weakening global efforts to ensure AI is used for good. In the long run, that could harm everyone, as AI’s challenges are cross-border (cybersecurity, arms control). If the U.S. isn’t pushing for guardrails, those might not happen – increasing global risk of incidents (like an AI arms race mishap, or AIs exacerbating global inequities).
- **Erosion of Public Trust in AI:** As AI is rolled out faster and with less oversight, it’s likely there will be high-profile failures or harms (e.g., an AI banking algorithm causes many wrongful account freezes). These incidents can cause the public to lose trust in AI systems broadly. That would be a harm because beneficial uses of AI (like medical diagnostics or climate modeling) might face more public resistance. Essentially, by not tempering AI’s rough edges, the tech community risks a backlash that slows adoption of even the good AI. The EO’s approach is short-term pro-expansion, but long-term it could hurt AI’s reputation, ironically undermining U.S. AI leadership if people/policymakers later insist on heavy-handed restrictions after being burned.
- **Marginalization of Vulnerable Populations:** Historically, lack of consideration in tech design hits vulnerable groups hardest. By stripping out “equity” measures, this EO means **AI tools will be less likely to account for needs of people with disabilities, racial or gender minorities, non-English speakers, etc.** For instance, an education AI might only be trained on English data, so non-English-speaking students get left behind by EdTech advances. Or a hiring AI might automatically screen out people with unconventional work histories (often including marginalized folks), exacerbating existing inequalities. So the harm is that the benefits of AI will not be distributed fairly – they may accrue to those already advantaged, leaving others further behind, thereby increasing societal disparities.
