- **Internal Government Mishaps and Inefficiencies:** While the EO aimed to increase efficiency by cutting “red tape,” implementing AI without due diligence can lead to **government failures**: e.g., an AI system for processing benefits could malfunction and create huge backlogs or erroneous denials that then require expensive, time-consuming remediation by humans and legal battles. So ironically, by not doing careful up-front analysis, agencies might face bigger messes later. That hurts citizens needing services and wastes taxpayer money, the opposite of “efficient government.”

**How a Future Administration Could Undo or Repair Damage:**  
- **Reinstate AI Safeguards and Ethical Guidelines:** The next President should **reinstate Biden’s EO 14110 or issue a comparable directive** restoring requirements for AI safety, security, and trustworthiness in both government and industry. For instance, reissue the directive that agencies perform algorithmic impact assessments and public AI risk disclosures. Reinstate the requirement for NIST to set AI risk management standards and for agencies to adopt them. Essentially, **pick up the infrastructure that EO 14179 tore down**. This might involve re-issuing the OMB memos M-24-10 and M-24-18 (which Trump told to revise) in their original or updated form to guide agencies on using AI responsibly. It should also explicitly revoke any guidance stemming from Trump’s EO – e.g., if agencies were told to skip bias testing, instruct them to resume it. 
- **Re-establish the AI Bill of Rights principles:** Biden’s OSTP had created an AI Bill of Rights (nonbinding) outlining principles like algorithmic discrimination protections and data privacy. The new admin could formally adopt those principles across agencies and even seek to codify parts of them. For example, issue guidance that any AI used in federal decision-making must allow human appeal and must be tested for bias – aligning with those principles. This would directly counter the “no ideological agenda” stance by saying **we do have an agenda: civil rights and fairness are non-negotiable in AI use**. 
- **Re-create an AI oversight framework:** Possibly propose **legislation** or support efforts to regulate high-risk AI (similar to the EU’s AI Act but tailored to U.S.). If Congress is willing, push for laws requiring companies to do safety testing and bias audits for AI systems in critical sectors (finance, healthcare, etc.). Even without new laws, use existing powers: e.g., the FTC Act to go after companies whose AI causes harm by being biased or insecure (which FTC signaled under Biden). The new admin can publicly commit to using enforcement if AI deployments violate consumer protection or equal opportunity laws, essentially reactivating a deterrent for companies to consider ethics or face penalty. 
- **Balanced “AI Dominance” Approach:** The new strategy should articulate that **American leadership in AI will be achieved by leading in responsible, trustworthy AI, not just fastest-to-market**. So include in the national AI strategy sections on ethics, international cooperation, and societal impact. Repair means shifting from purely competitive rhetoric to *“We will lead the world in developing powerful AI that is safe and benefits everyone.”* This retains a leadership frame (to continue focus and investment) but marries it with safeguards (so it doesn’t become an excuse to ignore risks). That includes rejoining international initiatives (like the Global Partnership on AI) in earnest and maybe hosting a global summit on AI ethics and governance to show the U.S. is back at the table shaping responsible norms. 
- **Re-integrate Social Scientists & Ethicists into Advisory Roles:** Under Trump, voices concerned with social impacts were likely sidelined (e.g., AI ethicists left government). The new admin should bring those experts back into OSTP, PCAST (as earlier discussed), and agency advisory boards. Ensure every major AI project in government has an ethics team consult or a red-team exercise. Institutionalize processes – e.g., create a standing **Algorithmic Accountability Council** in each major agency to review AI use cases from a civil rights perspective. This rebuilds internal checks that Trump removed. 
- **Set Federal Procurement Standards for AI:** Use the power of federal purchasing to drive ethical AI. For example, require that any AI system the government buys or uses must meet certain standards (transparency, bias testing, security). Biden’s EO had something like this in progress; enforce it. This not only fixes government’s direct use but also influences vendors (they’ll comply to sell to USG, then often use those standards commercially too). It’s a way to raise the floor across industry indirectly. 
- **Public Communication & Education:** After the free-for-all under Trump, public trust may be shaky if there were incidents. The new admin should communicate clearly why it’s reintroducing these “guardrails”: emphasize stories of AI harms that happened (if any) during the lax period to illustrate why oversight matters – *“We saw an AI recruiting tool discriminate against women; we are ensuring that doesn’t happen again.”* Show empathy for those negatively affected by unregulated AI. At the same time, reassure innovators that the goal isn’t to stifle innovation but to ensure long-term viability of AI by earning public trust. Possibly start public campaigns about “Trustworthy AI” to highlight positive examples of well-governed AI (like a hospital AI that underwent rigorous validation and now safely helps detect cancer). This helps shift the narrative from “any regulation = anti-innovation” to “smart regulation = better innovation.”
- **Address Specific Fallout Cases:** If Trump’s policy led to particular problems – e.g., an unfair denial of benefits by an AI – the new admin should remedy those cases. This could mean reviewing controversial AI decisions made by agencies in the prior 2-3 years and correcting wrongful outcomes (perhaps through appeals or reviewing denied claims en masse). Also, investigate any alleged discriminatory outcomes (like if reports come that a federal mortgage AI gave fewer loans to minority applicants, have HUD’s civil rights office investigate and fix). Taking corrective action shows commitment to making the harmed parties whole and underscores the importance of oversight. 
- **Regain Global Trust in US AI Policy:** Let allies and international partners know the U.S. is back to prioritizing AI ethics. Re-engage in forums like OECD’s AI principles implementation, or propose a joint U.S.-EU initiative to align on some AI standards (maybe around transparency or safety for advanced models). Also, resume funding research into AI ethics and safety (Trump likely cut OSTP/NSF focus on that). Possibly lead an effort on creating an international code of conduct for military AI (to address concerns of lethal AI that Trump’s approach exacerbated). This leadership can help avoid an arms race and prevent misunderstandings (e.g., clear norms might reduce risk of an autonomous weapon miscalculation between great powers). 
- **Future-proof AI Policy with Legislation or Norms:** Consider advocating for establishing an **independent AI Safety Commission** or something by law, similar to the Nuclear Regulatory Commission concept. If bipartisan agreement on specifics is hard, at least set up multi-stakeholder bodies (academia, industry, government) to monitor AI progress and advise on needed adjustments regularly. By building a structure that outlives one administration, it can keep momentum on responsible AI even if politics shift again. Codifying some principles (like requiring testing of AI systems that affect human rights) would also make it harder for another president to just erase them via EO.

In short, a future administration must **restore a balanced approach: embracing AI’s promise while re-imposing sensible checks**. That means undoing the wholesale deregulation of EO 14179 and replacing it with a framework that marries innovation with safety and fairness. Done right, this will not only prevent the worst harms but also ensure public acceptance of AI – which in the long run supports the goal of American AI leadership (since people won’t support what they deeply distrust). By repairing the omissions and excesses of Trump’s policy, the U.S. can pursue AI advancement in a sustainable, inclusive manner that secures both competitive advantage and the public good.

---

## EO 14180 — **Council To Assess the Federal Emergency Management Agency**  
**Alternative Title:** *“Politicized ‘Reform’ Council Aimed at Undermining FEMA and Disaster Aid”*

**What the EO Does:**  
- **Creates a FEMA Review Council:** Establishes a **Federal Emergency Management Agency (FEMA) Review Council** of up to 20 members to thoroughly evaluate FEMA’s performance, structure, and policies. The Council is co-chaired by the **Secretary of Homeland Security** (who oversees FEMA) and the **Secretary of Defense**. Other members include relevant Cabinet secretaries and agency heads (likely from agencies involved in disaster response like HUD, HHS, etc.) and importantly, **“distinguished individuals… from outside the Federal Government”** appointed by Trump. These outside members are to have expertise in disaster relief, emergency preparedness, state-Federal relationships, budget management, etc. So effectively, it’s a mix of federal officials and perhaps former state/local officials or experts, but those outside picks will reflect Trump’s point of view. 
- **Mandates a Broad Study of FEMA’s Effectiveness & Bias:** The Council is tasked to advise the President on whether FEMA can “capably and impartially” address disasters across the U.S., and to recommend changes to ensure it serves the national interest. Specifically, within 180 days it must produce a report including:
  - **Assessment of FEMA’s disaster responses over the past 4 years** (i.e., 2021-2024, during Biden’s term) including staffing sufficiency.
  - **Comparison of FEMA’s responses to those by state, local, and private sector** in the same events, evaluating speed, supplies, efficacy, etc.. This likely seeks to see if states or NGOs outperformed FEMA.
  - **Review of commentary and debate about FEMA’s role in our federal system** and how disaster relief functioned historically before FEMA or before it joined DHS. (This suggests considering whether disaster response should revert more to states or pre-FEMA approaches.)
  - **Examination of the traditional role of States vs. Federal government in disasters** to see if FEMA has supplanted too much state control.
  - **Evaluation of whether FEMA can serve as a support agency rather than supplanting state control of disaster relief**. This is key: it directly asks if FEMA should step back to let states lead more (a clear ideological push).
  - **Recommended improvements or structural changes to FEMA under current law**, and analysis of arguments for and against FEMA reform (including legality and merits of certain proposals).
  - It must also solicit public comments and hold at least one public meeting within 90 days, indicating some transparency and input (though likely dominated by those invited or who share the Council’s leanings).
- **Points to Alleged Bias & Bureaucracy Issues:** The EO’s preamble notes *“serious concerns of political bias in FEMA”* ([Executive Order 14180—Council To Assess the Federal Emergency Management Agency | The American Presidency Project](https://www.presidency.ucsb.edu/documents/executive-order-14180-council-assess-the-federal-emergency-management-agency#:~:text=There%20are%20serious%20concerns%20of,staff%20and%20resources%20to%20support)), including an anecdote that a former FEMA responder said managers directed her to avoid helping homes of Trump supporters ([Executive Order 14180—Council To Assess the Federal Emergency Management Agency | The American Presidency Project](https://www.presidency.ucsb.edu/documents/executive-order-14180-council-assess-the-federal-emergency-management-agency#:~:text=There%20are%20serious%20concerns%20of,staff%20and%20resources%20to%20support)). It also says despite $30B in yearly disaster aid, FEMA has “left vulnerable Americans without the resources or support they need”. And it accuses FEMA of losing mission focus by diverting staff to other tasks (hinting at things like climate change planning or maybe migration assistance) ([Executive Order 14180—Council To Assess the Federal Emergency Management Agency | The American Presidency Project](https://www.presidency.ucsb.edu/documents/executive-order-14180-council-assess-the-federal-emergency-management-agency#:~:text=There%20are%20serious%20concerns%20of,staff%20and%20resources%20to%20support)). This sets the stage that the Council will examine those issues – hinting it might find FEMA mismanaged or biased and thus needing overhaul.
- **General Provisions:** It clarifies the Council terminates 30 days after submitting its report, so it’s a temporary body. And it says nothing in the order shall limit DHS or DoD authority (the co-chairs) or OMB’s role, and that it’ll be implemented as permitted by law and funds. So not too much beyond the core tasks.

**What it Claims to Do (Stated Justification):**  
The EO claims to be a thorough, non-partisan review to **“drastically improve” FEMA’s efficacy, priorities, and competence** based on lessons from recent disasters. It cites perceived failures in response – e.g., referencing *“Hurricane Helene and other recent disasters”* leaving Americans without needed support. (Hurricane Helene is presumably a fictional example; in any case, it implies disasters under Biden weren’t handled well, even though by most accounts FEMA did pretty well for COVID and hurricanes like Ida, etc.) 

It says *“Despite obligating nearly $30B each of the past 3 years, FEMA has left vulnerable Americans without support when they need it most.”*. So the justification is that FEMA’s bureaucracy may be failing survivors, and possibly even injecting politics (helping or neglecting people based on political factors) ([Executive Order 14180—Council To Assess the Federal Emergency Management Agency | The American Presidency Project](https://www.presidency.ucsb.edu/documents/executive-order-14180-council-assess-the-federal-emergency-management-agency#:~:text=There%20are%20serious%20concerns%20of,staff%20and%20resources%20to%20support)). Trump’s order explicitly flags *“political bias”* – that an official allegedly said to avoid homes of his supporters ([Executive Order 14180—Council To Assess the Federal Emergency Management Agency | The American Presidency Project](https://www.presidency.ucsb.edu/documents/executive-order-14180-council-assess-the-federal-emergency-management-agency#:~:text=There%20are%20serious%20concerns%20of,staff%20and%20resources%20to%20support)). So he justifies the Council as needed to root out such bias and refocus FEMA on impartial service.

It also notes FEMA has lost focus by “diverting staff and resources to support missions beyond core disaster response” ([Executive Order 14180—Council To Assess the Federal Emergency Management Agency | The American Presidency Project](https://www.presidency.ucsb.edu/documents/executive-order-14180-council-assess-the-federal-emergency-management-agency#:~:text=There%20are%20serious%20concerns%20of,staff%20and%20resources%20to%20support)). This likely alludes to FEMA involvement in things like the border (if FEMA was used to help with migrant shelters under Biden, e.g., or with COVID vaccination efforts). So he justifies examining whether FEMA is stretched thin by non-disaster tasks – implying a need to limit its scope.

The **stated goal is to ensure FEMA best serves the national interest and truly supports states rather than controlling efforts**. By comparing FEMA to state/private responses, it suggests if states or NGOs did better, maybe FEMA should step back or be restructured.

In summary, the official rationale is *“FEMA is potentially broken or biased; we need an expert council to review its failures, examine if it should be restructured or its duties returned to states, and recommend reforms so that Americans get better disaster help.”*

**Real Purpose or Effect (Critical Perspective):**  
Critics see this as an attempt to **politicize FEMA and justify scaling back federal disaster aid**, perhaps punishing blue states or aligning with conservative small-government ideology:
- **Undermine Trust in FEMA:** By raising allegations of political bias (especially that anecdote about not helping Trump supporters) ([Executive Order 14180—Council To Assess the Federal Emergency Management Agency | The American Presidency Project](https://www.presidency.ucsb.edu/documents/executive-order-14180-council-assess-the-federal-emergency-management-agency#:~:text=There%20are%20serious%20concerns%20of,staff%20and%20resources%20to%20support)), Trump is seeding doubt about FEMA’s impartiality. The Council, presumably stacked with people sympathetic to him (maybe a GOP governor or a Heritage Foundation budget hawk), might “find” evidence of bias or mismanagement that justifies purges or a takeover. The effect could be to weaken FEMA’s credibility, making it easier to justify drastic changes (like replacing leadership or moving functions). It also preempts blame: if a disaster response under his watch goes poorly, he can claim “FEMA was dysfunctional because of the previous admin, as our Council showed.”
- **Lay Groundwork for Cutting/Restructuring FEMA:** Many conservatives have historically argued FEMA should be downsized or even abolished, with responsibilities given back to states or the private sector. This Council’s remit to consider historical disaster relief and whether FEMA “supplants state control” hints strongly that it may recommend scaling back federal role, maybe even suggest moving FEMA out of DHS or breaking it up. The real intent may be to justify **cutting FEMA’s budget or narrowing its mission** – e.g., eliminating long-term recovery programs or pre-disaster mitigation grants that they see as “wasteful” or politically driven (like FEMA’s equity initiative under Biden). It might even propose privatizing some functions (like contracting out more).
- **Politicize Disaster Aid Allocation:** Trump often complained about aid to states he didn't favor (e.g., threatening to withhold wildfire aid to California). A Council that “finds” e.g. that California’s emergency management is better left to itself might provide cover to give less federal aid there. Essentially it could rationalize a **more partisan distribution of disaster assistance** – rewarding allies (red states) and being stingier with adversaries (blue states), under the guise of following the Council's “expert” recommendations about state vs. federal roles. The Council’s mention of commentary may include conservative criticisms that say FEMA under Democrats was too generous to Democratic constituencies. 
- **FEMA Redirection or Purge:** If the Council says FEMA lost focus by doing DEI or climate or minor crises, Trump could use that to slash those programs or purge staff who championed them. For example, if FEMA did a lot on COVID or equity initiatives, the Council might label that mission creep, and Trump would then cut FEMA’s Office of Resilience (which handles climate adaptation) or its equity programs. In extreme, he might replace current FEMA leadership with someone who shares his philosophy (even before Council finishes, he fired Biden’s FEMA Administrator and put a loyalist). 
- **Increase Military Role in Disasters:** Co-chairing with Defense suggests interest in how DoD could take on more. The Council might highlight how the military often is effective in disasters (National Guard, Army Corps) and possibly suggest shifting more response leadership to DoD (e.g., formalizing a bigger role for Northern Command in big disasters). That could lead to a quasi-militarization of disaster response – something historically avoided because civilian control is preferred for humanitarian response. But Trump might like the optics of generals handling crises. So the effect could be FEMA being eclipsed by DoD in some scenarios, raising concerns about the appropriateness of military leading domestic missions.
- **Centralize or Devolve?** There’s a tension: Some language suggests devolving to states more, other parts having a council and Defense involved hints at more White House control. Likely Trump’s aim is to centralize control in his hands (via the White House and maybe Defense assets he commands) for when he wants, but otherwise push routine burden to states. So basically **politically-driven centralization**: use FEMA when it suits him (and ensure loyalty), but not as an automatic first responder for all disasters (especially those in states he thinks should fend for themselves). 
- **Budget and Ideology:** It fits a broader small-government, cost-cutting ideology. FEMA spends tens of billions on disasters (and climate change is making disasters worse, so costs rising). Conservatives often balk at this spending (especially for things like mitigation that they tie to climate policy). The Council might provide justification to cut mitigation grants or require states to cost-share more for relief. This shifts burden to states or individuals, aligning with right-wing views that federal disaster spending creates moral hazard or is a “blue state bailout.” 
- **Rebuke to Biden’s approach:** Biden expanded FEMA's role (e.g., funding for COVID vaccination, building shelters for migrants). This Council explicitly condemns using FEMA for things like migrant shelter support (the “diverting staff” line likely refers to that) ([Executive Order 14180—Council To Assess the Federal Emergency Management Agency | The American Presidency Project](https://www.presidency.ucsb.edu/documents/executive-order-14180-council-assess-the-federal-emergency-management-agency#:~:text=There%20are%20serious%20concerns%20of,staff%20and%20resources%20to%20support)). So it’s an ideological course-correct: *“FEMA should stick to hurricanes and floods, not pandemic or border or equity stuff.”* That appeals to Trump's base who bristled at humanitarian spending in those areas.

**Fit into Trump’s Broader Agenda:**  
This EO matches his pattern of attacking institutions he perceives as aligned with Democrats or as part of the “deep state.” It’s reminiscent of his approach to, say, the FBI – claiming bias and setting up reviews (like he wanted with FBI “witch hunts”). Here FEMA is cast (baselessly, many would say) as biased against him. It also aligns with his **states-rights rhetoric** – like his comments during COVID that states should manage things and not rely on feds (except when praising his own interventions). It fits a Tea Party-ish view that federal emergency aid makes states reliant and people reckless (e.g., building in risky areas expecting bailouts).

It also is a populist move: if he can say *“FEMA was failing you and biased, I’m fixing it,”* he positions himself as the champion of the forgotten disaster victim (particularly, his narrative would be, rural Trump voters who he implies were neglected by FEMA under Dems – even though there’s scant evidence of partisan allocation of aid historically). It partakes in grievance politics – *“they even politicized hurricane relief!”* – which riles up his base and justifies drastic actions.

Another angle: It helps him justify not acting strongly on climate. If FEMA downplays climate adaptation (because Council might say FEMA should only react, not work on mitigating future disasters, which they sometimes do via resilience grants), then the admin can ignore climate risk in emergency planning, consistent with his climate change skepticism.

**Harm Caused:**  
- **Disruption of FEMA Operations and Morale:** This council and its implications likely unsettled FEMA’s workforce. If leadership and employees suspect the administration believes them biased or is looking to slash the agency, **morale and effectiveness could suffer**. Key staff might leave (fear of purge or disagreement with direction), taking expertise with them. During its review, decision-making at FEMA could slow or become overly cautious (to avoid political landmines). For example, if a disaster hit a blue state, FEMA officials might second-guess how much help to send, worrying about appearing biased one way or the other. That **hesitation or attrition could degrade response speed and quality**, ironically causing the poor outcomes the council is ostensibly investigating to become more likely.
- **Potential Reduction in Disaster Aid:** If Trump followed through with changes (either via administrative actions or push for legislative changes) that cut FEMA’s role, disaster survivors may get **less federal assistance or slower help**. For instance, if more responsibility is shifted to states, poor states might struggle to fill the gap, leaving communities without adequate shelter, debris removal, or rebuilding funds after a major hurricane or wildfire. The **harm is to the victims** who might not get help they would have received before. If federal aid is more “targeted” or limited, some long-term recovery projects (like rebuilding public infrastructure or housing) might not happen or be much delayed. That can stall regional recovery for years, increasing economic distress and personal suffering after disasters.
- **Unequal Impact (Wealthy vs. Poor States):** A pullback of FEMA would widen inequities. Richer states or those with robust emergency management and budgets (like Texas or Florida) might handle more on their own (though even they rely heavily on FEMA funds for huge disasters). Poor or smaller states (like Mississippi or Puerto Rico territory) that can’t muster large-scale aid themselves would fare worse in disasters, deepening regional disparities. This contravenes FEMA’s mission to ensure a baseline of aid for all Americans. Americans might experience **different levels of recovery based on where they live** more than before – an Alabama tornado survivor might get less support compared to a similar event in a wealthy state that can supplement aid.
- **Politicization Leading to Neglect of Some Disasters:** If recommendations justify it, Trump could have selectively decided which disasters “merit” major federal help. Possibly disasters in blue states (wildfires in CA, floods in NY) might have seen less robust federal support, under argument that states should handle more or that FEMA doesn’t need to overextend. Conversely, red-state events might get full support for political reasons. **Disaster response should be apolitical** – once that trust is broken, it can cause enormous resentment and even loss of life if aid is withheld. People in slight-of-hand disfavored areas might be left more vulnerable. 
- **Delay and Confusion in Active Disaster Response:** The Council’s existence and any interim findings could have hamstrung FEMA’s ongoing operations. For example, if a hurricane struck during the Council’s review, would FEMA leadership worry about how their actions will be judged? Possibly they might be extra stingy or bureaucratic to toe some anticipated line, hampering improvisation that often helps in crises. Or if Trump instituted changes before the council even finished (like requiring higher state cost-shares or paperwork to “ensure impartiality”), that could slow down immediate relief distribution. **Lives and property could be at risk if FEMA’s agility is reduced** by second-guessing or new hurdles, exactly when speed is needed.
- **Loss of Preparedness & Mitigation Focus:** The Council’s likely critique of FEMA doing things beyond response (like climate resilience, mitigation grants, or even pre-deployed assistance) might lead to cutbacks in those areas. The harm would be **less investment in preventing future disasters or reducing their impact**. For instance, FEMA’s BRIC program (Building Resilient Infrastructure and Communities) funds projects to fortify against floods or fires – if that’s deemed out of scope and defunded, communities will enter the next disaster more vulnerable. That can lead to greater destruction and higher costs/loss of life long-term. It’s a short-sighted trade-off: saving money now but risking much bigger harm (and expense) later.
- **Military Overreach in Civil Domain:** If one outcome is more reliance on Defense for disaster response, there’s potential harm in the **erosion of the Posse Comitatus norm** separating military and civilian roles. Military-led responses might not prioritize humanitarian nuance (they're great at logistics, but a community recovery is more than logistics – it’s also about empathy, working with local officials, etc., which FEMA specializes in). It could also set stage for conflict if states object to federal militarization of what has been a civilian cooperative effort. The trust local authorities have in FEMA might not directly extend to a DoD commander. That mismatch could harm coordination and thus the efficacy of relief operations. 
- **Internal Government Knowledge Loss:** If structural changes occurred (like moving FEMA out of DHS or firing many career staff), there’s a harm in the **loss of institutional knowledge and coordination**. FEMA within DHS has established relationships (with CBP for moving supplies, with Coast Guard, etc.). If Trump had, say, tried to make FEMA a stand-alone or under DoD, those ties might weaken. In disasters, time lost rebuilding coordination can cost lives. Also, if experienced staff were ousted due to perceived bias or inefficiency, FEMA loses skilled emergency managers. The new folks might be less experienced or politically chosen, possibly reducing competence when it counts.

**How a Future Administration Could Undo or Repair Damage:**  
- **Disband the FEMA Review Council Immediately:** If the Council was still active or its recommendations pending at the end of Trump’s term, the new President should **dissolve it**. If the Council had issued a report (likely recommending controversial changes), the new administration can **officially receive it but not act on it**, essentially shelving its findings. The President could issue a statement thanking the Council for its work but indicating a different approach will be taken. Any ongoing implementation of Council suggestions (e.g., if Trump started some reorg via executive action) should be stopped or reversed via new orders or policy memos.
- **Reaffirm FEMA’s Mission and Impartiality:** The new administration should strongly **reaffirm FEMA’s mandate to assist all Americans impartially**, and praise the workforce for their service. This could be done via an early address or memo by the President or new DHS Secretary clearly stating that **politics has no place in disaster response**, and that FEMA is expected to continue responding vigorously to all disasters, whether in red, blue, or purple areas. This reassurance is important to boost morale and public confidence. If any specific allegations (like the avoid-Trump-supporter homes story) were unsubstantiated or isolated, say so and emphasize that’s not tolerated but also not systemic. Essentially, **clear FEMA’s name** of broad bias, restoring trust that was eroded. 
- **Restore Focus on Climate and Preparedness:** If Trump’s Council or interim moves caused FEMA to scale back mitigation or climate adaptation efforts, reintegrate those priorities. E.g., **resume climate resilience programs** that might have been paused. If staff were diverted from such projects, put them back. Possibly increase funding for predisaster mitigation (the evidence shows it saves money long-term). Also, explicitly have FEMA incorporate climate projections into planning (something Trump likely squashed). This repair ensures FEMA isn’t just reactive but building capacity to handle future climate-driven disasters. The new admin can tie this into its larger climate agenda and show synergy between climate policy and disaster management.
- **Reject Politically Skewed Aid Practices:** If there were any new rules under Trump making it harder for certain areas to get aid (like stricter documentation or higher thresholds conveniently affecting certain disasters), roll those back to traditional norms. For example, FEMA’s threshold for individual assistance or public assistance declarations should be based on needs, not new arbitrary criteria. If some disaster declarations were denied under Trump for suspect reasons, reconsider them (possibly providing supplemental aid after the fact if appropriate, which could mean convincing Congress to appropriate funds for disasters Trump might have under-aided). 
- **Engage State and Local Stakeholders Inclusively:** One positive idea from the Council could be engaging broad stakeholders – the new admin can **hold its own listening sessions** with governors, mayors, emergency managers, etc., to gather input on how FEMA can improve. But crucially, do this without the Council’s political bias. Hear from blue and red state officials alike and career professionals. This can address legitimate concerns (maybe states want faster reimbursement or more flexibility) without the hostile framing. Show states that the federal government wants to be a supportive partner. Possibly reinstate or strengthen FEMA’s **National Advisory Council** (which exists by law and includes local officials) to incorporate feedback in a non-partisan way that outlives any one admin. By channeling improvement efforts through established, less politicized avenues, you repair the damage of the Council’s divisiveness with a sense of collaboration.
- **Rebuild Trust with FEMA Workforce:** Internally, if the Council’s existence demoralized staff or led to departures, the new leadership should do town halls or surveys within FEMA to identify pain points. Provide assurances that **no, FEMA won’t be dismantled**; instead, emphasize support for their work and plans to strengthen the agency (maybe by streamlining certain processes not for ideology but for efficiency’s sake – and involve staff in identifying those). If any personnel were improperly removed or sidelined (e.g., if someone was moved for having implemented an equity initiative), consider reinstating them or utilizing their expertise fully. Also, appoint a respected FEMA Administrator who has the confidence of the rank-and-file and of state leaders (someone experienced, bipartisan respect ideally, similar to how Biden kept Trump’s appointee initially to avoid disruption). This continuity or competence at the top helps settle nerves.
- **Address Legitimate Criticisms Constructively:** It’s possible there were grains of truth in criticisms – e.g., maybe FEMA bureaucracy could be more efficient. The new admin can pursue improvements here: for instance, invest in better IT for processing aid applications, simplify some overly complex grant rules where possible, and improve hiring to boost staff capacity (Trump harped on staffing issues). By *constructively* addressing these, you remove the wind from partisan sails and actually help survivors. For instance, if the Council noted slow housing assistance in some disaster, fix that by adding surge personnel or using new authorities like the Disaster Housing Assistance Program. If the Council flagged duplication or overlap with state efforts, work with states to clarify roles in a collaborative manner (maybe update the FEMA-State Agreements templates). The idea is to **take any real lessons without the political spin** and implement them, so the public gets better service and the rationale for drastic anti-FEMA moves disappears.
- **Legislative Guardrails:** If Trump’s efforts indicated how easily a president can mess with FEMA, maybe seek statutory protections. For example, Congress could clarify that FEMA’s mission is to provide impartial aid, or even incorporate nonpartisanship language in the Stafford Act. They could also formalize some practices, like requiring objective criteria for disaster declarations (to prevent partisan bias in granting/denying aid). This is tricky, but even a Congressional oversight spotlight can deter future politicization (i.e., letting any future FEMA-bashing council know Congress will push back).
- **Public Recommitment to Equity in Disasters:** If Trump’s Council undermined focus on helping the most vulnerable (his line about “illegal racial discrimination under guise of equity” in EO 14173 likely extended to disaster aid context too), the new admin should double-down on **the importance of equitable disaster recovery**. For example, resume efforts to ensure marginalized communities get equal access to FEMA programs (something Biden had instructed). Show success stories: e.g., how investing in poorer communities’ resilience saves money and lives for everyone. This counters the narrative that equity is a waste or bias. In practical repair, that means instructing FEMA’s Office of Equal Rights and new equity advisors (if removed under Trump, rehire them) to continue identifying gaps (like why certain neighborhoods get aid slower) and fixing them. Over time, this will yield a more fair system, combating any lingering bias (systemic or perceived).
- **Reinforce FEMA’s Value Proposition:** To get public and political support back, highlight FEMA’s achievements: e.g., how many homes it helped rebuild, how its coordination saved lives in some disaster. Essentially, run a bit of a PR campaign for FEMA’s positive impact to wash away the negative narrative Trump painted. Have survivors from both red and blue states speak about how FEMA helped them – a humanizing approach. This can be done via FEMA’s public affairs but also by President and DHS Secretary in speeches around disasters (pointing out FEMA’s good work, not just coming to hand out checks themselves). This rebuilds bipartisan appreciation for the agency, insulating it from future partisan attacks.

By dissolving the partisan “reform” effort and focusing on genuine improvements and a recommitment to FEMA’s core mission, the new administration can **repair damage to FEMA’s reputation and capacity**. The goal is to ensure that disaster survivors get robust help no matter who they are or where they live, and that the federal-state partnership in emergencies is strong and apolitical. Undoing EO 14180’s effects secures that **when disaster strikes, Americans can trust their government to be there fully and fairly**, which is exactly the trust that the prior council had jeopardized.

---

## EO 14181 — **Emergency Measures To Provide Water Resources in California and Improve Disaster Response in Certain Areas**  
**Alternative Title:** *“Exploiting Wildfires to Override California’s Water Protections and Environmental Laws”*

**What the EO Does:**  
- **Invokes Wildfire Emergency in Southern California:** The EO is framed around devastating wildfires in the Los Angeles area that firefighters struggled to combat due to water shortages (empty hydrants, dry reservoirs). It describes at least 28 deaths and hundreds of billions in damage, blaming *“inadequate water infrastructure”* for hampering the response. It declares it in the national interest to act. This sets the stage to justify extraordinary measures regarding water in California.
- **Commands Federal Agencies to Ensure Water Supply:** Section 2 orders the Secretaries of Defense, Homeland Security, Commerce, Interior, and Agriculture to **“take all measures, consistent with applicable authorities, to ensure adequate water resources in Southern California”**. They must report within 15 days on all emergency authorities available to ensure infrastructure needed to fight and prevent massive wildfires. This effectively mobilizes a multi-agency effort to funnel water to SoCal by any means.
- **Overrides Environmental and State Constraints:** It specifically directs Interior and Commerce to **“immediately take actions to override existing activities that unduly burden efforts to maximize water deliveries”**. It references considering actions consistent with the *“No Action Alternative”* in a 2024 Bureau of Reclamation EIS on the CVP/SWP operations. The “No Action Alternative” likely meant pumping water without environmental cutbacks. So this signals **suspending environmental rules (like endangered species protections for fish) that limit pumping water from Northern California to Southern California**.
- **Empowers Bureau of Reclamation to Bypass State Laws:** It instructs the Interior Secretary, via Bureau of Reclamation, to **use his discretion to operate the Central Valley Project (CVP) to deliver more water and hydropower “notwithstanding any contrary State or local laws.”**. It even says to ensure state agencies (like California’s Dept. of Water Resources) do not interfere with maximizing water delivery, including issuing a new Record of Decision consistent with a 2020 Trump-era plan maximizing pumping. This is a direct **preemption of California’s authority over its water**, asserting federal control to send more water south (which mainly benefits Central Valley agribusiness and SoCal urban users) at the expense of environmental flows.
- **Fast-Tracks Environmental Waivers:** In accordance with a Jan 20, 2025 National Energy Emergency (which Trump presumably declared in another EO), it directs expedited consideration of any *“Endangered Species Act exemptions”* needed for long-term operation of water projects. It also orders Interior to review and revise any ESA Section 7 consultation regulations that burden water projects, to align with a narrow “plain meaning” reading of the law. Essentially, it **pressures the use of the ESA’s “God Squad” to waive species protections if they impede water deliveries**, and to rewrite ESA rules to be less protective.
- **Identifies and Speeds Up Water Projects:** It requires Interior and Commerce to identify all major water supply/storage projects in California they are involved in (through ESA or NEPA processes). For each, they must assign officials to coordinate and within 30 days **identify regulatory hurdles “unduly burdening” the project and recent law changes affecting them, then develop a plan to suspend, revise, or rescind such regulations that aren’t necessary for public interest or law compliance**. They define “unduly burden” as any action that *“unnecessarily obstructs, delays, curtails, or impedes”* the projects. In effect, it’s a directive to **fast-track big dams, reservoirs, canals, etc., by loosening environmental review or other rules.** For example, the Sites Reservoir project (a proposed new reservoir in CA) might be expedited by trimming environmental mitigations.
- **General Provisions:** The EO references an earlier Jan 20, 2025 EO “Declaring a National Energy Emergency” to justify some actions (likely using emergency powers to override laws). It also states nothing in it shall limit any emergency authorities already in play. And that it should be implemented consistent with applicable law (though it’s pushing legal boundaries). It doesn’t explicitly mention any new funding, presumably expecting agencies to use existing funds or emergency funds.

**What it Claims to Do (Stated Justification):**  
It claims to address a **water emergency crippling wildfire response in California** and by extension threatening the nation. It recounts a dramatic scenario: *“raging fires… firefighters unable to fight blaze due to dry hydrants…28 dead… hundreds of billions in damages”*. So it justifies drastic steps as needed to *“ensure adequate water resources in Southern California”* for fire-fighting and prevention. 

Trump positions it as an urgent *“emergency measure”* to save lives and property, essentially saying environmental rules and California’s own policies are intolerable luxuries in the face of deadly wildfires. He uses emotive language of beloved pets and childhood memories lost to flames to justify overriding normal processes. 

The EO hints that **California’s policies are to blame** (e.g., “existing activities burden efforts to maximize water deliveries”). It likely alludes to California’s environmental regs (like flows for fish) or local opposition to new water projects. It thus justifies federal override by saying state mismanagement caused a national-scale disaster. It declares current conditions a tragedy affecting the entire nation, so national interest demands federal intervention that might normally be left to state control.

Additionally, by referencing a National Energy Emergency, it ties water to energy (the hydropower mention suggests blackouts maybe due to low reservoir levels hindering hydroelectricity). So it claims to also bolster energy reliability by getting more water behind dams.

Summarily, the EO claims to:
- Save lives and property from catastrophic fires by rushing water to SoCal.
- Cut through red tape and environmental “burdens” that left firefighters without water.
- Use emergency powers because the crisis is severe and California’s own policies failed (implying California prioritized fish or environmental concerns over people’s safety).
- Improve disaster response in critical areas by overriding “disastrous California policies” (the subheading of Sec. 2 says “Overriding Disastrous California Policies” explicitly ([Executive Order 14181—Emergency Measures To Provide Water Resources in California and Improve Disaster Response in Certain Areas | The American Presidency Project](https://www.presidency.ucsb.edu/documents/executive-order-14181-emergency-measures-provide-water-resources-california-and-improve#:~:text=Sec,with%20all%20applicable%20authorities%2C%20to))).
- Also expedite water projects to prevent future shortages (like building more storage).

**Real Purpose or Effect (Critical Perspective):**  
While dressed as wildfire response, the real purpose is to **advance a long-standing agenda of Trump and California Republicans to divert more water to farmers and cities, weakening environmental protections and state control**:
- **Weaponizing a Wildfire Crisis for Water Politics:** The EO leverages a wildfire emergency (something Californians are very sensitive to) to push changes in **water allocation and environmental laws** that the agriculture lobby and Central Valley Republicans have wanted for years. It’s a Trojan horse: people focus on fires, but the substantive actions are about pumping water from the Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta (which predominantly benefits Central Valley agribusiness and some SoCal water districts) and fast-tracking new dams. Essentially, it uses the emotional impetus of fire deaths to implement the **Trump 2018-2020 water agenda** (which had been partially blocked by lawsuits and the Biden admin’s reversal).
- **Undermining California’s Environmental Autonomy:** By declaring federal supremacy “notwithstanding state law”, Trump aims to override California’s rigorous environmental statutes like the California Endangered Species Act and the California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA), at least regarding major federal-state water projects. This is a significant intrusion into state sovereignty. Real effect: **diminish California’s ability to manage its water resources and protect its environment**, shifting power to federal agencies more aligned with Trump’s extraction-friendly views. It’s likely targeted at boosting water deliveries to the Central Valley Project contractors (large farms) who have been restricted by flow rules for fish like the Delta smelt and salmon.
- **Advancing Anti-Environmental, Pro-Development Projects:** The directive to expedite projects and find regulatory hurdles is aimed at **pushing through controversial infrastructure** (like Sites Reservoir, raising Shasta Dam (which is currently illegal under CA law), or water tunnels to bypass the Delta) that face state or environmental opposition. It cloaks them as urgent for disaster response, but those projects are decades-long undertakings, not immediate firefighting fixes. So the real goal is to use emergency powers to circumvent usual environmental reviews and get these projects approved before opposition can stall them. 
- **Boosting Favor with California Conservatives:** This likely plays to Trump’s base in California’s Central Valley and among national conservatives who see California as overregulated. By “taking on” California’s policies (like the Delta smelt protections which have been a right-wing talking point), Trump signals he’s delivering for farmers and standing up to liberal California government. So a purpose is political: strengthen his support in a region he’s popular in (rural CA) and stoke the narrative of commonsense (him) vs. liberal mismanagement (CA Democrats).
- **Rollback of Environmental Laws Under Guise of Emergency:** The EO effectively attempts to **suspend parts of the Endangered Species Act and National Environmental Policy Act (NEPA)** using emergency authorities. The “plain meaning” revision suggests altering how Section 7 consultations (which ensure federal actions don’t jeopardize endangered species) are implemented to make them more lenient. This is a continuation of Trump’s first-term rollbacks of ESA regulations, now on steroids with an “emergency” rationale. The real effect is a **downgrading of habitat and species protections** if they conflict with water deliveries or other chosen “critical” projects. Once done, the changes could persist beyond the emergency if not reversed. 
- **Favoring Human Use Over Ecosystem Health Absolutely:** The EO clearly prioritizes water for human use (firefighting, presumably also cities and farms) over any ecological concerns. Phrases like “regulations that...unduly burden...not necessary to protect the public interest or comply with law” basically set a presumption that environmental regs are unnecessary burdens unless proven otherwise. It's a radical anthropocentric shift in balancing uses. The effect would be **possible collapse of fish populations** (salmon runs, Delta smelt) if outflows are cut severely – an irreversible ecological harm – but one the EO writers deem acceptable sacrifice to maximize water supply. 
- **Punishing Blue Areas (Reading Between Lines):** While not explicit, there's an undercurrent of sticking it to California’s leadership (Newsom, etc.). Trump's anecdote about pets and memories lost in LA fires suggests blame on local authorities (like implying mismanagement of water infrastructure). But California’s hydrant/water issues in fires are usually drought-driven, not regulatory (they do struggle with water distribution in remote areas sometimes). He might be implicitly blaming environmentalists for not having enough reservoirs, etc. It positions him as the savior stepping in where California failed. Possibly it sets up to later blame California if they resist his measures (“they care more about fish than stopping fires”). This all fosters a political narrative useful for 2028 campaigning in the West.
 
**Fit into Trump’s Broader Agenda:**  
This EO aligns with his first-term patterns: aggressively reducing environmental regulations in favor of development, claiming to champion ordinary folks (farmers needing water, residents threatened by fire) against what he calls liberal mismanagement or overregulation. It's a continuation of his war on California specifically – he often tangled with California (emissions standards, immigration, etc.). Now he uses a crisis scenario to override California’s own policies, reflecting his agenda of **asserting federal override when it suits him politically**.

It also meshes with his fossil fuel/energy stance. By labeling a “National Energy Emergency” and connecting water to hydropower, he ties in his push to remove hindrances to energy production (like how he invoked emergency to boost oil pipelines or keep coal plants online). Freeing up hydro by ignoring fish flows fits that view of maximizing energy outputs at environmental cost.

Another agenda point: **loyalty to agricultural business interests**. Trump had strong support in the Central Valley farm belt. This EO essentially delivers on their wish to get more irrigation water. It's reminiscent of policy moves he did in 2019 (signing a plan to send more water to farmers and fewer to fish) – which Biden paused. Now he forcefully reinstates and amplifies those moves. It likely pleases big donors or influential Rep. politicians in California’s ag region, fulfilling his promises to them.

Also, it fits his disregard for climate and environment: While wildfires have clear climate links (drought, heat), Trump doesn’t mention climate change at all. Instead, he blames lack of water infrastructure and state policy. This echoes his past claims that California should “rake forests” or that environmental laws cause fires by preventing logging or water usage – a scientifically dubious claim. It’s part of his agenda to deflect climate causes and instead push an ideological solution (deregulate and extract resources). 

**Harm Caused:**  
- **Ecological Harm & Species Extinctions:** By overriding water protections, **fish populations like the Delta smelt (already near extinction) and endangered salmon runs could collapse**. Reduced river flows increase salinity and reduce habitat in the Bay-Delta estuary, possibly dooming some species. This has broader ecosystem impacts (fisheries collapse, food chain effects, water quality degradation). Once lost, these species can’t be recovered – an irreparable environmental harm triggered by short-term water diversions.
- **Long-Term Water Sustainability Issues:** Pumping maximum water south provides relief now but **depletes reservoirs and groundwater** unsustainably. If more water is pulled in dry conditions, reservoirs like Shasta, Oroville could be drawn too low, reducing supplies for subsequent years or reducing cold water pools needed for salmon survival. It also may encourage more planting of water-intensive crops in arid areas (farmers get water signals that perhaps aren't reliably sustainable), leading to a worse crash later. Essentially, it prioritizes immediate use at the expense of future reliability, risking more severe shortages down the line (which ironically could exacerbate fires and drought impacts).
- **Interference in State Water Management & Legal Conflict:** By ignoring state laws, this sets up a **federal-state conflict**. California likely sued or resisted. That conflict (legal battles) can delay effective cooperation on other aspects of wildfire response and water management. It wastes resources on court fights (CA did sue Trump admin previously on water). If federal agencies forcibly operate infrastructure against state opposition, it could get messy operationally (state water project and federal CVP are intertwined – conflict could hamper efficient coordination, causing *less* effective water delivery in chaos). This disharmony is harmful to both environment and water users in the confusion.
- **Marginalized Communities Impacts:** Water allocation changes often have uneven impacts. If urban areas and big farms get more water, it might come at the expense of smaller farms or rural communities dependent on groundwater that gets less recharge if surface flows are diverted. Also, environmental degradation hits subsistence fishers or tribes reliant on salmon and river health. For example, tribes on the Klamath and Sacramento Rivers depend on salmon runs culturally and economically – further declines harm them deeply. The EO’s approach didn't consider those environmental justice aspects. Plus, pushing more water through delta might threaten the drinking water quality of some communities (saltwater intrusion into delta islands' groundwater, etc.). So there's likely harm to **tribal rights and vulnerable communities’ resources**.
- **Weakening Environmental Law Precedents:** If by emergency or after it, Trump’s changes to ESA or NEPA regulations stick, that can harm environmental protections nationwide. E.g., altering ESA Section 7 rules to “plain meaning” might gut considering climate impacts on species or reduce habitat protections in all contexts, not just CA water. That undermines conservation efforts and could embolden development interests to push similar “emergencies” to bypass laws elsewhere. It's a slippery slope undermining rule of law.
- **Potential Firefighting Benefit Overstated:** Ironically, the actual benefit to firefighting from these measures is dubious. Fire hydrants in LA going dry is unlikely due to environmental flow rules – it's more about local distribution or drought. The EO’s main effect (increasing delta pumping) doesn’t directly provide water for immediate fire suppression in SoCal mountains (those rely on local water sources, often small reservoirs and groundwater). So the immediate life-saving benefit is probably minimal. Thus, **fire outcomes likely wouldn’t dramatically improve** from this (wildfires are more constrained by weather/winds and forest management than municipal water availability, except in fringe cases). Meanwhile, resources and attention might be diverted from real fire mitigation (like vegetation management or resilient infrastructure) to focus on water shipping. That mismatch means **wildfire risk may remain high** despite these measures, possibly causing similar tragedies in future because root causes (like climate drying, forest fuel loads) aren’t addressed in this water-centric approach.
- **Public Backlash in CA and Division:** Californians value their environmental protections. This heavy-handed federal override likely triggered significant backlash, protests, and anger especially in Northern CA (where ecosystems are impacted). It could deepen the **urban vs. rural, coastal vs. inland divides** as one side sees this as necessary for human safety, and the other sees it as a cynical water grab harming their environment. Social trust in government – either state or federal – can erode when these battles happen. If people lose trust that environmental laws will be upheld, they might resort to litigation or radical activism, creating further conflict and hindering cooperative disaster planning. It's also traumatic for communities watching their local wildlife and landscapes sacrificed under emergency edict, possibly breeding resentment that could last beyond Trump’s term.
- **Confusion in Water Management & Potential Engineering Risks:** Rapidly altering water operations can have unintended physical consequences – like if state systems are not ready to handle increased flows or if joint federal-state facilities get unsynchronized. For example, pushing pumps harder could risk infrastructure strain or levee stability in the delta (higher flow rates can pressure aging levees, raising flood risk). Also, suspension of certain procedural safeguards might lead to mistakes (like not properly monitoring water quality or fish screens if that’s deemphasized). Those mistakes could cause local flooding or water supply contamination, ironically adding new emergencies.
- **National Environmental Harm Norm:** Using an “emergency” to override environmental law sets a precedent that could be used elsewhere (e.g., a future admin might declare emergency to build pipelines without NEPA). It hence harms the **integrity of environmental governance** nationally. People may see that if you want to bypass protections, frame it as an emergency – potentially weakening adherence to environmental review processes as stakeholders might expect they’ll be waived whenever politically expedient. That unpredictability is bad for planning (both for developers and environmental groups).

**How a Future Administration Could Undo or Repair Damage:**  
- **Revoke EO 14181 Immediately:** The new President should revoke Trump’s water/fire emergency order on Day 1. This rescinds any directives agencies were following to override laws or expedite projects. If any federal agencies issued decisions (like a new Record of Decision for delta pumping, or interim operations ignoring ESA limits), the new administration should **withdraw or reverse those decisions**. For instance, instruct Interior to abandon the new Trump ROD and revert to the prior (more environmentally balanced) operating rules or to whatever a court might have mandated. Similarly, if Commerce/NOAA was halting fish protections, order them resumed. This stops further harm. 
- **Cancel ESA Regulation Changes:** If Trump’s Interior tried to permanently revise Section 7 rules (the “plain meaning” bit), the new admin should publish notice to repeal those changes and revert to the previous regulations. If they used emergency powers to grant an ESA exemption for water operations via the Endangered Species Committee (“God Squad”), the new admin’s Interior and NOAA can **re-initiate consultations and restore protections** – effectively undo any exemption (noting that a God Squad exemption issuance is rare and would be legally complex to undo, but they could mitigate by voluntarily continuing measures to protect species and working with California to step in with state protections).
- **Cease Project Fast-Tracking & Restore NEPA:** Immediately instruct agencies (Interior, Commerce) to stop implementing any “streamlining” measures for California water projects from EO 14181. If they identified some reg to suspend, unsuspend it. For projects like Sites Reservoir or Delta Tunnel, commit to full NEPA/ESA review being reinstated. Possibly augment those reviews with extra resources to make up for lost time rather than skipping steps. If any projects had received emergency waivers under Trump, consider **voluntarily redoing** environmental analysis for the record, to ensure decisions are legally and scientifically sound.
- **Coordinate with California:** The new admin should reach out to California’s government to **repair federal-state cooperation on water and wildfires**. Rescind federal preemption claims and affirm California’s rightful role in managing its resources. Possibly set up a joint federal-state task force on wildfires that deals with water issues collaboratively (for instance, exploring building more local water storage for firefighting that doesn’t involve delta exports, thinning forests near watersheds to improve retention, etc.). Show respect for California’s laws: e.g., publicly commit that any federal water infra expansions (like Shasta Dam raise) will not proceed without state approval. This helps rebuild trust and align efforts rather than working at cross-purposes, benefiting both environmental outcomes and disaster readiness.
- **Address Wildfire Issues Directly:** Separate water fights from wildfire solutions. The new admin should implement a robust wildfire strategy: increase funding for forest management (clearing dead trees, controlled burns), improve firefighting resources (more air tankers, mutual aid), and tackle climate contributors by pursuing emissions cuts. By focusing on these, they mitigate fire risk in ways that **don’t scapegoat environmental rules**. They can point to those actions to show progress in protecting communities, undermining the notion that only by diverting rivers can fires be fought. If the admin helps CA harden infrastructure (burying power lines to prevent ignitions, etc.), that also reduces catastrophic fires. This makes the water vs fire narrative moot by addressing fires directly. 
- **Reinstate Environmental Protections:** Reverse any damage done: If increased pumping harmed fish, implement restoration actions (like habitat restoration projects or even consider voluntary water buybacks to leave more flow for environment in the short term). Resume or strengthen collaborative programs with CA for ecosystem health (like the joint federal-state plans for the Bay-Delta that Trump had ditched). Show commitment to long-term solutions like a sustainable water sharing agreement balancing farms, cities, and environment (something CA was working on with stakeholders). Possibly push Congress to fund drought resilience in CA (recycling, desalination research, etc.) so that extreme measures aren’t considered next time. All this repairs ecological harm and reduces future conflict by adding water supply or efficiency (so there’s less zero-sum fighting).
- **Damage Control for Species:** If any species were driven to critical brink (e.g., if a salmon run had a bad year due to low flows), emergency measures to help them recover (hatchery releases, fishing moratoriums) might be needed. Acknowledge the harm done and make it a priority to save those species – e.g., have NOAA develop a salmon recovery boost plan with CA. This not only tries to fix environmental harm but signals to conservation communities that the new admin is reversing the anti-wildlife stance.
- **Legal Settlements:** If California or NGOs sued over EO 14181 actions (likely they did), the new admin can settle those suits by conceding to reinstate protections or reconsider decisions as plaintiffs asked. For example, if a court case was challenging the ESA exemption, government can agree to vacate the exemption and do proper process. This ends litigation and mends fences with environmental stakeholders. It also sets a precedent that such extreme actions were indeed flawed and won’t be defended, discouraging their future use.
- **Legislative Clarifications:** If feasible, Congress could amend the Stafford Act or related emergency laws to clarify they can’t be used to waive environmental laws (closing the loophole Trump exploited). Or amend the ESA to explicitly disallow use of national emergencies to bypass species protection without Congress. That’s tough politically, but even discussing it in oversight hearings can highlight that misuse. Similarly, reinforcing states’ rights in water in federal law (like stating clearly that federal projects must follow state water law unless Congress says otherwise) could prevent such preemption attempts. 
- **Public Communication:** The new administration should publicly refute the idea that environmental laws caused the fires or hindered response. Explain factors: climate change, extreme winds, utility infrastructure – so the solution is not gutting fish protections, it’s tackling those factors. By educating the public (especially those in CA) on what really happened, they build support for their corrective actions and dispel any lingering belief in Trump’s narrative. Also emphasize cooperation: *“We’re working with California to ensure they have water for all needs and to fight fires, without sacrificing the environment – we don’t have to choose between safety and nature.”* This helps heal the divisiveness stoked by the EO.  

In doing all this, the future administration essentially **un-does the overreach of EO 14181 and reinstates a balanced, law-abiding approach to California’s water challenges and wildfire threats**. It reinforces that **sound environmental management and effective disaster response can go hand in hand**, rather than being at odds as Trump portrayed. This not only repairs immediate damage (to ecosystems, intergovernmental relations) but also sets a precedent that emergencies should not be exploited to sideline environmental stewardship.
